"THE BULLETIN ” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated (which is published price with
postage added), Books and Periodicals in the subjoined lists will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
OF OMAR KHAYYAM.
The announcement that George Meredith
ottered his maiden speech at the last meeting of
the Omar Khayyam club, awoke (it is to be feared)
in few Australian bosoms a responsive thrill.
Conan Doyle we know, and Anthony Hope ;
of “Ships that Pass” and “Trilby” we can
prattle with assurance; but George Meredith,
“ the greatest living master of English fiction ” —
how rare his disciples in the booksellers’ flock !
As for Omar Khayyam—ask for him at the
counter, and observe the more than Egyptian
darkness which shrouds the bland assistant’s
phiz ! Yet among all the guides, philosophers
and friends that Man has vouchsafed unto men—
not forgetting Solomon, and his brother Ecclesi-
astes, and his cousin Ecclesiasticus (a most admir-
able-foolish cuss); not forgetting Horace, and
Sterne, and Montaigne, and the grave Antoninus
—there is none like unto Omar—none !
Meredith is “ tough ” —to the flippant literary
slap-dasher. Maybe. So is Henry George (not his
sentences, but his ideas). Yet you will meet more
than one enthusiastic Single - taxer who has
assimilated “Progress and Poverty” as thoroughly
as a Plymouth Brother his Bible. What saith
(with emphatic inversion) the Scriptures : “ Unto
whomsoever much is given, of him shall be much
required.” And Meredith gives more than he
requires by 90 times 9—and ever so many ciphers.
The book to begin on. of course, is “The,Ordeal
of Richard Feverel,” which most appeases the
hunger for plots—and chapters 15 and 20 may
be dipped into by way of foretaste. Anyone then
who gets no farther, deserves not to —which is a
cruel thing to say, but quite fair in the circum-
stances.
Omar is another matter (Khayyam—“the tent-
maker ” —is a tagged poetical pen-name, like —a
suitable modern parallel that won’t come to mind).
Money cannot really be measured against Fitz-
gerald’s Omar—practically the only one worth
knowing ; but, commercially speaking, there is no
“cheap’ edition (the least price is 11s. posted;
and Eli Vender's famous illustrations run his
edition up to anything over £o 5s.), so that the
book is better not bought if anywhere, from any-
body,, you can borrow a copy and learn it by heart
—there are only 101 stanzas in the latest version.
Only 101 stanzas—begotten of Omar, conceived
and born of Fitzgerald, with the wit and wisdom
tain fully gathered by a million philosophers of
last and \V est inherited and distilled in their
fragrance! And the essence of all is
Carpe Diem !
“ Go thy way, eat thy bread with joy, and drink
thy wine with a merry heart. Let thy garments
be always white; and let thy head lack no oint-
ment. Live joyfully with the wife whom thou
lovest all the days of this vanity—thy life.”
A Book of _Verses underneath the Bough,
A**Jug of Wine, a Loaf of Bread— and Thou
Beside me singing ip the. Wilderness—
Oh, Wilderness were Paradise "enow !
.Ijjfvery j!)piaj][§t Iris his verse_ ip &-
gefald s exquisite metre. Here is one (spoken by
a “ shape of clay in the “ Potter’s house ”) :
“ W ell. murmured one, “ leT whoso make or buy.
My Clay with long Oblivion is gone dry:
But fill me with the old familiar Juice,
I might recover by and by ! "
Another j—
And much as Wine has play’d the Infidel,
And robb'd me of my Robe of Honour —Well,
I wonder often what the Vjntners buy
One-half so precious as Ihe stuff they sell.
Others :—
I must abjure the Balm of Life, I must,
Scared by some After-reckoning ta’en on trust,
Or lured with hope of some Diviner Drink,
To fill the Gup—when crumbled into Dust I
Oh threats of Hell and Hopes of Paradise !
One thing at least is certain —This Life flies ;
One thing is certain and is Lies;
The Flower that once has blown for ever dies.
Strange, is it not ? that of the myriads who
Before us pass’d the door of Darkness through,
Not one returns to tell us of the Road,
Which to discover we must travel too.
I sent my Soul through the Invisible,
Some letter of that After-iife to spell:
And by and by my Soul return’d to me,
And answer’d “ I Myself am Heav’n and Hell: ”
Heav’u but the Vision of fulfill’d Desire,
And Hell the Shadow from a Soul on fire,
Cast on the Darkness into which Ourselves
So late emerged from, shall so soon expire.
Best of all : —
Some for the Glories of This World ; and some
Sigh for the Prophet’s Paradise to come ;
Ah, take the Cash, and let the Credit go,
Nor heed the rumble of a distant Drum!
PERIODICALS.
With the excellent Aug. number, Pall Mall
Magazine raises its price Gd. (now 2s. sd. posted).
It has Jong been obvious that this, the most sump-
tuous of English •magazines, must be printed
at a loss, in spite of its fine array of advts. Pro-
bably the circulation in England, whei - e its patrons
belong to a class which does not value incidental
sixpences, will be unaffected by the change ; but
the Australian sale is likely to decrease. So many
good books are obtainable at 2s. or 2s. Gd. that the
Pall Mall, still splendid value, goes down con-
siderably in the scale of comparative value ; and
the Australian buyer, though he never grudges
his money, always wants to make the best bargain
possible.
The Aug. English Illustrated (lid.) is a
specially good number of a specially good maga-
zine. A tale —“ With Madness in his Method ”
a charming example of the lighter American style
—and the continuation of Weyman’s “Memoirs
of a Minister of France,” plead for space-forbidden
comment ; and one illustration, “Dawn,” is the
prettiest anywhere to be seen this month.
The July Englishwoman (lid.) is well-varied
and readable. The best story is Richard Pryce’s
“ Book of the Curl.” Frankfort Moore gives
another glimpse of that remarkable country,
English - Australia.
The Album for July 15 (Bd.) cannot be too
highly praised. The illustrations are exquisite,
Krticularly those which depict the beautiful
ames from Oxford to Hampton Court. This
weekly is well worth a continuous subscription
(15s. Gd. per half-year).
The July Yellow Book (6s. 6d.) doesn’t reach
previous standards; it has less originality, less
literary value. There are one or two good pic-
tures ; the verse (with the exception of a trans-
lation from the Portuguese) is dim and echo-y;
only two stories can be called good—“ in an
American Newspaper Office,” and “A New
Poster.” Kenneth Grahame’s “ Long Odds ” is a
clever and suggestive paper.
The July Foedm (37s. per annum) has a par-
ticularly good article by Frederic Harrison on
“Charles Kingsley’s Place in Literature”—the
eighth of a splendid series which will be well
worth buying when republished. Harrison is a
better critic than Saintsbury ; and no less pleasant
a writer.
SOME RECENT BOOKS.
“ Australian Zoology,” by G. Metcalfe, M.A., a
N.S.W. State school-teacher, is published by E.
W. Cole at Is. 3d. (posted), and is heartily recom-
mended as a most interesting book, worthy a
much better dress. Mainly, of course, it is com-
pilation ; but the work is exceedingly well done,
and the little volume should be bought by every-
one interested in Australian birds, beasts and
fishes.
Gunsberg’s just-published “Chess Openings”
(Is. 3d.) is a very good little manual, with the
great advantage of being up-to-date. (Other good
chess books are Green’s general handbook (Is. 3d.);
Staunton’s Hand-book, Praxis, and Chess-player’s
Companion (5s. 6d. each); and Morphy’s Games
(5s. 6d.). A more scientific book is Horwitz and
Kling’s “ Chess Studies and End Games ” (8s. Sd.).
Steinitz’s “Instructor” (11s.), of which only
Part I. is yet published, is the completest so far
as it goes.)
“ Almayer’s Folly ” (paper, 3s.; cloth, 4s.), by
Joseph Conrad, is a powerful tale of life in Borneo
which has been rather over-praised by the English
press—always grateful to the writer who exploits
a new milieu. The central idea is the straggle
between civilised and barbaric instincts in a half-
white, half-yellow girl; and her Dutch father’s
broken heart when barbarism triumphs and she
rushes to the arms of a Malay lover, is the last
drop in a cup of tragic life-failure.
Percy Fitzgerald supplies a gossipy, readable
“Life of Henry Irving” (paper, Is. 3d. ; cloth,
Is. lOd.)
“ The Cult of Beaut}*, a manual of personal
hygiene ” (3s.), by 0. S. Thompson, is an elegantly-
gotten-up little book, for which any lover would
deserve the thanks of his lady.

NOTEB.
Alex. Lindsay is issuing an “ Australander
series ”.(ls. 3d.), to include new books by favorite
Australian authors. The first number, “His
Native Wife,” by Louis Becke, will bo out in three
or four weeks.
Booksellers find Morris’s “Life of Higin-
botham ” very dead stock. The life of a democrat
should certainly be published at a more demo-
cratic price than 9s. Bd. (posted).
The late Sir James Stephen put a familiar life-
philosophy in some lines quoted by his biographer-
brother Leslie:
The know-nothing books may alarm you ; but
many a better man
Knows he knows nothing and says so, and lives
the best life he can.
If there is a future state, face its hopes and terrors
gravely;
The best path to it must be to bear life’s burthens
bravely.
And even if there be none, why should you not
live Hire a man,
Enjoying whatever you have as much and as long
as you can ? ”
Melb. and Sydney retailers complain bitterly of
the ladies—generally related to most influential
personages—who get parcels of new books sent
home ‘ r on appro.,” and return them after any
time from two months to two years ; when ? of
course, they are quite unsaleable. “My relation,
Sir Blank Blank,” covers a multitude of book-
lenders’ curses.
New volumes in the pretty pocket edition of
Chas. Kingsley (Is. lOd. each) are the “ Poems ”
and “Alton Locke.”
Mis j Wilkins, a writer of charming American
village tales, has won the Chapman’s Magazine
first prize of =£loo for a short detective tale, “ The
Long Arm.” Brander Matthews, another Ameri-
can, came second among 3000 competitors.

Medical.
ALCOHOLISM.
THIS terrible disease of drink, contracted through
excessive sociability, deprives the best of men
of all respect and reason ; it leads them to acts of
indecency and crime. Alcohol weakens the mind
in subjection to the awful Crave, or morbid desire
for stimulants. The unknown impulse drives the
inebriate to often drink what his palate may even
repel, yet he cannot resist, though he knows him-
self to be drifting to Utter Ruin. At this stage
pledges and punishment prove useless.
The only hope in this condition is to arrest the
crave, eradicate the accumulated alcoholic poison,
and to re-invigorate the nervous and general sys-
tem —to give the subject his self-control and inhi-
bition. Conscious victims —or their friends—
should not delay investigating the marvellous
results achieved by the ORIENT INSTITUTE
for ALCOHOLISM. During the past 18 months
many prominent and valuable men have been
restored to health, Sobriety, and Position in
Life. Inebriates of the most sceptical nature
have been amazed at their restoration.
Cure for Inebriety has been established beyond
all doubt. The U.S. Government has introduced
it into the army, and already numerous States have
legislated for the medical treatment of inebriates.
Communications confidential. Pamphlets per
sealed envelopes. D. B. THOMAS, Manager,
“The Orient,” Moore Park Road, Sydney.

Q URSELVES!
We wish it to be understood that the character of the
business we do, and the absolutely inclusive variety of the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, considering the low prices we
ask for everything we sell, and the consequent blessings
we are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves
PUBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life —honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, dnd the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
neverctase from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
somewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.

Various 'Verses.
APART!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I stand upon an unknown shore,
And, rolling at my feet
In ebb and flow for evermore,
The unknown waters beat,
And on my brain, in sad refrain,
Their endless tale repeat.
Another lonely islet lies
Amid the waters grey,
Enshrouded by the mists that rise
From out their scattered spray,
And on its rim, far-off and dim,
I see thee wait alway.
Far-off and dim I hear thy cry—
I hear it in despair ;
And unto thee, in anguish, I
Throw out wild hands of prayer—
Vain hands flung wide across the tide
Upon the empty air!
And yet I see to yonder shore
Shall never pathway be;
We live apart for evermore,
And evermore the sea
With mighty deeps between us sweeps—
A stern fatality!
And day shall follow after day,
And moons shall wax and wane,
And winters banish summer’s sway
And melt to Spring again ;
And years go by linked endlessly
In one eternal chain ;
But never shall the trackless deep
Divide or flow apart,
The waters ever round me leap,
Their wail within my heart,
And from their graves beneath the waves
Forbidding ghosts upstart.
And well I know between thy soul
And mine on this lone shore
Unfathomed depths of ocean roll,
Unmeasured waters pour,
And mists arise that blind rny eyes
For ever evermore.
Tasmania.
K.A.
IN TOWN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out of work and out of money—out of friends that
means, you bet—
Out of firewood, togs and tucker, out of every-
thing but debt,
And I loathe the barren pavements, and the
crowds a fellow meets,
And the maddening repetition of the suffocating
streets.
With their stinks my soul is tainted, and the tang
is on my tongue
Of that sour and smoky suburb and the push
we’re thrown among,
And I sicken at the corners polished free of paint
and mirk
By the shoulders of the men who’re always hang-
ing round for work.
Home—good Lord ! a three-roomed hovel ’twixt a
puddle and a drain,
In harmonious connection on the left with Liver
Lane,
Where a crippled man is dying, and a horde of
children fight,
And a woman in the horrors howls remorsefully
at night.
It has stables close behind it, and an ash-heap for
a lawn,
And is furnished with the tickets of the things we
have in pawn;
And all day the place is haunted by a melancholy
crowd
Who beg everything or borrow, but to steal are
not too proud.
All day, too, come weary women, folk with
famine-haunted eyes,
Hawking things that are not wanted—things that
no one ever buys.
And I hate the prying neighbors, in their animal
content,
And the devilish persistence of the man who
wants the rent.
I, who oared for none, and faltered at no work a
man might do,
Felt a fierce delight take hold me when the trucks
went surging through,
When the flood raced in the sluices, and the giant
gums swang round
’Fore my axe, and flung their mighty limbs all
mangled on the ground—
I who hewed and built and burrowed, and who
asked no man to give
When a strong arm was excuse enough for ven-
turing to live—
I am creeping by the gutters, with a simper and a
smirk,
To the Fates in spats and ’toppers for the privilege
of work.
Far out the hills are all a-flame; the blossom
golden fair
Streams up the gladdened ranges, and its scent is
everywhere,
And the kiddies of the settlers ’long the creek are
red and sweet,
Whilst my youngsters have the sallowness and
savour of the street.
To escape these endless vaults of brick, and pitch
a tent out back,
If I get a chance I’ll graft until my very sinews
crack.
Meanwhile may all the angels up in Paradise look
down
On a man of sin who died not, but was damned
and sent to town !
Melb.
EDWARD DYSON.

W IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
E beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
. Ltd.,.of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “ Allen and
Ginter s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo ” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called ‘Pure Gold and “ JDixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W, D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ THREE CASTLES ” and cc C-A-IPST-A-ZEST.”
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills ’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan,” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of- any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
Three Castles” and “ Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HETDE, TODMAN & CO. {Agents for W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Ltd.)

“ Yankee Doodle , 99 blended with Polities,
The above is, of course, a caricature of the famous American national picture, which will be
given later on.
“YANKEE DOODLE” is everywhere spoken of as the finest plug-tobacco that has ever
appeared here, and it has won greater success during the short time it has been on the market than
anything previously. This is no idle boast, and, to prove it, any smoker can test the statement, for
there are few towns in N.S.W. where “YANKEE DOODLE” is not known and sold. Its sale
has this year increased nearly one hundred (100) per cent over that of last year. And why is this?
Simply that “YANKEE DOODLE” has struck the popular taste. In the first place, it comes, after
years of careful study, from a combination of certain choice Virginian tobaccos, so blended and
exquisitely flavoured, that any smoker who can stand a tobacco heavier than the pure gold-leaf is
forced to admit that he has at last discovered something far and away better than anything he has
been accustomed to. DIXSON & SONS have taken over half-a-century to accomplish this; and, as
their leading business has been built up on their reputation for QUALITY, they could not afford to
make the foregoing statement if any doubt existed. DIXSON & SONS, who use more American leaf
than all the other Tobacco Factories combined, are noted for several high-class brands: —“CON-
QUEROR,” “SIR ROGER,” “CHAMPION,” and “DOLLAR;” but “YANKEE DOODLE”
ranks supreme.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have Tim Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, hut is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. P.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White,
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal :—The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also :—R. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 21, 1895.
Praying for Rain.
After a few years of good seasons a period
of drought and fire and barrenness has again
settled down on Southern Queensland, New
South Wales, and other parts of this con-
tinent. Ever since Australia had a history,
and long before it had a history, the drought
has been the most conspicuous feature of its
climate. It comes, doubtless, according to
fixed laws, and when man has learned these
laws he will be able to foretell its coming,
its intensity, area, duration, and everything
else about it. So far, however, he hasn’t
learned them, partly, perhaps, because he
devotes so much time to football, cricket,
horse-racing, organising Chinese missions,
arguing about creeds, splitting hairs of theo-
logy, drinking, and other unintellectual pur-
suits that he lacks the necessary leisure for
studying this most essential subject; there-
fore the drought is still a mystery, and its
arrival and departure are equally unforesee-
able. When the causes of droughts and
floods, dry seasons and wet, are fully dis-
covered, it may quite possibly be found
that man can in part, at least, control the
rainfall, but so far no progress has been
made in this direction. Therefore, the
Government of New South Wales resolved
last week to have recourse to “a day of
humiliation and prayer,” and to ask the
Unknown to put back the universe for
the especial benefit of that province. On
such occasions nine-tenths of the popu-
lation don’t pray, because their belief in
the efficacy of prayer is nil, andraost of
the other tenth prays half-heartedly, not
in person, but per parson, on the assump-
tion that it may do a little good and
it doesn’t seem likely to do any harm. The
said parson, in many cases, frankly acknow-
ledges his belief that prayer for rain is a
foolish attempt to substitute religion for
irrigation and tank-digging, and that the
results to be expected are of the smallest.
And he prays at the direction of a “godless
Government,” which is of opinion that the
average cleric is not fit to be entrusted with
the work of national education, though he is
good enough to bo used for rain-supplication
purposes on an emergency.
• • •
Faith is a thing of curious limitations. The
Australian does not believe in Providence as
a reliable source of moisture. Therefore, he
constructs water-works, tanks, dams, irriga-
tion works, and artesian wells. So far as these
go he recognises that the Unseen won’talterthe
climate of his thirst-cursed land, and that no
amount of devotion will provide a regular
water-supply, so he falls back on the pick
and the spade. But, as he doesn’t make
enough tanksand reservoirs and artesian wells,
and as he lets the surplus waters of good
seasons run to waste, and his irrigation works
are miserably insufficient, and he devotes no
intelligent attention to forest culture as a
means of increasing the rainfall, and is guilty
of neglect in many other shapes he is still
troubled with seasons of awful scarcity. He
takes the drought at first philosophically,
then anxiously, and then in a spirit of despera-
tion, and in this latter stage he appeals to
the Deity and the local member of Parlia-
ment indifferently, on the principle of the
drowning man who catches at a straw. The
Deity is the last resource, and, in his mind,
clearly represents the straw. His belief is
first in his tank, and last of all in Provi-
dence ; if his tank were larger he would not
fall back on Providence at so early a date ;
if it were large enough he would not fall
back on Providence at all. The fact that
he has spent vast sums on boring artesian
wells in the thirsty West is the natural result
of past experience to the effect that Heaven
will not alter climatic conditions at his re-
quest, and that the Western plains being
dry by nature they will remain so unless
their state is altered by hard, manual labor.
The periodical drought is part of the regular
climatic conditions of the country. There-
fore, in asking the shadowy Power, which
he has half-forgotten, and which he less than
half believes in, to arrest the drought before
its time, he asks It to perform a miracle,
while, at the same time, his works express,
in a feeble, unfinished fashion, his convic-
tion that miracles are extinct —if they ever
existed.
• * •
If the thirsty Australian prays for rain it
will come —some day. If he did not pray it
would also come—some day. As no one can
prove that it would or would not in either
case have come on the same day, or on any
other day, the question of the efficacy of
prayer will never be wholly settled. The
droughts which existed in Australia in the
days when the black aboriginal owned the
land and religion had not even been heard
of, came to their natural end without any
request for Divine interposition, and no one
can say that they would have come to an
end sooner under other circumstances. Rain
is not necessarily caused by prayer because
it comes after it, any more than Sydney
cathedral was caused by the crusades—if it
were, then it would be equally true that
prayer was caused by rain if the position of
events was reversed. No single individual
in all this earth can show by any reliable
evidence that prayer ever brought rain down
before its time, or that it ever altered the
natural course of events in any other respect
by so much as one hair’s-breadth. It rains
alike on the just and the unjust—on the just
man who prays and the unjust one who
scoffs ; therefore, if all men were just, or all
were unjust, the end of the drought-season
would arrive just the same.
# # •
With the diffusion of knowledge it has
come to be more and more recognised that
this little, unobtrusive globe, as well as the
rest of the universe, is governed by fixed
laws, and that no supernatural power goes
out of its way to suspend these laws under
any circumstances whatsoever. Therefore,
man drains the Koo-wee-rup swamp by busi-
ness-like toil, on the understanding that no
appeal to any Divinity will remove moisture
from low-lying regions that have no natural
outlet, and that unless he learns how to do
it himself things must remain as they are.
In earlier days, before engineering became
an art, he would probably have prayed for
its removal, if the said removal would have
involved any great personal benefit, and
been much disappointed at the result ; and
to-day, if he knew that his ancestors had
endeavored to remove moisture by prayer,
he would comment strongly on their ignor-
ance, and rightly assume that their appeal
was not answered. And some centuries
hence, when man has learned—as he pos-
sibly will sooner or later—how to control
the rainfall by scientific means, he will look
back with the same scorn upon the ignor-
amus of to-day who supplicates his Divinity
for the removal of droughts, instead of re-
moving them by hand according to a per-
fectly simple process, and he will also rightly
assume that the appeal brought no response.
The man of the nineteenth century who calls
upon Heaven to remove the present dry
spell before its time has no right to ridicule
the much more devout person of the tenth
century, who asked the same Divinity to
remove comets before their time. And
when he recognises the fact that the tailed
portent preserved its ordinary course, and
departed not one second the sooner for all
these supplications, he gives away his whole
creed in one sentence.
• * *
The little that is known of the mystery
of the Cosmos goes to show that every-
thing has a cause, and is itself the cause
of other things' 1 which come after it, and
that every cause works out its result to
the end. Therefore, the ages are an un-
broken chain of events, not one link of
which can be displaced without upsetting
the past and the future, as well as the pre-
sent. The causes of the present drought
began when the first wind blew upon the
surface of chaos. The whole series of events
has been leading up to it for billions of ages,
since before the time of the cave-man, and
the glacial period, and the days before the
glacial period was in its infancy. It could
not have existed but for the atmospheric
disturbance which some ape-like ancestor
made in Asia Minor when he fanned his
decaying fire with a leaf to accelerate the
cooking of the hindquarter of the wild ass.
It is partly owing to the hiccups of the first
recorded “drunk” under the shadow of
Ararat, and partly to the fiery breeze which
rose from burning Sodom, and partly to the
temporary cooling of a few pints of air
beneath the shade of Jonah’s gourd. And,
for the rest, it is caused by everything that
was done or left undone since the beginning
of all things, to the rustle of every leaf on
every tree in five continents, to all things
that are or ever have been ; and it, in turn,
is an essential feature in deciding the destiny
of everything that is now or that is to be as
long as this globe lasts and, it may be,
even longer. And the people who ask their
Deity to interfere with the course of nature
ask Him, not only to reverse the whole course
of the future, but to undo the past from its
commencement to now.
* * #
Some day, probably, man will learn how
to control the rainfall, and thus avert the
disasters of drought and flood. If so, he
will learn it as part of an orderly sequence of
events which began when the first man, or
semblance of a man, first learned anything.
Then, instead of one drought being cut short
by a violent interference with the usual
order of things, all such calamities will cease
through the operation of a cause which was
in preparation before protoplasm—if there
ever was such a thing—began to exist. Mean-
while, experience teaches this thing above all
others : The world is a slaughter-house,
where no Power interferes to save anyone
from the consequences of his own ignorance,
no matter whether he is personally responsible
for his ignorance or not. If he lives by a
malarious swamp, because he knows nothing
of sanitation, he dies of ague, or yellow-
fever, or some other complaint suited to his
surroundings. If he has granular disease
of the kidneys it kills him, because he has
not yet learned how to cure that ailment.
If he acquires true leprosy the result will be
fatal, humiliationand prayer notwithstanding,
unless someone discovers the remedy which
has hitherto been sought in vain. The
question whether the discovery of the
remedy will be a divine answer to prayer or
a mere earthly circumstance is one which is
not worth discussing. And, similarly, the
drought-curse will only cease in this dry
land when some remedy is provided by
human agency, and that remedy will be the
inevitable consequence of an unbroken chain
of circumstance whose beginning was before
the commencement of antiquity. What
supernatural power was the cause of that
beginning is a question which this paper
leaves theology to determine in any way
that suits it best. At the same time
its faith in the modern parson as a
means of throwing light on that stupen-
dous mystery is strictly limited. The
mystery is so vast, and the expounder
thereof is so small, and so mentally abbre-
viated, and so chained to the literal inter-
pretation of an impossible Genesis, that the
two are hopelessly incongruous.
A Social Cancer.
The knife of the abortionist may have slain
Jessie Nicholls, but Society placed the knife
in the abortionist’s hand. So long as social
traditions make motherhood out of wedlock a
social shame, so long will the trade in abortion
flourish, and so long will ignorant girls risk
death rather than “ disgrace.”
* * m
“Disgrace!” The “disgrace” of being a
mother, of performing the sacredest and most
necessary function of humanity ! The “ dis-
grace ” of the supreme self-sacrifice which lights
the Madonna’s face with its ineffable glow—
that mystery of beauty which a thousand men
of genius have vainly sought to place on canvas,
while Nature mocks them from the eyes of the
humblest peasant-girl who bends over her babe !
The “disgrace,” being a woman, of flowering
into perfect womanhood, of going down into
the Valley of the Shadow of Death to bring up
the jewel of a new life, of making the race one’s
debtor, and placing in the hands of Existence
another torch to light the path through the
voids of Eternity ! Motherhood a “ disgrace ”
under any circumstances whatever ! What a
grotesque perversion of ideas !
* Hr *
Vet to this perversion, which conventional
religion has embedded in her sex, Jessie
Nicholls fell a victim. Continually before
her uprose the vision of the pointed finger, and
the sidelong glance, and the skirts hastily
gathered from the reach of “contamination,”
which besets every girl who has dared to give
way to natural impulses without waiting for
conventional religion to patter its formulas.
Jessie Nicholls’s blood was spilt on the altar
of the Moloch of Chastity, whose priest is the
abortionist, and whose worshippers are “ vir-
tuous women.”
* * »
Only the packing-case in a Woolloomooloo
lane is new : the rest is an old story. From
the number who trade in abortion, notwith-
standing the risks, judge the profits and the
number of clients. From the number of deaths
discovered, judge the number successfully
hidden. Effects so widespread must have a pro-
found cause. But Society deals always with
the effects, never with the cause. Society
drives a girl to the abortionist, who kills her
and her child, and then Society, if it is lucky
enough to catch him, and if the circumstances
are sensational enough, hangs the abortionist.
Three lives are blotted out, and Society goes to
sleep again—surrounded by other abortionists.
As if a physician, set to cure a smallpox
patient, were carefully to extirpate one pustule,
heedless of the deadly virus coursing through
the whole body I
« « *
Abortion will never be ended until public
opinion is enlightened enough to understand
that the difference between chastity and un-
chastity, legitimacy and illegitimacy, is not a
difference of morality or religion, as those terms
are generally understood, but one purely of
individual and social convenience.
* * *
Abortion may be restricted by providing hos-
pitals where the only conditions of admission
are imminent motherhood and no questions
asked. No woman not a patient should be
allowed within five miles of any hospital. A
possible man under the bed should never hear
Lady Blank, the patroness, or Mrs. Dash, the
committee-woman, clinch routine queries with
the inevitable, “And now, my dear, who is
he '( ” The hospital should take charge of the
children, if desired, and should return them to
the mothers when desired. Only voluntary
contributions to the cost of the children’s main-
tenance should be received. So the State may
save many good mothers and rear many good
citizens.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Kanakaland Judgeship.
The jobbery in connection with the vacant Q.
Judgeship is an interesting example of the
gentle art of bleeding the country. In the
first place, the deceased Justice Harding’s
place need not have been filled at all. Five
Supreme Court Judges sitting in Brisbane are a
wasteful and ridiculous excess for a 400,000
population distributed over 668,000 sq. miles
of territory. Three Judges could easily do all
the work brought before the court at present,
or likely to be brought before it for a long time
to come. The fourth should undoubtedly be
sent on circuit in the Central district. In the
second place, if the vacancy w T ere filled, it
should be filled by the promotion of one of the
three District Court Judges, Paul, Noel, and
Miller —all well-qualified men who deserve
a step up. But the shocking Q. Govt,
couldn’t let slip such a fine chance of
patronage. Attorney-General Byrnes, of
course, had the first offer of the vacancy, but
that acute personage probably aims to follow in
the footsteps of his master Griffith, and lift
himself through the Premiership into a £3500-
a-year Chief Justiceship. It is far too early for
him to write “ Closed ” on the door of his poli-
tical career. So the claims of broken-down
politician Garrick, one of Griffith’s tame
poodles in the palmy old Liberal days, were
warmly espoused by the Griffith-Byrnes
alliance. Griffith made Garrick Agent-
General 10 years ago, and he has hung on to
the office like a leech ever since. MTlwraith
dislodged him in ’BB, but Griffith apparently
made his reinstatement a condition of the
’9O Coalition, and the bebaubled James
has “ been there ” till date. Several
times there has been talk of recall, and
two years ago M'llwraith actually sent
Wm. Forrest to England to succeed him, but
Garrick managed to hold on. He is over 60,
his health is not good, and he makes an exceed-
ingly bad Agent-General. He would make a
much worse Judge, seeing that he has not
practised at law for about a dozen years, that
he wasn’t much of a lawyer when he did prac-
tise, and that his long absence from the profes-
sion and the province has put him out of touch
with both. Apart from that, Queensland
wants on its Supreme Court Bench young and
vigorous men, not elderly valetudinarians.
The struggle in Garrick’s mind must be in-
teresting. He badly wants the judicial pension,
but lie doesn’t want to leave London, and he
doesn’t want to expose himself as a Judge. At
time of writing, he hasn’t decided whether to
come or not, but the awful Q. Government is
urging him all it knows how, for the Agent-
Generalship is wanted for invalid MTlwraith,
and when the two nominees drop out shortly,
as they are bound to do in the natural course of
things, there will be more patronage which can
be liened as may seem desirable. The electors
are expected to sit down under this sort of
thing quite patiently. The Q. elector rather
prefers to be kicked before he licks the boots of
the gang in office.
A Representative Citizen.
In modern Melbourne the term “ representa-
tive citizen ” has lost all respect. It has de-
generated into a scornful epithet applied by
irate cab-drivers to over-zealous policemen, and
hurled at merchants by hands who find their
services hastily dispensed with. Conferred
upon a bishop it is flat blasphemy. And this
peculiar state of affairs is easily understood.
Mayor Bingy Benjamin and Speaker Matthew
Davies were contemporaneously the two
gaudiest citizens that Smellbourne ever boasted.
Speaker Bent, if less gaudy, was distinctly
more aggressive, and the city hasn’t forgotten
the spectacle of a representative Bent and
Benjamin paying homage to ex-representative
Davies on his return from a trip to London via
America. Premier Munro was still the most
exalted citizen of them all when 4000 of his
fellow-creatures crushed into the Town Hall to
proclaim him unfit for the Agent-Generalship,
and another 4000 swelled the roar of derision
from outside the building. These are matters
of history, showing how the possession of a
dignified place in Victoria’s capital justifies a
strong suspicion that the holder ought not to
be there. Likewise they are presumptive
evidence that any Victorian whom the
Queen delights to honor deserves no particular
honor in his own country—a reflection which


A monster kangaroo—9ft. 6in. from tip to
tip— was run down and killed at Sullivan Bros.’
Rock Forest estate (N.S.W.), a few days ago. It
killed two dogs ere it got its death-blow.
many knights of the Michael and George
order can put in their pipes and smoke at
leisure. Lawyer Snowden, Mayor of Mel-
bourne, is one of the latest recruits to the
Davies and Benjamin rank. The Queen
honored him on her last birthday. He was a
“ representative ” citizen; the old lady s advisers
had not profited by experience ; so she made
worshipful Snowden a K.O.M.G. Therefore,
in the natural course of events, when this newly-
dignified person attempted to preside over a
great public meeting, convened for the purpose
of considering a question that touches Mel-
bourne’s heart, brain and stomach, the multi-
tude present arose and practically spat Snow-
den off his representative platform. True, the
promoters of this popular gathering had in-
vited the Mayor to take the chair. F rom a
Tory point of view they assumed that his office
made him a fit and proper person to preside
over them, but they reckoned without the host
of unorthodox humanity seated elsewhere than
upon the platform. To these it seemed that
a meeting of protestants against Sweating
was morally bound to repudiate any
official connection with a lawyer-mayor, who,
by his acts and utterances, had proved himself
the Sweaters’ patron. It was nothing to them
that Snowden is now in his third year of civic
glory, aK.C.M.G., and a Leg. Councillor elected
without opposition. They judged him on his
merits in relation to the labor question, not as
a representative figurehead at quarterly lun-
cheons, and on his merits they despised and
rejected their quaking chairman. A more
vigorous outbreak of indignation has not oc-
curred in Melbourne since the night when the
same hall resounded with jeers for James
Munro. It was a healthy explosion which
cannot fail to leave a good influence behind it.
The representative Snowden stands revealed in
his true public light. This triplicate Mayor
and baubled citizen, this new ornament of the
Legislative Council, is so notoriously out of
sympathy with popular feeling that the people
refuse to tolerate his presence, even under the
auspices of their favorite spokesman, if a popu-
lar grievance comes up for discussion in the
L'own Hall.
The Yellow Man in the Golden West.
One clause in the Westralia Goldfields Bill,
providing for issue of miners’ rights and busi-
ness licenses, puts a wholesome embargo on
Asiatic and African aliens. Colored claimants
to the glorious, etc., rights of British citizen-
ship can only be licensed to mine or trade “ by
authority of the Minister.” Premier Forrest
“ thought the clause would prevent all such
from obtaining rights at all.” Moran (member
for Yilgarn) wanted the embargo to be made
stringent and absolute so as to bind future as
well as present Ministers, and then R. F. Suoll
rose on his hind legs breathing a hot gale of
jingo wind :
Clause 12 I also object to, with the hon. member for
Yilgarn, and that is as to the proviso, which says:—“ No
miner’s right, consolidated miner's right, or business
license shall he issued to any Asiatic or African claiming
1,0 be a British subject, without the authority of the
Minister first had and obtained.” I presume this relates
lo Asiatics or Africans who are British subjects, and I
consider this is an interference with the rights of British
subjects. It is the inherent right of all British subjects
to be placed on equality, in whatever part of Her
Majesty’s dominions they may he. The hon. member for
Yilgarn says he draws the line at color ; hut when wecon-
sider that India, which is one of the most important
parts of Her Majesty’s dominions, is occupied by a
race of a different color from ours, it is hardly for this
small colony to say we will not admit British subjects
from India on the same terms as ourselves. I think this
proviso will jeopardise the passing of this Bill, because
Her Majesty s representative in this colony, when he
finds there is an interference with the liberty and rights
of British subjects, will not be doing his duty to his
Sovereign unless he reserves the Bill by sending it Home
for Her Majesty’s assent. When this Bill gets into com-
mittee, I shall move to strike out the proviso ; and, if not
successful, I shall afterwards make it my business to draw
the attention of Her Majesty’s representative in the
colony to this matter.
Haw ! The lonely idea wandering about in
Sholl’s head - cavity that the abject Hindoo
from India’s cholera strand can willy - nilly
claim citizenship anywhere in Australia, is so
inherently vicious as to suggest the possibility
of its being correct in law. Anyhow, such law
is like silence—name it and you break it. Aus-
tralian Parliaments are used to having their
own foolish way, filling up the gaps with God
save the Queen and refreshments —but imme-
diately the Queen tried to save herself or her
Asiatic abjections at Australia’s expense, the
true inwardness of the British connection
would dawn on our legislators. The Rulletin
hereby offers its assistance to Sholl to stir up
Acting-Governor Onslow, and all the official
foolishness Onslow stands for, to enforce Aus-
tralia’s political obligations to certain festering
millions of Asia. The sooner Australia gets
the opportunity of sitting down hard on some
inherent rights of “Her Majesty’s” yellow
men the better.
The Parsbn and Private Judgment.
Judge Williams addressed a few remarks to
the Melbourne Methodists on a recent Sunday,
the burden of which was that dogmas w ere of
little importance compared to kindness, honesty
and other virtues, and that it was possible to
be a good man and yet be “ wholly unortho-
dox.” The natural result was that the Churches
boiled over with a beautiful unanimity all the
next week. This was only to be expected ; a
theory which implies that true piety doesn’t
necessarily involve going to church and con-
tributing to the salary of the man who grinds
out the tale of woe in the pulpit, always makes
the Church boil over furiously. The parson
who boiled hardest was the well-known Dr.
Rentoul, who began his reply by remarking
that “no one would take too seriously Judge
Williams’s advent into the higher regions
of theology ; whatever his opinions were they
c«uld be of no importance.” Of course, Mr.
Williams, being a layman, bis opinions are of
no importance in the eye of an infuriated cleric.
The Protestant churches all professedly travel
on the doctrine of freedom of conscience and
the right of private judgment—provided the
owner of the conscience adheres absolutely to
one or other of their various forms of ortho-
doxy. But if he doesn’t, then Rentoul
asks with a snort what any layman wants with
a conscience or a private judgment of his own
if he won’t use them the way he is told; and
rises to point out that no one but parsons and
people who think as parsons direct them
knows anything about religion, and when
a person who doesn’t believe as the parsons
direct starts to believe at all he is a
theological impostor. The fact that the Pro-
testant parsons have some 200 different systems
of theology, about which they contradict each
other constantly, doesn’t in the least diminish
this high-toned contempt for the layman who
has a 201st system of his own ; the fact that at
least 199 of their own creeds must be wrong in
no way tones down their lofty assurance that
his one creed can’t possibly be right. The
Rentoul attitude practically amounts to this :
Every man has the right to think as he pleases
provided he pleases to think in the way some
recognised variety of preacher tells him to, and
not otherwise. But if he starts thinking out-
side the well-worn grooves of preacherdom,
then the cleric rises to ask in tones of wither-
ing scorn what right a common lay person has
to think, or imagine he thinks, at all? Which
is just the question that the Holy Roman
Church used to ask in the Middle Ages.
The A.A. Monopoly.
About 70 years ago sundry English speculators,
calling themselves the Australian Agricultural
Co., applied to the lamented George IV. for a
grant of land in N.S. Wales, and that benevo-
lent monarch promptly handed over to them
a little more than a million acres at the nominal
price, so far as can be ascertained from some
confused old documents, of <£22,500. There
seems to be considerable doubt as to whether
this amount was ever paid in full; at all events,
if it was, none of the money ever came to New
South Wales. The co. also got 2000 acres of
valuable coal-lands, and a 20-years’ monopoly
of the coal-mining industry in the Hunter River
district. Since the beginning of its career it
has collected its rents and profits assiduously,
and worked its coal-mining property to the best
of its ability. Its grants remain almost intact.
It owns 464,640 acres of very valuable land
in the vicinity of Newcastle, including three
river frontages. It possesses 313,298 acres in
the neighbourhood of Tamwortb, and 249,600
acres in the vicinity of Gunnedah. Its territory
covers nearly half the county of Parry ; about
one-fifth of the county of Gloucester ; and
something like one-sixth of the county of Buck-
land. Over a great part of these areas it blocks
settlement assiduously, and as there is no
land-tax in New South Wales, and no
system of local government within its domains,
it contributes practically nothing to the revenue
and never has contributed anything worth
mentioning. Last year—a year of unexampled
depression in its history—it took out of the
country nearly £70,000, of which £55,000 was
clear profit to its British shareholders —a sum
which is about equal to all the English money
it actually brought into the country in all its
lengthy career. N.S. Wales has paid this great
incorporated landlord, as far as can be esti-
mated from the scanty sources of informa-
tion available, directly or indirectly, between
£1,000,000 and £2,000,000 for no other reason
save that it does the country the honor
to exist and draw its profits regularly. As
land grows more valuable through the increas-
ing demand it will go on paying the Agricul-
tural Go. for existing and hindering the national
progress at a steadily-increasing rate ; the fact
that the co.’s shares, nominally paid-up to
£2l 10s., are now selling in London at £66 10s.,
is a sufficient evidence of the excellence of its
present prospects. If New South Wales desires
to get rid of the incubus, this valuable British
institution will probably want anywhere
between one and two millions as payment for
leaving the country which it has burdened with
its presence for nearly 70 years. Altogether,
the famous A.A. Co. is about the most power-
ful argument in favor of a graduated
land-tax which could be imagined. Under
the scheme now before Parliament this
gigantic foreign monopoly will be taxed
at exactly the same rate as the owner of
a comparatively small estate who has paid to
the Treasury the full value of his holding. In
Maoriland the A. A. Co. would be struck by an
ordinary land-tax of Id. in the £ per anqumon
the capital value of its property, and, in addi-
tion, a graduated land-tax of 2d. in the £ for
the reason that its estate is above the value of
£210,000. This would mean that the State
would annex one-fourth of the profits, and the
probable result would be that the A.A. Co.
would sell out, at a considerable reduction, to
small holders, and the country would no longer
be under an obligation to find 12J or 15 per-
cent. dividends for the British shareholders.
Also, if it didn’t sell out and leave the country,
an absentee tax and a heavy Local Government
tax would further reduce the profits, and the
A.A. Co. would probably contribute £25,000 a
year or so to the revenue. In New South
Wales, even under the Reid land-tax, it won’t
contribute anything worth mentioning.
The Anglo-Jap Treaty.
A person of no special account named Brown
moved the adjournment of New South Wales
Council one day last week “ to call attention to
the neglect of the Government to take steps to
bring the colony within the operation of the
commercial treaty agreed to some time ago
between Great Britain and Japan.” And in
reply to Brown, Attorney-General Want ex-
plained that the Government had, as yet, come
to no decision on the subject, but it recognised
that the treaty “bristled with advantages,”
and “ when anything definite was done the
House would be at once informed.” Consider-
ing the circumstances of the case, it evidently
needs to be impressed upon the Government
that the House must be informed before any-
thing definite is done—a good while before.
The Anglo-Japanese treaty is about the most
ruinous agreement to which Australia could
possibly be a party. Its main provisions are
that there shall be Freetrade, or something
very closely resembling it, between Britain
and Japan, and that the subjects of either State
entering the territories of the other shall be
subject to no disabilities which do not apply to
all other foreigners. It is also provided
that Australia shall be included in the
treaty if it so desires. The arrangement is
eminently satisfactory so far as Britain
is concerned. That country admits the
goods of all the world free, with a very
few exceptions, therefore it only gives to Japan
what it would have given in any case, and gets
a substantial concession in return. Britain is a
great manufacturing country on cheap - labor
principles, and, under the treaty, will probably
sell large quantities of goods to Japan and do
pretty well out of the business. And whereas
the English capitalist will probably pervade
Japan in considerable numbers, and build rail-
ways and open up mines and run syndicates to
his own great advantage, the Jap isn’t likely to
come to England in any number, and even if
he did he would hardly be noticed among
40,000,000 inhabitants, and the fact that his
goods come there won’t make things much worse
than they are, seeing that England is already
flooded with the goods of all nations. But, if
New South Wales or any other Australasian
province becomes a party to the treaty, the
Japanese cheap laborer will come here in
myriads, this being the most conveniently
situated country on earth for a Jap inva-
sion, and he can’t be poll - taxed or
otherwise discouraged. Also, his cheap-
labor products will come in abundance and
swamp this country’s comparatively small and
feeble industries, which, under existing wage
conditions and working on eight-hour prin-
ciples, cannot possibly compete with the irrup-
tion ; and, except for a small and very dubious
trade in meat and wool —if the cotton-clad and
vegetarian Japanese wants meat and wool,
which is doubtful—there is nothing which Aus-
tralia can sell in return. The Anglo-Jap.
agreement is a good enough bargain for
England. For Australia it is so utterly one-
sided and ruinous that it comes as a hair-
raising surprise to learn that the Reid Govern-
ment has found anything to consider in the
matter. According to Mr. Want, “the
Government wants to eliminate the objection-
able clauses so as to obtain the advantages
without the evils.” It would be interesting to
know what are the advantages, and what view
the Ministry takes of the evils, and which
objectionable clauses it wants to eliminate out
of a measure which is objectionable all through.
The Bulletin has again and again called in a
loud voice on Tories and all anti-Socialists to
enact the minimum wage for protected indus-
tries. This provision is needed because Pro-
tection can only checkmate the monopolist
without, leaving the monopolist within un-
hampered. Fat Man swears that the minimum
wage is a dangerous idea, and an impedi-
ment to the glorious doctrine of competition,
and The Bulletin’s suggestion is “economi-
cally ” so suicidal as to command the admiration
The Argus has ’verted to The Bulletin doc-
trine. Witness 10th September, 1893 :
Now the desire is (or should be) that the operatives in
all these trades should receive some benefit from the high
duties which protect their employers. Everyone will
admit the justice of that contention. Why not enforce
it? Parliament has the power ; it has the right to say to
the master in any particular trade, “You are defended
against outside rivals; you have license to bleed the
people generally; but you are required to give a fair
share of the plunder which comes to you to the men and
women who work (or you.” Mr. Trenwitii, Mr. Prbn-
dbrgast, Mr. Bromlbt, and other Corner members owe it
as a sacred duty to their constituents to do something
tangible for their benefit before the tariff passes out of
hand, by inducing the Assembly to apply the minimum-
wage principle to protected industries.
In view of the obvious manner in which the
S.M. Herald frowns down any attempt to have
the Mount Rennie iniquity discussed in N.S. W.
Parliament (as evidenced by its exclusion from
its general summary of all mention of the
matter whenever it crops up in the Assembly),
it is interesting to remember that the Herald,
up to the very morning of the massacre, strongly
fa voted the hangingof the Mount Rennie convicts.
Yet on 7th Jam, the day of blood, it asked for
a reprieve—though by the time its leader came
under the eyes of most of its readers at the
breakfast-table, the four condemned boys were
dangling in the air ! The circumstances of the
trial, theretofore persistently urged by The
Bulletin alone, are alluded to in the Herald
(post-mortem) leader of that morning in these
words:—
It is the very essence of our criminal jurisprudence that
every criminal should not only be allowed the benefit of
counsel in his defence, but that every reasonable facility
should be afforded him by the court as well as by the
Crown. It has rarely happened that any cause for com-
plaint in this respect has arisen of late years ; but the
present instance is a proof that even this merciful rule is
not without its exceptions. Whatever the facilities
afforded to prisoners on their trial may be, they can be
of little avail when the sittings of the Court are pro-
tracted from day to day to such an extreme as to exhaust
the physical and mental energies of counsel and jurymen
alike. The celebrated Ticiibornb trial in England was
only one of many instances in which courts presided over
by the most eminent of English Judges have patiently
sat from day to day, for weeks together, for the purpose
of administering justice in the merciful spirit of English
law. But no case has been heard of in modern times in
which English Judges have compelled Jurors and counsel
in criminal cases invoicing issues of life and death to sit
all through the night in 'spite of repeated protests and
requests for adjournment.
Sooner or later, this much-shirked subject will
have to be debated in Parliament. It will
yet be thoroughly debated in spite of the general
legal conspiracy to smother it up in the interests
of the Judge ; despite the shuffling of political
lawyers too cowardly to do justice to one of a
number of persons who are indirectly the cus-
todians of the crusts for which those political
lawyers hunt when at the Bar.
Tub N.S. VV. Government proposes to spend
,£200,000 or so on forest-thinning, and it is
already spending large sums for lifting sand
from one place and putting it down in another,
both of which works are just about as useful as
shovelling the sea. Query : How much cheaper
is this than Protection, even as the Freetrade
party represents it to be? The Reid Govern-
ment proposes to destroy the N.S.W. sugar-
industry, which employs over 8000 bread 1
winners, on the ground that it costs the people
,£109,000 a year in the shape of a Protective
duty, which amount, by the way, helps to eke
out the public income, and saves .£109,000 a
year in other taxation. And, allowing
the Government pays only 10s. a week per man
in wages, it will cost .£208,000 a year in wages
alone to keep these 8000 people employed at
forest-thinning ; ako the State will have to pay
for tools, railway transportation and super-
vision, and it will lose the £109,000 which the
Treasury now receives. Even if protected
industries are as costly as they are said to be,
how do they compare with Freetrade, coupled
with sand-shifting and forest-thinning? Also,
what kind of an asset is a thinned forest or a
sand-heap that has been removed to a new
location ? The Bulletin hereby recommends
G. H. Reid to set the unemployed to baling-
out the Beautiful Harbor with pannikins. It
would save the railway-fares and supervision
expenses, anyhow.
The re-trial of prisoner Johnson, at present
serving a three years’ sentence for perjury,
said to have been committed in connection with
the celebrated Butler (N.S.W.) divorce-case,
is interesting in more ways than one. John-
son was committed by Judge Windeyer, and
tried and sentenced by Chief Justice Darley.
The depositions of the enquiry just concluded,
together with the presiding Judge’s report,
have been sent through the Justice Dept, to
the Lieut.-Governor (who, for the time being,
is Darley, C.J., aforesaid) for his decision.
Hence, if he decides that Johnson is to be
released, he is giving himself a back-handed
knock in his judicial capacity ; and, if he con-
siders Johnson must finish his sentence, it is
open for anyone to make invidious remarks.
Two facts protrude here. Firstly, no Judge
should himself send for trial any man whom he
accuses of perjury. The fact of a man being
committed by a Judge goes about half-way
towards convicting him, whether he is guilty
or innocent, inasmuch as the trusting public
still retains a superstitious respect for the
opinions of all Judges. All perjury-proceed-
ings should be taken by the Attorney-General.
Secondly, as The Bulletin pointed out long
ago, and as is provided by the Hew South Wales
charter of justice, no Chief Justice should
occupy any other paid position. “No man is a
competent judge in his own cause.”

Sydney Royal re-opened on Saturday in a
state of mitigated magnificence. The new decora-
tions are a very moderate success, and the aggre-
gate effect is greeny-yallery and feebly esthetic.
The general effect of “My Sweetheart” is also
disappointing. As it was played by Minnie
Palmer, some eight years ago, it was a pretty
little nothing about a wholesome, loyal country
girl who loved a lazy, picturesque youth in broken
German, and at last married him, after the other
woman was satisfactorily bowled out, and made
him a much better wife than he deserved. The
charm of it, on that previous occasion, lay in Minnie
Palmer’s brilliance and quaint prettiness, and in
Roberts’s picturesqueness and broken German;
and the dances and songs and miscellaneous bric-
a-brac filled up the rest in a cheerful, frothy kind
of way. Unluckily, William Gourlay’s thin,
anxious face doesn’t look in the least like the
laughing, irresponsible Tony. He acts the part
well enough, though he is rather cold and mechani-
cal at times, and his broken English wants to be
iialf-soled, and is gone in the front wheel, and
seems to have fallen with the buttered side down
and broken its knees, so to speak; but his haggard
eye is an impossible circumstance all through.
Miss Amy Gourlay, too, though she is a decided
improvement on brother William, is a painful
reminder of the Minnie of long ago; her singing
voice isn’t quite equal to the occasion, and her
dancing is commonplace, and, though she does
some excellent comedy here and there, the sum-
total is disappointing. The one unqualified suc-
cess of the play is the clever and graceful Nellie
Mortyne in the part of the lady with a horrid
past. Also, Ralph Roberts and Charles Field do
some respectable work, and the stupendous figure
of Mario Majeroni stalks around like a bird of
Jrey on top of a high mountain, and Miss Maggie
'ord fills an ugly part with success and a pair of
spectacles.
• # •
Somebody has been re-writing “My
Sweetheart ” since Minnie Palmer first produced it
in Sydney, and that person’s improvements don’t
keep step with the original. Tony is amusing at
first, but towards the long, sad end of Act 111.
his brilliant dialogue with Tina degenerates into
such remarks as “You vas a snoozer ! ” “I vas
not a snoozer ! ” “ Nein ! ” “ Yah !” or some-
thing to that effect. Also, there is a long and
dreadful discussion about the way to spell
“potatoes,” and, to lend point to it, the fierce
lady with the spectacles spills a quantity of
potatoes in Acts I. and 111., and the drawing-
room in Act 11. reveals some of the vegetables
she dropped in Act I. still lying on the floor as an
evidence of good faith. Further, Tina’s first
lover, the young doctor, has been turned into a
grizzled old person who looks like a gamekeeper
in a decayed state, and various other things have
been done to “My Sweetheart ” which come
under the head of unprovoked assault and battery.
mm * *
William Elton’s “Judge,” at the Criterion,
is even a better show than The Bulletin re-
ported it in last issue, and the comparative weak-
ness of the public support is unaccountable. Pos-
sibly the public objects to the Judge washing
his feet on the stage, though, considering the
things that Australian Judges wash on the bench
without arousing much hostile comment, this
seems unlikely. Or, it may be that the hurried
incursion of the Judge in his night-shirt when he
is looking for the wooden infant’s feeding-bottle
does not accord with its finer feelings; but as he
is obviously not wholly dependent on the night-
shirt this seems unreasonable. Anyhow, a good
part of the community stays away with great suc-
cess, and thereby loses one of the best shows that
ever brought the judicial office into well-deserved
contempt.

The two Sydney variety theatres continue
to live up to their srood reputations. Also, Prof.
Kennedy consistently lives up to the bad character
which The Bulletin gave him last week and the
one before, and which it will gladly give him next
week also, and on various other occasions, unless
he removes from the oity or mends the ghoulish-
ness of his ways. A show which consists mainly
of a fat man persuading a hypnotised thin man to
drink kerosene and chew onions is an unclean
aggravation and a sewer-gas.
»* * *
The Comic Opera co. begins a brief season
at Sydney Lyceum this Saturday, the programme
for the first week being four nights of “MaMie
Rosette ” and two of “ Madame Angot.” In view
of recent developments in the moral atmosphere,
the management considers it advisable to show
once more the fatal results that ensue, when a
guileless maiden in a brief frock takes a cup of
milk to the palace late at night without a chaperon.
The awful results of milk will be demonstrated in
the co.’s best style, assisted by first-rate scenery
snd accessories.

To-morrow (Friday), “Joseph of Canaan”
will resist the charms of Potiphar’s lady for the
last time. At least, he will do so if the business
justifies the sacrifice, but if the house is very thin
ifc is just possible that Joseph will simply remark
that it isn’t worth while keeping up the semblance
of virtue any longer to please such a thin audience,
and will elope with Mrs. Potiphar on the spot.
This would necessitate a revision of the last two
acts, but if Potiphar put a detective on Joseph’s
track, the rest of the evening might be filled in
pleasantly enough. Mr. Rignold makes his very
last appearance at Her Majesty’s on Saturday
when “ OJancarty ” will be played for one night
only.
* m * *
The Jerusalem, of the Sydney Cyclorama,
has come from austere Adelaide via malignant
Melbourne to sinful Sydney, and is an artistic and
magnificent attempt to idealise the ancient, one-
templed, many-hovelled city. It is a poet’s dream;
an artist’s triumph ; it is a Dead Sea painted as a
Lake of Killarney ; a hovel painted as a gorgeous
palace ; a Mount Rennie painted as a Mont Blanc
with sunrise glories. And it probably cost more
than the whole of ancient Jerusalem itself, includ-
ing the Temple, if there ever was one.

Mark Twain has been interviewed on his
arrival in Sydney, and has cordially approved of
the Harbor. Then, being asked about the duel
which Max O’Rell proposed to fight with him, he
seems to have got into a violent frame of mind and
been restrained with difficulty from bad language.
M. Twain is announced to lecture for the first
time at the Protestant Hall to-night (Thursday.)
* * * *
The two portraits printed on page 10 repre-
sent Miss Amy Gourlay and brother William, now
appearing as Tina and Tony, in “My Sweetheart,”
at Sydney Royal. Miss Gourlay is the daughter
of the late Scotch comedian, William Gourlay,
and was born in the bleak land of haggis a few—
certainly not very many—years ago. She has
been on the stage for about seven years, and has
played many parts with the B. and B. co.,
Williamson, Garner and Musgrove, and lately
with Mr. Stokes’s co. through Maoriland and
N. Her last Sydney appearance, if
The Bulletin remembers right, was at
the Criterion. Brother William was born
in Sydney at a comparatively recent date, and
commenced his dramatic career at the age of
three, when he took a hand in his father’s Cale-
donia entertainment. He is a composer as well as
a comedian, and the pretty song, “ Thee I Adore,”
which he sings in “My Sweetheart,” is a little
thing of his own. The brother and sister have
recently finished a highly successful season in
Maoriland, during which they played at Govern-
ment House for one night only, and led Gov.
Glasgow to express himself “deeply gratified.”
And Gov. Glasgow is reputed to be a serious
person who is not often gratified.
* * * *
Bland Holtand “The Prodigal Daughter’’
took a long farewell of Melb. Royal on Saturday
night, and that experienced purveyor of lurid
melodrama is now en route to Maoriland with his
chariots, horses, carefully selected company, and
about 30 tons of scenery, including the Living
Bridge. Bland Holt, as he stated in a last grand
final advt., “ bears a natural antipathy to fare-
well speeches,” so he was allowed to tear himself
silently away, after expressing his feelings in a
series of eloquent smiles. Melbourne will miss
him very much. His season started at a time
when the opposition was strong enough to crush a
feeble class of show, and all the money he
made during twenty • two weeks of solid
success was well earned. The public had
never been offered such a filling entertainment at
the price. For a week or two the Royal will
remain hermetically sealed. The boards whereon
the lowering villains in the long frock-coats used
to enjoy their nightly 2.J hours of evil-doing until
Bland Holt whistled for the police are now being
scrubbed and otherwise made ready for George
Rignold, On October sth at latest G.R, will set
up his Biblical spectacle of “Joseph,” when that
redeemed section of Melbourne society which
abhors burlesque will roll up to witness the temp-
tation of Jacob’s white-haired boy by Mrs.
Potiphar.

» * * »
The return of the Gaiety Girlers from
Sydney ought to stir the city to its vitals. They
appear at the Princess’s on Saturday, starting
with “Gentleman Joe.” Four months have
faded and gone since Spencer-st. station was
moistened with the tears of many johnnies, who
mourned the flying angels of the co. as lost for
ever. The thought that they have fluttered back
again to play another brief engagement seems
altogether heavenly, and the box-plan is being
ticked off with enthusiasm.

The Bulletin takes the present oppor-
tunity to mention that Mrs. Grundy would draw
the line at more than one double entente which
tainted the air of Melb. Opera House, last Satur-
day night. A cerulean joke is often tolerable if
there be a certain delicacy in its tint. But a bold
beastly suggestion only serves to make half-a-
dozen gallery boys laugh, and everybody else
squirm. The Cambridge has wisely dropped the
burlesque portion of its show, substituting an
ordinary second part and a “screamer.”

Dear Bulletin,— Touching certain “Poverty Point”
pars, on the decidedly thin subject of Miss Juliet Wray.
In the first place, Juliet is nut ttie prima-donna of W. and
M.’s co., or she wouldn’t have to stand aside whilst
another girl plays Mu Mie Rosette, Dorothy, and other
singing characters that fell to Nellie Stewart. As a
matter of fact, Wray is engaged at a good salary to play
“leading parts only at the management's discretion.
Nilouche, the tomboy, and Clairette, the fish-fag’s
daughter, are the most successful leading parts which
Miss Juliet has undertaken hitherto. One of your para,
declares her voice to be “as fresh as a linnet’s.” Well,
it isn’t as fresh as the man (or woman) who made that
statement. Another “ Poverty Pointer ” says “ she lias
high notions about studying under Marches! for grand
Opt ra." lam not in the secret of Miss Wray’s notions,
but I'm game to bet you £1 sterling that she has far too
much intelligence to justify any such allegation. Juliet
Wray in grand opera! When are we to be told that
Daisy Chard is going ’Gome to play Lady Macbeth?—
Yours, Weary.

T ie terrible bat at which the nags in the racing-scene of
“ The Prodigal Daughter,” at Melb. Royal, rushed down
the stage was a puzzle to those who only saw the play
from the front. The horses were really started outside
Ah Goon's furniture-shop, 50 yards above the stage-door,
and, after running down Little Bourke-st., would turn
into the theatre up a carefully-prepared slope into the
view of the audience. The back-cloth itself was sus-
pended by two poles right in the middle of Little Bourke-
street, and four or five hundred unpaid supers, Chows,
larrikins and police, could be plainly distinguished from
the circle every night.
Nine West-end London theatres were open on Aug. Ist.
Their programmes included five farcical comedies, three
burlesques, and one melodrama. Ne’er a 41 problem
play,” serious comedy, or classical drama among the lot.
Dear Bulletin,—The special beauty of a chorus lady’s
voice is one of those things which gets discovered by
herself, as a rule. The discovery seldom spreads to the
musical conductor or the manager, who merely hear of
these wonderful song-birds in newspaper pars., and listen
for them in vain at rehearsal. A needy girl would
hardly take a valuable voice into the ordinary comio-
opera chorus when she could turn it to much more profit,
with much less trouble, at one of the variety-shows.—
Yours, Bah.
Lord Wodehouse, who (says the cable) has been struck
off the roll of J’s.P. for making a belligerent ass of him-
self at an election meeting, is, presumably, the Lord
W. whose brougham used to wait for Nellie Farren at the
Gaiety stage-door in her younger days. Lordly admirers
of burlesque ladies seem to incline to pugilism. There
was Lonsdale, addicted to putting Violet Cameron’s hus-
band outside on the mat, and Queensberry, the devoted
follower of Marion Hood to Australia—bruisers both.
None but the brave deserve the fair Gaiety girl.

in a charge of insulting language in Adelaide recently
The trouble arose over a plate of cold pork and roast
potatoes, which did not suit Bernage’s palate. Case dis-
missed without costs.
Brough is despondent about the British drama. He says
there has been no improvement; the theatres are flehting
a losing battle with the music ’alls, probably because the
masses follow the aristocracv in preferring legs to brains.
Brough is doubtful about Mrs. Patrick Campbell, whose
one great success was as the Second Mrs. Tanqueray. He
says Forbes Robertson is a great actor, but Irving still
leads, his acting as the veteran soldier in “A Story of
Waterloo " being grand.

author, Watson, critic of St. James’s Gazette, the latter
to lie first played in Sydney.

Dear Bulletin, —Pressing their claims for pulpit-mono-
poly, Sydney goodies maintain that the theatrical im-
personation of Joseph’s temptation, as bowdlerised at
Her Majesty’s theatre, is immoral. Time enough for
their objections when the theatres give a living picture
of the dramatic Mrs. Potlphar episode, taken straight
from the hook which goodies hand to their innocent
daughters.—T. D.

Penley’s new farcical comedy is based oh the idea of a
sick man regaining health by the transfusion into his
veins of blood from those of a healthy rogue. Naturally
the latter’s proclivities are transferred also. Like the
German tale of the man who had lost an eye, and having
it replaced by that of a cat was for ever catching mice in
the dark.
A London critic recalls to mind Irving’s first production
of “ The Corsican Brothers ” at the Lyceum, when Pinero,
entering early in the character of De Meynard, was
mistaken for Irving and given a mighty reception.
The strong resemblance, in figure, gait and upper part of
the head, betweeen the two actors, was accountable
for the same droll mistake on one or two other first nights
when Pinero had to appear in advance of the star. See-
ing that the author of “ Mrs. Tanqueray ” has remained a
firm friend of his old manager since he retired from the
Lyceum Co. and the “boards,” it’s a strange thing that
he has never appeared in one of his original parts for a
dramatic fund benefit. Perhaps he may lend himself as a
special attraction (and “ Pinero, for one night only,”
would be a draw) when Ellen Terry farewells at fancy
prices.

tendency now being toward melodrama and romantic
plays and sparkling farcical comedy.

Prof. Marshall Hall is now educating his pupils up to
the Wagnerian standard, and people passing the musical
academy’s new abode—the deserted Queen’s Coffee
Palace—flee terror-stricken as sudden blasts burst on to
the pavement from the immortal soul of the corporeally
defunct meister who used to take off his tile to nis owri
image whetiever he looked in the glass.
Dear Bulletin, — It would require a great actor to ade-
quately portray a man lashed to a mast awaiting death
from the explosion of the powder-barrel beneath. The
stock-actor in this thrills the uncritical where the great
actor would astoidsh the most fastidious. In such scenes
the supposed uncultured patron of the “ darrellolloway
panorama-drama” dimly recognises the great opportuni-
ties lacking in the restrained drawing-room comedy, and
with a fully-developed nervous system ekes out the defects
in the acting from his own imagination.—P.

worth mentioning after making a modest pile from bis
share in 44 The World” and 44 Youth.” Merritt and
Pettitt lie near one another in Brompton cemetery.
