A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists lias been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who make*
a continuous use of Unole Toby’* Rolled Oat*.

To read this ! A handsome illustrated catalogue
has just been issued by that old-established drapery
firm of Riley Brothers, 6GB-570 George-st., Syd-
ney. It contains a wonderful amount of informa-
tion, and quotes the lowest prices of good articles
in Household Drapery, Ladies’ and Children’s
Wear, Men’s and Boy’s Clothing, Boots and
Shoes. Riley Brothers still continue the system
originated by them 20 years ago of sending A 5
Drapery Bales and £2 parcels, carriage paid, and
a large business is now done daily in
postal orders from Gd. upwards. One of Riley
Brothers’ latest investments is the purchase of the
entire stock in N. S. W. of Dr. Scott’s celebrated
electric corsets and hair-brushes. Eminent doctors
say, and thousands of testimonials aver, that the
corsets are a splendid remedy for ladies’ ailments,
especially backache, and the electric brushes give
instant relief in oases of sick headache, neuralgia,
sleeplessness.- The corsets are just like ordinary
stays, and perfect-fitting. Sizes only 24 to 20
inches ; price, Gs., post free. The brushes are
pure bristles, not wire; price, 3s. Gd., post free.
Please send to Riley Brothers, 508 570 George-st.,
Sydney, for new catalogue.

ficial to the human constitution as thorough
cleanliness, a theory verified by modern revela-
tions of the deadly microbes which infest the
flesh and blood—especially if we do not keep
ourselves in a clean condition, which ought to be
the first aim of every individual with any esteem
for himself. The use of Amykos has proved to be
very important to this end, by its great sanitary
power over the skin, mouth, teeth, &c. If you
value yourself, never be without this excellent pre-
paration,*

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
September 17, 1895.
My Dear Moorabinda—
The mantle of amateur efficiency possessed by
the “ Magpies ” has fallen on to “The Idlers,”
who gave their first performance, under the new
title, at Sydney Royal Standard last Thursday,
There was a large audience of the strictly society
order —the acting vice-royalties (they’re a star
part themselves just now) seemed to fit the niche
of patronage very well. It was a surprising
audience. Generally—at Sydney Lyceum, for
example—when you see a theatre full of cold
shoulder and boiled starch you settle yourself
down to dream of Babel; but when Society takes
to the boards the residuum of the Hot Tongs
gives a most patient hearing: “dog doesn’t eat
dog.” you know. And herein is a good idea to
settle the first-night-cackle question. Let the
management put salt on the tail of a Potts Point
amateur or two and lure them into walking “ on ”
with a banner when the d.c. threatens to be more
than usually fashionable.
“Going It” was the “Idlers’” worry. It
proved to be a most discreetly abridged version of
“L’Enfant Prodigue,” which made a rattling
farce, and perhaps it was the fact of two adaptors
having a hand in it that made it a little incoherent
here and there. For example, Mr. Gossett
(smartly and distinctly played by Captain
Dangar, whose bourgeois make-up was admirable)
tells Mrs. Dimpleton (brightly sketched by Miss
Austin) that he thinks of her “morning, noon,
and night,” regardless of the fact that he has only
heard of the lady five minutes ago—or when Mrs.
Gossett invited people to dinner “to-morrow”
and they turn up ten minutes afterwards. The
farce was excellently cast. Miss Gilles played
the ingenue with convincing artlessness; Miss
Winnie Austin played the pert, wily handmaiden
to the life—indeed, the only faults on the feminine
side were that the makes-up were not quite bright
enough, and that the dodging of stage love-making
was inartistically pronounced. Miss Qilles’s dive
out of the way of an imminent encirclement in the
last act was desperate enough to win Mrs.
Grundy’s most special approval.
The German baron was convincingly sketched
by Mr. G. H. Clibborn—make-up and accent
being excellent; Mr. H. Pollock made the major
the smooth and airy rogue he ought to be; Mr.
W. L. Rolleston played the lover (always the
worst part in ’chaw performances when hugging is
strictly proltibited) as warmly as circumstances
permitted, though I have no manner of doubt he
could put a lot more realism into it, while Mr. J.
P. Jones as a male prude, Mr. A. G. Lamb as
Bob, Mr. F. B. Wilkinson as a one-line captain,
and Mr. R. Innes as an outspoken valet, were all
letter-perfect and full of the rattling spirit of the
thing. In short, the Idlers went at it as hard as
possible and in a manner quite opposed to their
new title.
Talking of Mrs. Grundy reminds me that pretty
little Ethel Haydon is keeping up her reputation
for strict propriety in London just as she did in her
native Melbourne, where she was never seen in
public, you may say, without her portly mamma
mounting guard like the familiar ben with onechick.
On dit that May (Lady Francis Clinton
Hope) is more or less jealous or “ apprehensive ”
of Ethel, and that sparring-matches frequently
occur—the laurels going to the Victorienne in the
last “ scrum.” It seems that May got herself and
Ethel invited to a supper-party given by two
rowdedowdy lordlings—her idea being to queen it
over the Australian novice; but Miss Ethel
ruined the scheme by demurely replying that she
couldn’t possibly go anywhere, thank you, without
mamma. Evidently the blonde Ethel doesn’t
mean to return to us with her golden hair a-hang-
ing down her back.
The A.J.C. Spring meeting may lay claim to
showing the prettiest Derby dressing seen at
Randwick for some lime. The new fashions are
undeniably flighty—just suitable to give point to
a grand-stand or improve a lawn—and the gather-
ing recalled bright-butterfiy similes in conse-
quence. The hats alone suggested a very uncom-
mon garden where all sorts of flowers (we’re to be
regular Queens of the May so far as flowers go)
were amiably blossoming all at once, utterly re-
gardless of aearans, and though their style and
general aggressiveness (I mean the hats) augurs
badly for the comfort of summer patrons of the
stalls who wish to see the stage now and then, the
new head-gear is moat picturesque in a forty-acre
paddock, at any rate. As usual the acting-guber-
natorial box didn’t show the flower of the track-
ing—it seems to be the proud aim of A.Y.R.’s to
be as dowdy as the genuine article almost invari-
ably is. The handsome Acting-Governoress turned
out in a black silk skirt with a bodice the color
of ripe passion-fruit skin, and two crimson roses
in her bonnet—she also wore her fadeless smile,
which is the only unconvincing spot in her
performance, as real Governors’ wives are
generally champion scowlers. The A.G. turned
up looking faintly sportsmanlike about his pale-
grey-cheeked legs, but nothing short of a horse-
shoe scarfpin ami a straw in his mouth would turn
■his make-believe Ex. into a turfy character.
Mrs. Edward Hutton occupied a corner of the
A.Y.R. box (which was well patronised), wearing
a cottin-ish costume of black and silver, with a
black hat trimmed with pale-pink roses; the
Darley debutante wore cream, with a black gauze
hat trimmed with gay-colored roses ; Miss Kitty
Darley was in pale saffron with figured curry-colored
ribbons ; Miss Decima Moore (who was delighted
with her win on Bob Ray) wore black with mag-
pie trimmings, and an extra big hat (N.B. —Why
do little women always go in so for big toppings ?).
Another visitor, in bright yellow turned up with
green, suggested eggs-and-spinach.
Apropos of the vice-regal box, the back row of
that desirable society resting-place has an entirely
different meaning to what it has in the shows. In
the former the front row is always held by the
older and less alluring ladies who wouldn’t get
further than half-a-yard from the back-cloth if
they ever found themselves in the ballet, while
whatever of youth and beauty surrounds Gov-
mentouse is all jammed up in a heap behind ’em.
Theatrical managers must ache to re-arrange the
spectacle. Lady Darley tied the blue ribbon
round Bob Ray’s hot neck after the Derby—at
least she half did it —but horses are no courtiers,
so secretary Clibborn had to finish the trick.
Every Governoress isn’t a Lady Hopetoun whose
face would radiate delight at the prospect of get-
ting away from “ society ” into the company of a
thorough-bred horse, if only for five minutes.
The prettiest race-dress—a real race-dress only
fit for lawn work—was worn by a round-faced
Sydney society girl with a good figure. It was a
powder-pink silk, exquisitely fitting, and ethereal-
ised with pink chiffon on the bodice and here and
there a cluster of pink artificial roses and leaves ;
a large white hat weighed down with pink roses,
and a white veil and gloves. It was the men’s
favorite gown, too—men always like perishable
goods (except when they have to pay for them) —
and the only thing that rather commonified it was
a damsel in a pink silk blouse who was of the
party. Spring dressing is frivolous to the finger-
tips, and the surprise of the races was
the quantity of green worn in what was
predicted to be a blue look-out sartorially.
A society matron wore a sulphur-green silk
skirt with a pale-green pompadour bodice
flowered in pink, wide white hat trimmed with
pink roses and lilies, A Melbourne society spins-
ter (accompanied by Melbourne’s best-wearing
club man and a chaperone) wore an alpaca coat
and skirt, with white satin collar brocaded in
pink, and a black hat with cornflowers hanging
in it. A Melbourne society girl wore grey with
white muslin Puritan collar, black hat with red
roses; the Yic. Commandant’s wife looked
leopardish in blue with white spots. Miss Clarke
was the swell of the Geebung contingent, in blue
and pink striped brocade on a white ground with
pink and blue feathers in a white tulle hat —for
little Mrs. Rupert Carington was completely
out of it in grey, with pink flowers in a very
expressionless toque.
Australia’s popular sporting widow wore black
and heliotrope to signalise her deliberate progress
out of mourning. The point about Mrs. Eddy’s
toilet was her long cream gloves to the elbow, a
new fashion of wearing your paw-cases also pat-
ronised by Miss Nellie Hill, in a black-and-blue
frock. Mrs. Prendergast headed the subduedly-
elegant brigade in pale-green silk figured in black,
dodgy white satin bodice veiled in black chiffon
and trimmed with black jet and silver. Several
socially-prominent fat ladies wore things they
oughtn’t to have worn—for instance, grey and
black with flashing paste buttons, and obese black
lace parasols.
The Gees Gees were not strongly in evidence at
Randwick. Kaye was there got up like a race-
course tout, and Bradfield sported a large red
flower in his coat; also Flo. Lloyd and Ethel
Sehvyn were heard throwing maledictions at their
bad luck—but none of them were effective 1 And
Sydney’s popular ex-Mayor Manning turned up
at Randwick looking sunburnt after his trip ’Ome
and expressing himself delighted to be back again.
The Gaiety co. (which winds-up its sparkling
Sydney season to-night, Thursday) revived “A
Gaiety Girl ” —the most coherent spectacle of
them all —at Sydney Lyceum on Derby night, in
the presence of a full house, which didn’t seem to
have so much to talk about as usual. There were
two changes in the cast. Louis Bradfield played
the doctor in the manner suggestive of a person
who has some difficulty in forgetting what the
other man had done, and his Dr. Brierley was
another revelation as to how little the author has
to do witli the success of a principal droll. Brad-
field’s make-up was more medical-looking than
Monkhouae’s, but if the vulgar suggestion of
’Arry’s medico was pleasantly missing, the broad
humor of it was lacking also. Grace Palotta
played her pet part of Mina again with all her
former smartness and piquancy, her “philosopher”
song gaining the inevitable double recall. Miss
Palotta always feels more at home in short skirts
than long ones. Like a certain Judge, she believes
in there being much character in the feet.
Miss Moore played Rose Brierley in a brand-new
gown for the first act, and did wonders vocally,
considering the bronchial trouble she was battling
with. Her scene with Cecil Hope (in Bradfieid’s
part as Bobbie) went hilariously, owing to her
real life engagement with the youth being so well
known. The loud chuckling of the gallery over
the real • unreal spooning of these bona-fide
“engageds’’ made the scene on the bathing-
machine steps go very spicily indeed.
By the time these pearls of wisdom get
properly into type, the Gee - Gees will have
shaken the dust of Sydney off their
feet. It must be confessed that no insol-
vencies will strew their wake—there will be no
stone-broke Johnnies left behind like so many
dead marines after a picnic, pitiable testimony to
the 40h.p, fascinations of the tights brigade. The
truth is that Sydney youths have not lost their
heads, nor yet their hearts, over the Gee-Gees.
There has been a dearth of presents—it never got
much beyond the measuring of taper fingers for
diamond rings ; and there have been no expensive
surprises in the hearts of innocent bouquets. One of
the prettiest of Mr. Edwardes’ ladies will go back
with a glittering ring which she bought herself ;
another sports some new jewellery—'given to her
by the good old best boy she hawked along with
her. Other co’s went ’Ome with the news that no
Gaiety Girl could really say she had lived until
she had sounded the gamut of Australian extrava-
gance and star worship—nathless these latest
ladies struck a frost. Many reasons may explain
this. One may be that the local blades hadn’t the
heart to disturb such a complete family party.
The first Spring ball at Govmentouse was a
horrible spectacle for the still -on - the - hooks
brigade, who feel their stock growing more and
more flat when a bevy of damsels flood the market.
Half-a-dozen buds come out (officially speaking)
at the acting-vioe-regal dance, though their names
have flowed from the gushing pens of Society
scribesses many a time before. “ Coming out ”is
a weird ceremony in Australia,, where the nur-
sery takes a lot of finding, as a rule, and where
debutantes have taken the first edge off their
festive appetites with many small-and-earlies and
other unofficially recognised functions. Miss Sylvia
Darley, Miss Mort and her sister, Miss Harrison,
Miss Fairleigh Greene (lovely
and Miss Pearl Airey were the Society maidens
who went to the Society post on this occasion.
Good-looking some of them are, but judges don’t
consider them a very special lot of youngsters in
point of beauty. Somehow the best-looking people
get into Society by marriage. The picturesquely-
innocent effect of the debutantes was rather dis-
counted by there being so much white worn, as if
the idea was to swamp the coming-out show.
Pretty Decima Moore’s farewell “at home” at
her hotel on Monday was largely attended by sas-
siety, which took up Miss Moore during her
Sydney sojourn as violently as though she had
been Great Boulder shares. Miss Darley and Miss
Kitty Darley represented Govmentouse, and were
accompanied by Mrs. Rupert Carington, and the
only professional rays were Mr. and Mrs. Brad-
field. Mr. B. had a circle of society damsels
round him all the afternoon, and as he came in
with a button-hole and went away without it, one
can only surmise whether the aforesaid damsels
tore it from him piecemeal, or whether he gave it
away more sentimentally. Miss Moore enter-
tained her guests most übiquitously, and wore
a silken gown of shot-slate blue and straw-
berry, out of the pocket of which she
would produce—for the admiration of all and
sundry—a little bead-purse which was thrown to
her from the gods, attached to a floral spray. The
gods have thrown Decima many little tokens,
which she regards as her most complimentary
trophies. And certainly there is more compliment
in a rosebud from Olympus than in a bouquet from
lower down, for Olympus is disinterested—lT is
never asked to take afternoon tea with its divinity.
Mr. Cecil Hope, adorned with an Alec. Marsh
boutonniere, took it upon himself to see thatnobody
went away oppressed with sorrow which might
have been alleviated by tea, and so the afternoon
went smoothly on a quiet and highly-fashionable
plan.
Miss Ethel Stephens set the artistic private
views season a-rolling last Tuesday at her well-
lighted studio, 99 Pitt-street, which was combed
up for the occasion, with tea in the middle
distance. Miss Stephens has a dozen pictures,
including figures, flowers, landscapes, to exhibit
at the Society of Artists’ coming show. A study
of a well-known Sydney society girl, in pale
green, is a speaking facial likeness, but the mas-
siveness of the port has been rather kindly pared
down by the artist. It strikes me that two of
Miss Stephens’ best studies are among those not
destined to.be sent in. Beside her own pictures,
Miss Stephens exhibits the cream of her pupils’
work. The star of the students is certainly Miss
Hilda Humphery, aetat 13£, whose efforts show
much sympathetic talent.
About the most aesthetic report of an execution
that has ever appeared in print, so far as I know,
was the description of the hanging of Mrs. Dean,
the Maoriland wholesale baby-farmer and mur-
deress. It is full of picturesque points, and pecu-
liarly interesting in this day of woman-analysis.
Mrs. Dean had a “ dignified carriage and bear-
ing ” on the scaffold, walking with “ head erect
and thin, fine, iron-grey hair nicely brushed,
neatly parted in the middle, and fastened in a
knot behind.” (A woman, it would seem, does
her hair as carefully when she is going to be
hanged as she folds up her clothes before she com-
mits suicide !) As she ascended the steps of the
scaffold she managed (although her arms were
tied) “ to drop one hand sufficiently to lift up the
front of her skirt free from her feet and ascended
the steps more gracefully than I have seen many a
woman ascend the steps of a modern first-class
hotel.” Then she “ withered the hangman with a
look,” gracefully said “Thank you” to the Sheriff
—women are always polite to people in high
authority—and plumped into eternity. In her
cell afterwards was found “ a lock of her hair that
she had left for her husband,” and a bouquet of
violets and snowdrops—which are now, I presume,
somewhat out of fastiion in Maoriland for the rest
of the season. The Dean hanging, by the way,
had an interest for society, since the heroine was
the daughter of “an independent gentleman.”
Before going to her death, Mrs. Dean filled up
her own burial-certificate, as she “knew her hus-
band ” (who was tried and acquitted) " was not
familiar with all the necessary details 1”
Writes my Melb. correspondent
“A gilt-edged wedding took place the other
day, at St. George’s, Malvern, between Miss
Linda Langwell, of Toorak, and Mr. Wood, of
Newcastle, N.S. W. (a cousin of Mr. Essie Jenyns-
Wood). Miss Langwell is one of Melbourne’s
most admired belles; and Miss Wood, sister of
the bridegroom, who was first bridesmaid, is a
perfect model of what brunette-beauty ought to
be. The bridegroom looks rather as if he had
modelled himself on Brownlow, in “Nitouche.”
By way of thrilling novelty in bridal attire, Miss
Langwell wore white satin and Brussells lace, and,
to further cany out this innovation, the bride’s-
maids were attired in white silk, with orange
fixings. The newly-wedded are to reside in New-
castle.
“The lines of demarcation which aforetime
indicated different * sets ’ in Melbourne society
are gradually, by pressure of bad times, fading
out. These ‘ sets ’ used to cause much wonder
in the minds of visitors to Melb., who could never
make out where the ‘ best set ’ left off and the
‘ second set ’ began.
“ A little lady, who for years has been elbowing
her way into the ‘smartest’ set, gave a gorgeous
entertainment, to which she invited folk to whom
she dared not aspire only a year ago. It was
merely drawing a bow at a venture, so to speak,
and no one was more surprised than the hostess
when all the ‘ push ’ turned up. You see, there
is so little on now that one must put in one’s even-
ings somewhere. On this principle ‘ society 1
flocks to suburban mayoral balls, which were con-
sidered quite low only a few years ago. At the
Prahran mayoral dance, on Friday night, the
‘ best ’ people were much in evidence, and the
gowning was as superb as at any of the vice-regal
gatherings. It was an entertainment worthy of
‘the City of Dreadful Knights,’ for there were
five knights present with their appurtenances.
Yours affectionately,
SAPIHO SMITH.
Away in the far west of N.S.W, this is so
far the driest season for six years. And it has
been bitterly cold, too. Hundreds of square miles
of country west of Bourke are as bare as a Sydney
street.
Ain’t it sickenin’ ? S.M. Herald, 16/9/95 :
In connection with the bush fires there was last night a
vivid reflection north-west of Sydney. A correspondent
writes to say that the scrub about Clbntarf was burning
fiercely yesterday, and it was feared that the tree near
which the Duke of Edinburgh was standing on the
memorable day of the notorious Farrell shooting escapade
will be destroyed.
“ Whene’er two lions roar along the shore,
the second lion votes the first a bore.” Bret Hairte
and Mark Twain are the two most prominent
American humorists, and this is what Mark says
of Bret to a Sydney interviewer : “Bret Harte I
consider sham and shoddy, and he has no pathos
of the real true kind.” Now wanted, Harte’s un j
biassed opinion of Twain.
Q. Govt, was so eager to force Agent-
General Garrick’s hand that it gazetted him to
the vacant judgeship before it got his consent.
Since the par. elsewhere was written Garrick has
cabled that ill-health prevents him from accepting.
This disgusting obstinacy throws the Boodle-
wraith plans out of gear, and may force the
Govt, to act straight in spite of itself.
John Roper, the last survivor of Leich-
hardt’s first expedition, died at Merriwa, N.S.W.,
Aug. 16, aged 73. He accompanied the lost ex*
plorer and J. S. Calvert on the famous journey
from Brisbane to Port Essington in ’44, and the
Q. river Roper is named after him. In later life
Mr. Roper was a stock-inspector in N.S.W. public
service.
Possum Dean, the man whom Judge Win-
deyer saw, with the eye of faith, poisoning his
wife on several different occasions, has been sued
for maintenance and ordered to pay 12s. 6d. a
week. Mrs. D. demanded J5l, and her husband
offered 10s., hence the proceedings. He might
have escaped by offering the lady a home (“and
bring your ma along, dear ”), but he didn’t. Even
a more reckless bicyclist than Dean might have
acted similarly under the circumstances.
The noisy meeting at Melb. : Town Hall
was perfectly justified in howling Mayor Snowden
down every time he attempted to occupy the
chair in opposition to its desire. The conveners
of no meeting possess the right to nominate a
chairman ; the choice of that functionary rests
solely with the meeting itself, and the action of
Snowden in trying to force himself upon the
meeting and afterwards turning out the lights
when he failed in that object is only an im-
pudent piece of bluff. The triplet chanted in
the hall that night has evidently come to stay, and
will certainly be heard when Snowden’s time to
face the electors comes round :
Mayor Snowden’s pay:
Man, horse and dray,
Four’n’leven a dayl
The real truth of that “ unexpected ad*
journment” of the Vic, Assembly is not honestly
admitted by either side. The “tariff-reformers ”
saw a chance to rush a vote through in the absence
of several “high-duty” men. Graham Berry,
the old and ardent Protectionist, seeing a legiti-
mate chance to end the sitting, got out of the
chair and defeated the “reformers’ ” tricky tactics.
It might almost be argued that he fled almost in-
voluntarily. Anyway, the Speaker’s hasty depar-
ture couldn’t prevent a fair vote from beyig taken
when the House reassembled. Therefore, why
this howling and gnashing of teeth ? No vote is
better than a false vote, except for the side that
intended to snatch an inglorious victory.
The limp brothers Abrahams, who failed
to move an unsympathetic Law into letting them
off their sentences for the gun-fraud conspiracy,
have suddenly regained their appetites, and are
beginning to bloom again, all the profit to be got
out of fainting in court having been secured.
Louis, one of the precious trio, is said to have con-
fessed to malingering—which is rough on the
array of medical authorities who decided that his
was a genuine and pitiable case of physical weak-
ness. And when medical authorities meet with
a real case of suffering, they frequently turn it
away as bogus—as a man was turned away from
the expensive Sydney Hospital, the other day, to
die a few hours afterwards.
The three Melb. policemen who swore so
profusely in the Synnot divorce-case, to the effect
that the respondent was living in a local house of
ill-fame at a time when she was really in Europe,
have been found guilty of giving “reckless evi-
dence.” Therefore, they have been fined illO
each, with the alternative of two months’ gaol,
and they are now appealing to the Governor not
to ratify the decision. This makes up a very re-
markable situation. An ordinaryoivilian, under the
like circumstances, would have been tried for per-
jury, and would either have been acquitted on
ground that his false swearing was based wholly
on a mistake, or would have got two or three
years’ “ hard.” The Court takes an unsatisfac-
tory middle course in the case of the policemen ;
everyone knew that they had been guilty of
“ reckless evidence,” but what people want to
know is the cause of that lamentable recklessness.
All perjury is reckless evidence ; so is a lot of
evidence which isn’t perjury. Moreover, a sen-
tence on a civilian doesn’t require to be ratified by
the Governor, and why the “cop” should practi-
cally get a second trial which the other man
doesn’t is a mystery.

The Australasian, a Melb. “society”
organ, publishes a page of the beauties of a recent
fancy-dress ball at—ye gods ! —Daylesford, a goat-
infested hamlet somewhere in the interior of Vic-
toria, consisting principally of a State-school, a
lockup and a gorgeously-attired Mayor. The
local editor is reported to be very wroth at the
Melb. paper stealing his best thunder.
• * *
The color question again. Captain Neiten-
stein, The Bulletin hears, declines to keep black
boys on board the N.S.W. reformatory-ship
Sobraon. The Col.-Secretary would like to rap
Neitenstein’s knuckles for raising the question,
but fears to do so. Hence the Inspector-General
of Police has been asked to take charge of black
bays ordered by magistrates to the Sobraon.
• * •
In the dullest and most prosaic lives there
occasionally flashes forth a gleam of devotion.
The other day' it was decided to remove Santo,
a Kanaka leper residing at the Q. lazarette,
to new quarters on account of the improvement
in his health and general condition. Santo, how-
ever, begged to be allowed to remain as attendant
to a leper child, for whom he has conceived a
strong affection. His wish was granted.
mm*
From Cooktown. At the District Court
it was observed that only roosters’ heads would
suffice to swear in the local Chow. On enquiry
being made as to the why and wherefore of this a
Northern Chinaman thus explained matters :
“ Well—spose Chinaman go ’longa lock-up—lock-
up man say: ‘You swear cut cock’s head off—
welly good.’ Chinaman spose say ‘ All li,’ then
ho get too muchee tea, too nmchee blanket—all
welly ni.” The police had been feasting gratis on
poultry at 7s. 6d. a pair (Govt, expenditure),
through the exercise of a little gentle diplomacy.

At a recent Full-
Court sitting, a barri-
ster’s account of the
lo wer-cou rt proceed i ngs
was questioned by one
of the Judges. Then
ensued a typical display
of legal tomfoolery. The
maligned innocent, “in
a voice quivering with
' emotion, ’’disclaimed all
intention to wilfully
deceive, but declared
that, under the circum-
stances, he must with-
draw from the ease.
Finally, he orated :yi
impassioned farewell to
profession, reputation,
and all that made life
worth living, and fell
back into his seat with
bowed head and folded
arms. The offending
dignitary, anxious to
get on with the case,
deprecated this extreme
view of the situation ;
but his victim sat as if
turned to stone. The
second Judge took up
the conciliatory strain,
and the third followed
suit with no belter suc-
cess. At last the Bench
lost patience, and pulled
itself together with a
concerted snort so pro-
longed and ominous
that, the shark sprang
to his feet, forgave his
enemies, and resumed
his speech.

tieth shop in Melbourne

The cocksure Tozer, in Q. Assembly :
“No case of leprosy from contagion has ever been
known.” Putting aside Father Damien’s reported
death from contracted leprosy in Molokai, it is
well known that medical men are divided on the
subject. If there is no fear of contagion, why
isolate lepers? With this comes news that yet
another white Brisbaneite has been found to have
had true leprosy for at least three years, and that
a man from Herberton (N.Q.), who was isolated
in the Moretou Bay lazarette for some time, but
released on the assumption that his symptoms
were not those of true leprosy, is now, after a
year, back again “for good.”
mm*
At Melb. abattoirs during last year 1900
cattle and 1300 sheep were condemned as unfit for
human consumption. At abattoirs not under
inspection, 2500 cattle and 2200 sheep with tuber-
culosis are estimated to have gone into consump-
tion.
* m m
A reputable N. S. W. citizen who visited cer-
tain abattoirs the other day relates his experiences.
“ Tliey have two ways of killing pigs there—one
(Yankee fashion), hitching them up by the hind
legs and cutting their throats; the other, knock-
ing them on the head with an axe and then bleed-
ing them. One pig which had been knocked on the
head resolutely refused to die, and, as the vat
in which he was to be scraped awaited a tenant,
the butcher threw a bucket or two of scalding
water over him, apparently as a punishment for
his obstinacy. Many pigs are rejected as
tuberculous, but I noticed that the healthy pig
is put into the vat which the tuberculous
pig has been washed in. What a mockery it
is—in one quarter all that magnificent hospital-
accommodation for the cure of disease, while in
another quarter the most hopeless and horrible of
diseases are being manufactured wholesale ! ”

Mr. Justice Williams tries bard to live up
to his ideal, the late Vic. Chief Justice Higin-
botham, but falls short for want of material. His
recent Melb. lecture on the “New Religion ” was
a feeble effort after the outburst with which
Higinbotham shook up local orthodoxy. The man
who hopes to dethrone creeds, and starts out gin-
gerly with the assurance of his anxiety not “ to
say one single word which could jar upon the
feelings of anyone or offend in the slightest degree
the prejudices of anyone,” is battering stone walls
with a feather. Creeds and dogmas cannot be
attacked without lacerating the susceptibilities of
the devout, and the reformer who pretends to

Rev. Rickard, of Brighton (Vic.) : “ One
fthe most insane of customs is that of asking a
man to ‘have a glass along with me.’ I can’t
stand a man who is always guzzling; who always
wants you to wet bargains. When a man asks
me to have a drink, I always reply ‘No, we will
have a bun.' ” This puts the great Cold Tea
failure in a nutshell. The yearning to have a
flabby bun, which lies like a wad of blotting-
paper on the “innards,” and the longing to make
other people have a bun, is the curse of teeto-
tal ism.
The Cold Tea apostle is somewhat in the posi-
tion of the aggressive atheist, to whom the Chris-
tian cries : “ Yon would take away my faith and
give me nothing in return.” Similarly, the wine-
hibber to the apostle of ginger-beer : “ You would
take away my whisky and give me what?” What,
indeed !

The Bulletin doesn't mind Chief Justice
Darley having a State railway-carriage on the
cheap all to himself. It gives him a fair show to
gloat over The Bulletin, which he could hardly
do openly. To be seen publicly in company with
so scurrilous and disgraceful a paper would just
about burst up the Supreme Court.
A correspon-
dent : The other day
at Murwillnmbah,
N.S.W., a mob of
bullocks was being
driven through a
deep cutting. One
of the beasts, mad-
dened by over-heat-
ing and thirst,
charged a lady who
was coming towards
the mob. The lady
was knocked into a
narrow gully, and,
seeing her danger,
one of the drovers
named Bignell
spurred his horse
into a gallop and
crashed broadside
into the infuriated
beast, knocking it
down. Jumping
from his horse lie
grasped the bul-
lock’s horns, and,
placing his weight
on its neck, drew
his knife and cut
its throat. He un-
doubtedly saved the
lady’s life ; and es-
caped himself with
a few bruises.

Following the example of Justice Williams,
Justice Hodges delivered a Pleasant Sunday
Afternoon address at a Melb. Wesleyan
chosing naturally for his subject his blste noir,
“ The Evils of Drink.” As a serious, or even in-
structive, lecturer, he ran a bad second to bis
gifted confrere, but without doubt he contributed
unintentionally several humorous suggestions. For
instance :
He counselled all present never to take liquor unless
they felt that they absolutely needed it, and, instead of
entertaining their friends by offering them drink, he
recommended them to discuss questions of constitutional
h.story and of social improvement with those whom they
met, so that each would be better instead of worse after
the meeting.

PRAYERS FOR RAIN.
The cattle perish ’neath the trees,
The dead sheep rot upon the sod ,
The good man, posing on his knees,
Upholds an argument with God ;
And when the flood leaps o’er the ford
He feels he has convinced the Lord !

A SCIENTIFIC SING-SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We’re all automata, every one,
We’re conscious of nothing until it is done ;
The hue of the rose, be it ever so red,
Is nought but the whirl of a wheel in the head.
We’re self-adjusting mechanical toys
That weep mechanical pearls,
We’re only strong mechanical boys
And sweet mechanical girls.
Chorus:
We’re only mechanical, semi-Satanioal,
Clanical girls and boys,
Governed despotical, slightly erotica],
Penny-in-slotieal toys.
Our life is merely the plasrnic way,
The tune that the barrel must always play.
But it makes you feel uncommonly small
To think you’re mechanical, after all.
We’re a mountain of cells from the hair to the toe,
And we mingle, divide and swell,
For we’re only mechanical, don’t you know,
A rank mechanical sell.
Chorus :
Yes, we’re only mechanical, built-to-a-planioal,
Semi-Satanical toys ;
Our being is nought but mechanical thought,
Mechanical pains and joys.

SAMUEL CLIALL WHITE.
Summer months are now approaching—
Aye, the drought's already come—
Isnl you must not dream of broaching
Ca'ak&f beer or bottled rum.
Whilst the pretty barmaid’s fussing
Round with squashes ribbed with “sticks”
You'll refresh yourself discussing
Torqueinada’s style of trussing
Old-time Spanish heretics—
Minus customary “hies.”
Thirsty souls, completely "stony,”
In a scorching atmosphere,
Looking for a trusted crony
Good for one long, lingering beer,
Wish him not cast in perdition
If he seeks to ease your fate
Wiili a two-hour disquisition
On the Heaven-appointed mission
Of King Henry, number VllL—
'Specially re the marriage slate.
When the coming man is feeling
Lined with fresh-made Brunswick bricks,
H-'ll join brothers in revealing
All his inmost politics ;
Or into a corner chapel
Ask his friend to take a straight
Sermon on the primal apple,
Or hear parson Flapjack grapple
Themes that cheer their fallen state
Yet do not inebriate.

engaged in the abortion business don’t exactly
deserve their reputation, inasmuch as, aware that
they are suspected and watched by the police,
they treat every would-be patient as a trap. They
simply charge the woman two or three or four

* • *
The Bulletin has often wondered how on
earth the poor artist managed to pay the numerous
and stylish canvassers engaged in selling low-
priced art-union tickets—ostensibly for his (the
artist’s) benefit. The secret came out in Sydney
Bankruptcy Court last week, when it transpired
that an uncertificated bankrupt had been em-
powered to conduct a raffle of pictures from
the brush of a well-known Sydney painter.
The art-union was arranged before the pictures
were painted, the artist was paid the absurdly
small sum of £U for 17 paintings, and for the
purposes of the raffle the works were valued at
.£lB9, and tickets to this amount disposed cf.
• * #
The latest Melb. tragedy, where a man
shot his wife, and, after haying narrowly escaped
murdering his son, killed himself, is put down to
drink. But the prime cause, as in 75 out of 80
such oases, seems to have been want of work.
• • •
The “age of consent” in California is 10
years. In most American States it is 14 ; but in
some 16. In the State of New York, however,
mainly by the exertions of Women’s Reform
Associations, it is 18, and next session an attempt
will be made to increase it to 21. As the vast
majority of women make their matches between
16 and 21, strong opposition from the female
section of the community may be expected ; but
as minors have no votes the chances are that the
extended age will become law. Which should
give Australian girls something to think of.
Melb. Women’s Hospital, in deciding
between two applications for the position of assis-
tant-resident surgeon of the midwifery depart-
ment, has just selected that of a lady —Miss Lilian
Alexander, M.B.
# • •
At Melb. City Court, in a case of bigamy,
it transpired that the parties were married at a
well-advertised local matrimonial office by a
Presbyterian minister. Well may the Holy
Romans boast about their pastors having no con-
nection with hole-and-corner celebrations of the
agency description. Considering the pretentious
piety of Vie. Presbyterian sky-pilotry, that Scotch
parson’s struggle for the saxpence of the sinful
does seem infra dig.
• • •
Visitors to some matrimonial bureaux
must feel nervous when conversing with their
prospective partners in the parlor. How
many flabby ears may not be glued to listening-
tubes alongside the speakers, and what number of
spy-holes might not exist around the auburn hair
of Angelina? And just as the eager lover was
whispering “Darling, we will go straight away to
the registry-office and do these fools out their
commission ! ’’ the chances are that sounds of
derisive laughter would peal forth as a snap-shot
camera fixed the blissful couple, while the boss
made out his account for “ engagement effected,’’
and the office detective laced up his bluchers to
shadow them en route to the temple of Hymen.
• • •
Dr. Perks, medical superintendent of
Adelaide Hospital, on whose recommendation
Miss Gordon was appointed night superinten-
dent over the heads of several senior nurses, has
resigned. The board say because of Government
interference. Others affirm that he is to receive a
much higher salary as medical attendant to
millionaire and octogenarian Thomas Elder. Miss
Gordon’s resignation is said to have been written
out more than once, and it will probably be for-
warded in real earnest before long.
• # #
The Bulletin strongly advises “Leon-
ardo,” the Ahgus epigrammatist, to stop epigram-
ming. It is not because Oscar Wilde is in gaol
that anybody should dilute him with Yarra-water.
Also, “In a few years science will discover the
microbe of matrimony and destroy it altogether,”
is a paraphrase of Pinero’s “We must dis-
cover the bacillus of marriage and pluck it out.’’
Likewise, “He twined vine-leaves in his hair,”
belongs to Ibsen.”

Recently three Adelaide solicitors were
ordered terms of imprisonment for contempt of
Court, in re non-compliance with orders made
under unsatisfied judgment summonses.
The proposal of the N.S.W. railway de-
partment to construct electric trams along Pitt
and George streets, Sydney, opens up a blessed
prospect of peace and repose and holy calm
generally. The electric train, if constructed .on
anything like reasonable principles, is almost
silent, and if it is run on anything like business
principles it should wipe out most of the omni-
buses, which are anything but silent. Their
wheels bounce horribly over the wood blocks. The
horses’ hoofs clatter dismally as the raw-boned
team drags its load behind it. The drivers scream
at each other in exciting moments, and howl at the
public to incite them to come into the awful
vehicle. And many ’buses now have a boy hang-
ing on behind whose sole business apparently is to
yell in an unspeakable fashion, with a shriek like
that of a lost soul. When two or three of these
vehicles get together, and all the boys set them-
selves to out-yell the rest, the result is beyond

•* * *
The horrible part of attending a Melb.
funeral just now is the drummer-like way the
undertaker presses his black-edged advertising-
cards upon you. In a certain suburb there are rival
undertakers opposite each other in one street. Ihe
other day a local resident fell down in a fit in the
road. He was taken home, and while being un-
dressed the cards of the rival ghouls fell, from his
clothes. They had been among the first to rush
to his assistance.
# * *
Melbourne’s latest ghoul is the headstone-
fiend. Armed with a big album he invades your
house and insists on showing yon the latest design
in gravestones ; time-payment taken. If you tell
him you have no deaths in the family he will
answer that it doesn’t matter —it’s always best to
be prepared.
* * *
It was at first thought advisable to ask
Prof. Marshall Hall to conduct the musical part
of the show at the coming to Melbourne of Gov.
Brassey, but then it was remembered that the
wild young man is a Socialist, an Agnostic, a
Wagnerite, and a general scoffer at all things
flunkeyish. So the musical purveying was en-
trusted to the Liedertafel, an eminently respect-
able and harmless body of people, whose chests
are warranted to swell loyally to the strains of
“God Save the Queen.”

Trilbyisms. A well-known George-street,
Sydney, bootmaker states that during the past
few weeks orders for square-toed boots have been
unprecedented. Music-sellers also aver that every
second customer asks for “Ben Bolt; ” and three
houses just erected in a prominent position at
Paddington have been named “Trilby,” “ Litre-
bili,” and “ Svengali.”

oath, lam ; he saved my life. Put me
down for two bob.”
• * *
Free education doesn’t make much differ-
ence in the great fact that mankind are mostly
fools. A week or two ago some Sydney humorist,
commonly believed to be a butcher in an eastern
suburb, alleged that Astronomer Russell had fore-
told a tidal wave 90ft. high, which was presently
to strike N.S.W. with an awful whack and do
great damage. The story spread in all directions
and about half the population believed' it, more.or
less, and hardly anybody seemed to notice that a
wave 90ft. high was rather above the average, or
to know chat disturbances of this kind were not
foretellable. For several days _ the Govt.
Astronomer was swamped by terrified persons
who wanted information about a safe place to fly
to and other particulars. Then when the date
went by and no disturbance came, the general
decision was that the Government star-gazer
wasn’t up to his work, and his name is solemnly
handed down to posterity as that of the man who
foretold a tidal wave about 900 ft. high which
didn’t come off.
A correspondent: “ Sydney Technical Col-
lege the other day sent to a station near Nyngan,
N.S.W., half-a-dozen of its students to roll the
wool and pick the pieces in the shearing-shed. Do
these youths get wages, or do they give their ser-
vices in return for ‘ experience ? ’ Is the Sydney
Technical College heavily subsidised by the State
to supply cheap labor to the squatters and do
rouseabouts out of a job? ”

Melb. Turn-Yerein held a “comers’
night in honor of violinist Kruse. The guest
stayed till late in the evening, his hosts till late in
the morning. To the club at 4 a.m. came an
anxious frau, enquiring for the hat, one sock, and
one boot of her lord, who had arrived home in a
glorious, lashings-of-lager-beer frame of mind,
but incomplete in his attire.

On the notice-board at a Melb. church <
ST. LEGER CHURCH.
Subject of Discourse, next Sunday:—
“ What we may expect in Hell.”
Free Seats and No Collection.
There’s nothing mean about Hell.
It is proposed to make, by prison-labor, a
carriage-drive to The Springs, half-way to the
summit of Mt. Wellington (Tas.). The inmates
of Slowbart gaol are delighted with the scheme —
which they regard as a sort of brilliant camping-
out holiday planned for their especial benefit.
The Australia Hotel, Sydney, is a palace
which cost .£300,000, and whose sumptuousness
causes the average man to drop his jaw with
amazement. It is conspicuous upon a con-
spicuous site, and its highest towers look
down upon Government House, the Harbor
of Delectation, the sunlit-splendor of the
Pacific, the city of Sydney itself, and all the land-
scape beauties which ultimately lose themselves
in the Blue Mountains and Heaven’s azure hue.
By moonlight the watch-towers can see as much
of what is going on in the city as the moon
herself, and while the thrilling associations
of the seductive moonlight agitate the mortal
breast it is apt to state that The Australia
has 100 double-rooms capable of holding 100
honeymoon couples. Another glorious feature is
that the magnificent vestibule is adjacent to the
queen of bars, and, therefore, the M.P. who
waits for a man in the vestibule can be sure of
getting a tonic to fortify himself against the
sobering debates of the ’Ouse. Perhaps it is no
argument in the Australia’s favor that it is so
close to the theatres, the .Library, the Post Office,
and the banks. All the trams, too, are so close
that it seems the easiest thing in the world to step
from the Hotel Australia on to the tram itself —
up to date, however, people have preferred
coming down in the lift, whose splendor caused
one visitor to begin undressing in it after paying
for the best of the 320 rooms in the palace.
Thought reverts lovingly to the vestibule, not
because of its proximity to the bar, but because
it was here that the Divine Sarah Oleopatrioally
embraced a feminine friend, whom she had not
seen for years. Hot and cold water are in every
room, and the man who cannot quench his morn-
ing’s thirst with these has only to press the button
of the electric-bell in order to get a cwt. block of
ice, a hogshead of iced-water, a teaspoonful of
whisky, and a 100 o.p. iced-soda. The
cuisine makes life one beautiful idyll, and
the manager’s tact can be estimated from his
having captured the livers of Max O’Rell, Lord
Hopetoun, Parson Talmage, Parson Haweis, Prize-
fighter S'ullivan, Judge A., Judge 8., Judge C.,
and Judge D. One and all of these celebrities
have given the manager testimonials, which prove
that the Australian cuisine and attention fairly
mashed them. To sum up, the Australia is better
located for business or pleasure than any other
hotel in the Southern Hemisphere, and every
visitor tostimonialises it in the spirit, if not in the
words of Sarah Bernhardt: “May each traveller
find the same welcome and feel the same pleasure
in entering this hotel.” *
Looking backwards is not always a plea-
sure, any more than looking forward, although at
times, with some of us, both are a delight. In
retrospecting the advances of the last decade we
must notice the development of smoking, which
has now become with some men almost a science.
From the good old clay and strong-flavored Irish
twist of 10 years ago, we have advanced stride by
stride until a stage has been reached which seems
the zenith of epicurean desires. In Frossard’s
Cavour Cigars, which are the latest innovation,
we have a delicious, fragrant, mild, and aromatic
smoke, which have the nicotine extracted “in
vacuo ” by a process known only to the manu-
facturers ; the leaf is by this means freed
from every injurious element, and the smoker
enjoys all the fragrance of the tobacco without
any of the effects of nicotine. Looking forward
to the future, onegcanuot see how this invention
can be improved upon both scientifically and
economically, for these cigars are now retailed
all over Australasia in neat packages of 8, and are
the best possible value procurable. If any reader
of this paragraph has not yet enjoyed them, he
should do so at once. Heavy smokers should try
them. Young ladies should request their lovers
to indulge in nothing else. Mothers should advise
their sons to smoke only Frossard’s Cavour Cigars,
and we are sure that they will soon thank us for
the good advice.*
SHODDY.
Some side-lights are thrown on the wool-trade in
an interesting article which appeared in the
Leader, written by Mr. Synnot, of The National
Wool Co., Melbourne. Commenting on the causes
of the low prices for wool, the point taken is that
the frequently-recurring market collapses are not
caused by over-production, but are the outcome of
the introduction of other subsidiary matter such
us cotton and shoddy. Assuming that the practice
of mixing shoddy with our pure wool can be
limited, the prospects for the woolgrower are
encouraging, and it is to be hoped that all the
colonies will pull together and insist that importa-
tions of woollen goods, tweeds, blankets, etc., be
hall-marked, and the honest manufacture certi-
fied to by responsible trade boards.*

The Wild-Cat Column .
The recent Vic. Supreme Court decision re the
range of bills-of-sale has “ consternated ’’ Southern brew-
ing circles. Hitherto, a bill-of-sale was considered to
cover for 12 months (when It had to be renewed) the stock
of the hotel under lien. It has now been ruled that it
only applies to the stock particularised in '‘the notice of
intention to file;” and any interfering with the stock, prior
to the passing of the b.s., in all probability invalidates
the latter. If the Pull Court maintains this view, the
brewer and the wine-and-spirit merchant will have no hold
worth a cent over a grantor of a b.s., who, in most cases,
is worth little after paying his ingoing; the grantors
viewing subsequently-acquired stock as their property,
whilst the b.s. remains unpaid. Prom the grantor’s point
of view the new ruling is satisfactory, as it will give many
a miserable who has been lured into investing his all in
some far-over-valued pub., a chance to save something
from the wreck when payment-on-demand is enforced.
• • • *
When next ?the N.S.W. Local Government
Bill turns up (say 25 years hence), It should provide that
all municipal accounts shall be overhauled by a State
auditor, and that wherever a municipality has an over-
draft at its banker’s, or its accounts show a deficiency for
the year, the auditor should have power to strike a special
rate, up to a certain fixed limit, the proceeds of which
must go to meet the shortage. Most of the embarrassed
local bodies are embarrassed simply because they won’t
tax themselves sufficiently to cover ordinary expenses.
Some of the Vio. municipalities that are now praying for
more State aid to save them from bankruptcy have lately
ndueed their taxation from Is. to 9d. in the £, and are
suspected of making such absurdly low valuations that
the alleged 9d. amounts in reality to barely 6d.
• • • •
The creditors of the Australian Mortgage and
Deposit Bank have finally agreed to reconstruct on the
following basis : 10 per cent, of the deposits to be repaid
next January ; 10 per cent, without interest in Jan.,
1899; 10 per cent, without interest in Jan., 1901; the
other 70 per cent., with interest at the rate of 3J per
sent, annually, to be paid in 1911 if possible, but the bank
to have the right of renewing two-thirds of the amount
until 1916. Which is about the slowest reconstruction on
record.
• • • •
The Bulletin knows of one landholder who
will get a very bad shock when the land-tax arrives.
About 20 years ago this person let a piece of ground, in
a then remote suburb, on a very long lease—so long that
Ins grandson may possibly see'the end of it if he is of a
robust constitution, bnt not otherwise. The lessee said
lie meant to grow turnips on the property, but no veget-
ables were specified in the lease, though the ground
was let at market-gardening rates. That lessee promptly
bni't a terrace—one of the longest terraces in Sydney—on
the property, and a boom set in, and a big suburb grew
up, and land went up like a balloon thereabouts. The
value has risen so much that all the rent which the owner
receives won’t cover the land-tax, and, if values keep in-
ceasing, as they probably will, the property will be a
bigger and bigger loss for 40 years or so. It is’ impossible
to sell a property and almost impossible to give it
away with such an incubus on it, unless the owner gives
it to the church, which doesn’t pay land-tax. Obviously,
in such cases - and there are probably a good many of the
kind in a more or less aggravated shape—the leaseholder
should be got at by the State, but there is no provision in
the Land-Tax Bill for getting at him in any form.
• • • *
The Land and Income-Tax Bill got through all
its crucial stages, in N.S.W, Assembly, without a single
question being asked about those glaring and sinful dis-
crepancies—the exemption of church lands from the land-
tax, and the exemption from income-tax of the foreign
holder of N.S.W. debentures while the local holder has to
pay up. Also, no one called attention to the fact that
the foreign mortgagee is exempted from absentee-tax,
though the foreign landholder isn’t; that, though the
absentee private landholder is specially taxed, in a very
small way, it is true, the absentee landholding co., which
is twenty> times more important, apparently is not.
Also, though the absentee who derives his income from
land is specially taxed, the one who derives his income
from other sources isn’t—there is no absentee income-
tax in the hill at all.
• * • •
The N.S.W. railways and tramways paid, for
the year ended 30th June, ’95. £3 I2s. per cent, on the
cost of construction, against £3 9s. 2d. for the previous
12 months, and, but for the exorbitant rate at which
money was borrowed for construction purposes, they
would now be returning a handsome profit. As it is, the
deficit is very small, and is being steadily reduced.
The return of £3 12s. per cent, is the best that has been
realised since 1884. The total receipts (£3,160,520) are
£68.785 more than last year, while the working expenses
( £1.798,682) are £22,543 less, so that the net revenue has
increased by £91,328. On the railways, working expenses
now amount to rather under 54i per cent, of the entire
takings, the lowest rate since *lBBl. These results have
been achieved despite the fact that there has lately been
bung round the neck of the Department the Lismore-
Tweed railway, which shows a loss of £26,827, and the
Sydenham-Belmpre line representing a ins* of £8277, and
that a further loss (amount unstated) has been incurred
by the Ocean-street cable tramway, and by a number of
reductions in freights and fares. Altogether, the savings
madeduring the last seven years under the Eddv regime
may he roughly estimated at £2,790,000—in other words,
the railway system has paid that amount more to the
Treasury than it would have done if things had continued
on the 1888 basis. The man who has produced these re-
sults is not dear at £3OOO a year—nor at £6OOO.

The low salaries paid to their'valuators and
inspectors by certain insurance co’s are proving very
expensive economies. It, has leaked out that risks have
been overvalued considerably, and insurances recom-
mended in cases of the worst possible marks.

The Victorian deficit for the past year proves
to be only £42,000 instead of £114.000. as previously
announced. It is the custom in Victoria to make an
allowance, at the close of the financial year, for unpaid
liabilities, and now that all (he accounts have come in.
Premier Turner finds that he allowed £72,000 more (hau
was necessary. They don’t do tilings in this fashion in
the other two eastern provinces. Queensland leaves out-
standing liabilities to lie paid next year. N.S. Wales now
travels on a “ cash basis,” so that everything which isn’t
paid during the year goes into next account, even many
of the civil service salaries for June being shunted into
next year on the ground that they are not payable till Ist
July.

• * * »
Most of the new local depositors of the City of
Melbourne Bank got the tip in time and came in out of
the drought. There was nothing that could he called a
run—it was more like a crawl—hut, in an unobtrusive
fashion, the hulk of the public managed to be in time.
The management probably is not sorry. A feeling of
bitter aggravation at the Scotch depositor is the prevail-
ing sentiment,, and the less there is to divide when he
comes down in his rage and hie kilt the better a good
many people will like it.
* • • *
The United Steamship Co. (Tasmania) is think-
ing of going out of the business. This luckless concern
has paid just two riivs. in 30 years, and its history shows
that energy and pluck and a profound faith in the good
time coming are not what they were cracked up to be in
the unreliable copy-book that we used to get whacked
for spilling the ink-hottle over in the happy days of child-
hood.

for the last half-year of £7382, and the loss was £7Ol.
The directorate announces once more that nothing hut a
liquor-license will meet the case. The assets are now
nearly £17,000 less than the liabilities, and the freehold
property is taken at its book-value (£149,000) at that. Its
real value just now could hardly amount to £lOO,OOO.
* * • *
_ Melbourne Mutual Store shows a profit of
£2070 for the half-year, after making allowances for
doubtful debts, &c., and it pays a div. of Is. 6d. a share,
equal to £3 6s. Bd. per cent, per annum. Not much, cer-
tainly, but decidedly better than its recent experiences.
* • • •
Existing mortgages in Maoriland total
£30,571,000. Of this amount Canterbury—population
mostly Eaglish—owes nearly £9,000,000, while the Scotch
province of Otago, with a larger area, owes less than
£3,000,000.
* * » *
Sydney D.T., referring to the Illawarra smelt-
ing scheme, says : “ It is gratifying to know that the
sulphide problem has been solved by a citizen of
Sydney”—meaning Dr. Storer. At the same time it is
painful to know that any newspaper can say the sulphide
problem is solved many months before any possible
attempt can he made to prove it. The problem can
never be held solved in the laboratory or with any model
plant, and judgment on the scheme must he suspended
until details arrive. The gush about building a new city
and ocean-steamer jetties, making a great harbor, buying
mines notable for the absence of sulphide ore, and
spending two millions a year in wages, sounds very well,
but—the Illawarra crowd are good advertisers.
* * ♦ *
There's life in the Cremorne coal boomers. They
have let shaft-contracts to the extent of £60,000, and
involved themselves in other big financial obligations. In
Australia, no time is lost in spending J. Bull’s money.
In Oippsland, where the coal-speculators have to spend
at least some of their own, and the coal, though shallow,
is subject to numerous faults, they keep a number of
drills going to locate the seams beyond doubt ere they start
shafts or other workings. In Sydney Harbor, the Cre-
morne people (with quite as much of the N.S.W. Govt,
money as their own) put down two deep bores, one of
which struck burnt—and, therefore valueless —coal ; the
■other good coal. Then they went to London right away.
Sensible boomers! To put down more bores and chance
finding more burnt coal wight be their funeral; now it
would be Mr. Bull’s. But what a downy duck Bull must
be not to find out where “he are ” with a lot more bores
ere spending a quarter-of-a-million in shafts, etc. ! Let us
hope, however, it will turn out all right. There is every
reason to believe in the existence of good seams—hut, a
lot of Scotchmen working the show “on their own”
■would surely have bored a lot more ere sinking.
• • • •
C. B. Fisher, the insolvent Melbourne squatter,
reports that since 1882 he paid the banks £591,000 in dis-
counts and interest on overdrafts, and owed them
£590,000 for interest alone at the time of his insolvency.
If this amount has been reckoned among the assets, it
means another heavy knock to some badly-bruised insti-
tutions.
• • • •
Coal is again said to have been found in the
Cooktown district. What’s it’s heating power? Good
coal would be a godsend to the Northern goldfields. At
present coal costs upwards of £2 per ton on the Towers,
and there is not a tree above four feet high to be found
within 10 miles of the town, the rest having gone to
the furnaces. No wonder the local rainfall is scanty.

George Meudell, of Vic., has established him-
self in London as a director of the “ Investment Agency
of Australasia, Lt.,’’ the chief object of which is to act
for clients in buying and selling Australian stocks and
shares of every description either in London or Australia.

A big Australian advocate lately arranged
with his creditors for the acceptance of 12s. 6d. in the £.

They bottomed on wash at about 30ft. in a
South Melh. sewer shaft, the other day. The stuff pros-
pected a few colors to the dish. The general impression
is that had the deposit beer any good Matt. Larkin
would not have overlooked it.
• * » •
The practice of brokers transferring shares in
rocky concerns to their office-boys was shown up in the
insolvency case of Henry Darling, an ex-office-boy, in
Melbourne, the other day. Henry was encumbered with
an accumulation of liabilities amounting to about £2600,
all calls due upon shares in financial Institutions (mostly
bung) which shares had been fastened upon him in the
course of business when he was a defenceless junior clerk
in the office of a city broker. Transference is no robbery.
• • • •
Recent experiences of cattle-shipment prove
that cattle for export must he young and not heavy
beasts. Overcrowding in the pens on hoard results in
overheating and death. Green-hide should be used for
halters, and not ripe, which shrinks when wet with rain
or spray and causes discomfort. Unless a larger per-
centage of stock than that, landed from the Southern
■Cross last week reaches Europe in good condition,
exporters will only burn their fingers.

West. For the same period in ’94 the excess was only
£6357.

During Ang. there was a decrease of .£36,261 in
the deposits in S.A. Savings Bank, but an increase of 212
in the number of depositors. The idea of the Treasurer
is that some “ big ” men have been drawing out sums'of
money (interest is only given on £250) with the object of
making it appear that the State Advances Bill is frighten-
ing the customers of the bank.

• • • *
The South Australian Co. —that famous associa-
tion of British philanthropists which, many years ago,
obtained an enormous land-grant in S.A. on its solemn
assurance that it would not use the properly for money-
making purposes—is paying the respectable div. of 9 per
cent. Last year it paid 11 per cent., and for the seven
years 1887 93 it paid 13 per cent, annually. The only
good feature in the case is the steady reduction in the
dividend, and it is to he hoped that the graduated land-
tax will keep it down for the future.
* • • •
One of the most successful company-mongers in
London is Edmund Mitchell, erst of the Pastoralists’
Review. His hunting-ground is Glasgie, and, being a
Scotchman himself, he appears to have got Sandy fairly
by the wool.

Good pearling season at Cossack (W. A.). Several
good pearls were secured, one worth £6OO, and others
ranging from £2OO to £4OO. Cheap labour ; divers mostly
natives, who dive without a dress to a depth of eight
fathoms.

Prell and Co., of Melbourne, have bonded the
West Volunteer and several adjacent mines at Lefroy
(Tas.) for the London market. Now is the time to give J.
Bull all the Australian mines he wants.
• * * *
At Lefroy (Tas.), the reefs are often pheno-
menally rich near the surface, hut the payable gold,
apparently, doesn’t live to a depth. Years ago several
co’s shut down after paying big divs. Discoveries of new
reefs have brought the field to the front, and two co’s
have received funds for proving their lodes at a depth.
The Pinafore, whilst crosscutting at 800 ft., is still sinking;
and the Volunteer is also going down. Goldatadjpth
would revolutionise things at Lefroy, as there are several
known auriferous lodes on the field. One of these carries
a few dwts. at 800 ft.
* * • •
A syndicate of Sydney men, after much deli-
beration and inspection, has decided to buy the old Day-
spring mine at Parkes. Queer that this mine remained so
long unworked. It has a big ore-body, tens of thousands
of tons of payable ore have been crushed from it, and
there are two other reefs which have never been opened.
• • • •
The proposal of theN.S.W. Mines Minister to
encourage prospecting by erecting batteries is not too bad,
providing that the sites are left to the judgment of
miners, not to geologists, that they are so designed as to
be easily shifted when they have served their purpose or
the fields have failed, as many will, and that they are run
by hard-headed, horny-handed battery men—free from
the interference of pink-handed, soft-headed Govt,
officials.

One prominent Northern mining man, who
“has been there before,” has come to grief as the result
of “specking” three mines which, as soon as he had
largely oversold, simultaneously went up by leaps and
bounds.
* ♦ » *
The resurrection of Cobar as a mining-camp is
due to the five N.S. W. men who last year took the big
copper-mine t here on tribute ; and of these the hero is
Longworth. Throwing aside the old system of smelting
with reverberatory furnaces, they adopted water-jackets,,
and are now running two. Their output is about 4000
tons a month, and the mining, heap roasting, smelting,
refining and wood-carting give steady work to hundreds
of men, and require more than half the total freight of
the Cobar railway. With copper lower than the co. could
ever work the mine to pay, they pay the co. a royalty
and themselves good divs. They have also managed to
secure half the shares in the old co. The gold contents
of the ore average over £2O for each ton of refined
copper. The co. was blissfully ignorant of its existence!
It turned nut copper to the value of a million, paid
£154,000in divs., and threw—or rathergave—away the gold.
The old directors will now' know why Oohar copper was so
appreciated in Swansea. The divs. about equal the gold
they gave away.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
Probably the most distressed mining man in
N.S. W. to-day is Supt. Stewart, of B.H. Proprietary. A
month ago he said the fire was out; half-a-dozen times
since he has said the same thing. Every few days a shaft
is going to be opened “next week.” Still the gas con-
tinues to be poured into the mine ; and every time they
disturb the surface over the affected part smoke escapes.
By those who know how difficult a thing it is to
set a big log, even of light wood, blazing where the
draught is poor, it ought to be remembered that many of
the timbers in the affected part of the B.H. Proprietary
have been much crushed and splintered by creeps.
Following on the fire underground at the B.H.
Proprietary carae the burning of the sorting and dressing
floors and bins at the neighboring Block 10. Nor is that
the only outbreak of fire at the Hill. The B.H. Proprie-
tary concentrating plant, the B.H. Junction engine-house,
the South’s buildings—all, and perhapi others, have been
scorched. At Broken Hill they are uneasy about it.
Supt. Stewart, of the B.H. Proprietary, appa-
rently isn’t bound hand and foot to the Tarrawingee Flux
Co. Tenders are called at the Hill for raising 9000 cubic
yards of limestone flux from leases which the 00. has
acquired in the district.
The old Umberumberka mine, one of the oldest
company-properties on the Barrier, sold to the Silverton
Tram. Co. recently, is to be worked on tribute.
The B.H. North is sending 500 tons of sul-
phides to the B.H. South concentrators, in order that it
may be better ascertained what particular class of mill
and treatment is most suitable. When it is ascertained,
a mill will be erected on the mine.
B.H. Junction North sulphides to date are
largely mixed with sandstone.
For the weekended Sept. 7 the mines at Broken
Hill, other than the B.H. Proprietary, concentrated or
shipped 6857 tons, all but 300 tons of which were sul-
phides. The Central dealt with 1634 tons ; Block 14 with
1200 tons (besides ore bought); the South with 1178 tons.
The B.H. Proprietary during the same week smelted or
amalgamated 8197 tons. Total quantity of ore dealt with
during the week, 14,264 tons.
« • * *
Dear Bulletin, —A glimpse of Wyalong—from
the inside. Miners’ rights everywhere here, and every-
thing done by contract at starvation prices. Shaft-sink-
ing to 100 ft. Is. 6d. and 2s. per foot; driving levels and
sloping quartz at prices in proportion. Tributes are (aken
at 50, 55, and, in some cases, 75 per cent, above loz. per
ton—tributors to find timber, tools, &c. As for wages,
there are men working for from 15s. up to £2 10s. per
week ; so the bona-fide miner, as represented here, isn’t a
big improvement on the syndicator. There are at present
about 12 claims paying ; but ns they are mostly working
on short shoots of gold, don’t he surprised to see them
dwindle down to half in six months. Several are under
the loz. now.—Miner.
♦ * • »
A correspondent: There was interred at Wya-
long (N.S.W.) cemetery, the other day, Lawrence Paul
Conway, one-time part proprietor of the Cnrrajong and
Day Spring Beefs, Parkes, and a pioneer of the famous
Lucknow mines. In his day he was known ns a cricketer,
and was presented by the first English Eleven which
visited Australia with a bat for the highest score obtained
against them at Bathurst. He was a good fellow, and
was always willing to lend a hand as an amateur for any
charitable purpose. He came to Wyalong at the breaking-
out of the field, hut was not among the lucky ones. He
was 60 when he died.

The Auckland, M.L., mining-boom, writes The
Bulletin s local correspondent, lias come to stay.
During Aug., 18,000 acres were pegged out in the Thames
and Ohinemuri districts; also large areas at Kunolonn
and Coromandel. Every day two or three claims are
floated (usually as no liability co’s), and nromot rs’
shares are invariably over-applied for. A good feature is
that vendors get little cash, the money subscribed going
chiefly to development. The whole Thames Coromandel
p miimula, which contains large quantities of stone that
should pay with cyanide, will now be thoroughly pros-
pected.
The yield of bullion for July was valued at
£35,000. Figures for August not yet complete, but
should he nhout the same, principal yields being:
Waihi, £9519; Haurnki, £8900; Crown, £2200; Mav
Queen, £2100; Woodstock, £883; Wailikauri, £728; Try
Fluke, £765. .
Kapai-Vermont is looking well and is getting
good stone in large quantities. The yield for this month
(Sept.) should be worth close on £BOOO. The co. has a big
reef, which will keep it going for years.
The Grown, at Karangahake, is a steady bullion -
producer, and should shortly pay divs. The 00. has
£25,000 In hand for development of property and erection
of more machinery ; there is enough payable dirt in sight
to keep battery going for 18 months. As the surplus on
a month’s work is about £l2OO, the 00. is getting into a
snug position. The shares (80,000, 17s. paid), held chiefly
in Glasgow, are quoted at 425.
Waihi, Silverton, is opening up well. The reef
has been struck. A 20-head battery will be ready for
work end of Oct., and first cleaning-up should be about
Christmas. On present prospects the mine should turn
out £BOOO worth of bullion monthly. Shares, 20s. paid,
are readily saleable at 635.
Waihi co. have cut the Martha reef on the low
level. Reef 18ft. thick, and several assays, including
those of the poor section of the lode, average £3 ss. per
ton. The 00. pays a quarterly div. of 2s. this month.

Unionism as practised on the Murchison. When
the Cue Victory co. spoke of erecting a battery and
crushing for the public, many small claims reserved their
stone for the plant. But the C.V. 00. reduced the wages
of all surface hands from £3 10s. to £3 per week, and as
nearly all public crushing comes from small claims run by
working miners, the co. (up to date) has never done a
pound of public crushifig, and its battery has been idle
for three months, after working only six weeks on the
go’s own stone.

Another dodge in mine-flotation. Jones, who
has a duffer on hand, begs, borrows or steals’TOOoz
gold, and dumps it into the hank as the product of his
mine. A few days later he takes it out and replace sitby n
slightly varied parcel (more or less). Repeats this process
ad lib, and then solids Vhome” hank receipts showing
say, 3000 oz. from the Bound to Bust gold-mine. Re ult—
triumphant flotation. ’

Personal Items.
0. C. Kingston has become almost grey
during his two years’ premiership.
Judge Windeyer and Speaker Abbott were
both born on Sept. 29. The first in 1834, and the
second in 1842.
Pritchard Morgan still holds his Welsh
seat in the House of Commons, but by a much-
reduced majority.
The methodical Michael Davitt is believed
to be about the only Irishman who can keep an
appointment—except with a girl.
An attempt was made in the Carington
regime to induce the late Augustus Morris to
insert his head in a C.M.G. noose.
“THE BANJO" (Mr. Arthur Barton
Paterson).
Hampden, prospective Governor of N.S.
Wales, is summed up by Archibald Forbes : —“ A
used-up man-abuut-town in search of a new sensa-
tion.”
Jennings Carmichael is as much like Lady
Hopetoun as Lady H. is like Maggie Stirling.
Each of the three ladies is a reminder of the other
two.
R. S. Smytlie and his two sons are now
all in the entertainment business. They are
■known respectively as the Most, the More, and
the Much Travelled.
The new Vic. M.L.C., lawyer Godfrey,
who ousted J. M. Davies from the Council, is
otherwise notable as the father of Melbourne’s
first lady articled-clerk.
Dr. Pat Moloney, of Collins-street, Melb.,
is the sole survivor of the first class of “meds.” at
Melb. University in 1863. The others were Dr.
Carey Rees and Rev. Alexander Mackie.
Chief Justice Darley encourages diocesan
church building by saying that the more churches
are built the more support they will obtain. That
is lawyer-like. One lawyer only in a town will
starve, two will make a pile.
Ail the Queensland Supreme Court Judges
wear full beards. Excepting Judge Windeyer,
who shaves the upper-lip only, and Justice Man-
ning, who wears a military moustache, N.S.W.
Judges make free use of the razor.
Attorney-General Byrnes’s sing-song speech
in praise of the "dead Justice Harding smelt
strongly of the lamp. “He summoned to his
room the lay coadjutors in the administration of
justice.” What’s wrong with “jurymen ” ? Boil
it down.
Barrister Dr. T. P. MTnerney has been
re-elected warden of Melb. Univ. Senate. T.P.
had a bad habit of speaking for half-an-hour on
every item on the business paper, so, to silence
him, they five years ago elected him warden. The
warden must not speak.
News comes from New Guinea that George
Clarke, the veteran fossicker and explorer, was
murdered by natives whilst prospecting by one of
the rivers. He died as he lived, game as a pebble.
It was Clarke who, with two others, first dis-
covered and named Charters Towers.
A Sydney newspaper manager declares
that ho recently had an application for the post
of reporter from an Australian aborigine, and so
smart and generally competent did he find the
youth that he would have engaged him were he
■not frightened that the staff would revolt.
Perhaps the oldest white Australian native
died at Hobart, last week, in the person of Mr.
Charles Harrison Menzies, a native of Sydney,
where he was born in 179 >. He was taken to
V.D.L. by his parents when only a year old, and
resided there up to the day of his death.
Percy Spence is doing well in England ;
nearly every week he has in Black and White a
half-page drawing of some sort. He also contri-
butes to the Graphic's grandchild, The Golden
Penny, and, as a result of his introductions, lie
has in hand several commissions for portraits.
“Sir Wilfred Lawson,” says an English
paper, “ is probably the only man in the world
who has addressed a political meeting in his night-
shirt.” And G. R. Dibbs, of N.S. W., is probably
the only Premier in the world who has moved the
adjournment of the Assembly without his pants
on.
Father Clancy, of the Jesuit College, who
died last week, was the fifth who has died since
the college was established in Melbourne. Of
these, three, including Father Clancy, died from
the same disease, cancer of the liver. The con-
nection between celibacy and cancer is thus again
strongly forced on the attention.
During one of his speeches, a few years
back, S.A. Premier Kingston had occasion to
quote the Scriptural text which states that “ he
who hath lusted with his eye hath already com-
mitted adultery.” “This,” remarked Charley,
meditatively, “is the cheapest and most unsatis-
factory form of adultery known.”
A Melb. lawyer had an impression that
Higinbotham was soft on the women, and fetched
an attractive one before him, in Chambers, over a
claim against her for fraudulent contraction of a
debt. She dropped her handkerchief, which the
gallant Chief Justice darted to pick up—but he
ordered her six months’ gaol in default of pay-
ment.
A malevolent Australian writes from
London to Sydney ; You have quite a catch in
your new Governor. He gives at his dinners the
worst wine in London, and is known as “Mister
Gripes.” However, as in N.S.W. he will have no
duty to pay on his fizz, ho may not, on the
shores of the Beautiful Harbor, live up to bis
Big Smoke reputation.
Lormer, the Melb. J.P. who fell foul of
his class, the other day, is accused of notoriety-
hunting, of course, by the boomers and their bar-
rackers who bellow “Shame” whenever a parti-
cularly stinking abuse is approached by an un-
favorable critic. Notoriety is now only possible
to the clean man in Melbourne—the other kind of
individual is merely normal.
The recently-deceased P. J. Healy, bar-
rister-at-law and ex-N.S.W. Crown Prosecutor,
was one of a number of clever Irish-Australian
lawyers whose only fault was that they had more
brains than ballast. Three bright men were
Healy, John Dillon, and T. C. O’Mara, and not
one of them did more than show the outside world
a mere glimpse of his real ability.
