Saturday night the poultry-yard was invaded and
the sexes parted till Monday morning, when they
were again allowed to commingle. The Premier’s
attractive voice is said to have been developed at
the cock-fights arising from this custom.
David Syme, of the Age, is strongly
against the use of the Linotype machine in his
office during his lifetime. Replying to a deputa-
tion of his workmen, a short while back, lie stated
that none of the machines would be introduced
whilst he drew breath. “ Besides,” naively added
David “ I have a type-foundry, and it wouldn’t
pay to bring in the machines under the circum-
stances ”

that he is the sole survivor of the Upper House as
he knew it in 1868 when he represented the S. W.
province. Not only are the M. L.C. ’s of those
days all dead, but all the officers and employes

Lord Hopetoun will be
45 on Sept. 25.
Bishop Thornton told
the truth once to his hearers
in Ballarat pro-cathedral—
“ Your way to Heaven is
paved with threepenny-
bits.”
Mr. Justice Hodges is
the shyest man on the Vic.
Bench. There is no bio-
graphy of him to be found
in any of the reference-hooks
—neither in the “colonial
list ” nor in “ Burke’s Colo-
nial Gentry,” nor else-
where, He has always
persisted in refusing to
supply particulars about
himself for publication. He
was born in 1845—one year
after Chief Justice Madden,
and a year before Mr. Jus-
tice Hood.
The late Vic. Justice
Barry, when visiting Wan-
garatta on circuit, always
stayed with a certain J.P.
One day his J.P. friend sat
beside him on the bench,
and the prisoner, asked by
his Honor whether anyone
could speak as to his char-
acter, replied—“ Yes ; Mr.
J.P. there knows me. I’ve
worked for him.” Then
began theJ.P., “Ob, yes,
your Honor, I’ve known”
. The dignified Judge
waved his hand, “Excuse
me, sir, will you please
enter the witness-box?”
Tins made the Jaypee
angry, hut he boxed him-
self. and started off again
“ Oh, yes, I’ve ” .
Another wave of the judi-
cial hand. “ Excuse me,
sir ; you’re not sworn. Con-
stable, swear this witness.”
The oath was taken, and
J.P. made another start,
only to be again stopped by
Barry. “ Witness, what is
your name?” That fin-
ished him. He started pro-
fanity, when the Judge—
“ Sir, I will have no
swearing here; fined one
pound for contempt of
finrt. Stand down, sir.”
He stood down, paid bis
fine, went out, and swore
for an hour by the clock
But the Judge dined with
him that night, as usual.
A correspondent : —There is no truth in
the published report of the reconciliation between
Hori Grey and bis long-estranged wife. Lord
Stamford, a relative of the great pro-Consul s,
took great pains to bring about an understanding,
but failed. He invited Grey to his house, and in
the old man’s bedroom, by way of a “feeler,
placed a portrait of Lady Grey. The veteran was
greatly affected when he recognised the picture,
and expressed a willingness to be reconciled,
Lady Grey, however, refused his advances. She
reßented the stiKma that had been placed on her
, . , v
character, and considered that the smallness of
the allowance made to her (4150 per annum) had
been an act of unnecessary cruelty.
Ada Cambridge (Mis. Cross,
wife of a Williamstown vieur)
greatly objects to being “ inter-
viewed.” She writes, in answer
to a recent application from a
Melb. duly ; “Though I like all
possible publicity for my books.
I greatly shrink from it_in other
ways, preferring as the author of
them to live out of sight as much
as possible, particularly not to
attract notice by what may look
like self-advertisement in the place
where I live.”
Miss Elizabeth Reddall, the
oldest native inhabitant of Camp-
oelltown, N.S.W., and a connect-
ing link with the days of the
“ System,” died last week in that
town, where she lived all her life,
at the age of 73. Campbell town
was named by Gov. Macquarie in
1820, at which time he appointed
Rev. Thomas Reddall chaplain
Ur the district. Miss Elizabeth
Reddall was the parson’s seventh
daughter, and well remembered
the “ pomp and circumstance ” of
the official entry of Chief Justice
Forbes to open the first local
Assize Court. The procession
started from Liverpool, and con-
sisted of troopers with drawn
swords, the sheriff and his officers,
the chaplain (Mr. Reddall), 20
carriages “filled with gentlemen.’’
50 horsemen also assumed to be
gentlemen, and 100 “persons cn
foot,” the latter presumably not
gentlemen. Though hut a child
when the apparition was seen, she
well remembered the excitement
ovef “Fisher’s ghost,” which s
still a stock story in Campbell-
town.
The Argus sub editor and all his myrmi-
dons couldn’t make bead or tail of a certain leader,
and it was taken back to the author, the illegible
Willoughby. Willoughby stared at the slip fur
five minutes and seemed hopelessly fogged him-
self. Eventually a meaningful “ Ah-h !” pro-
claimed the mystery solved. “Why, confound
it,” he said, “I’ve been looking at the blessed
thing upside-down.”

POLITICAL POINTS
The Victorian pension-list is about sd. a
week per taxpayer.
Our usual Queensland leper has been
found—in the Burdekin district this week.
Engineer Bulcock (Q.) approaches opposi-
tion candidates with offers of support if only they
will renounce the Labor-party.
Mrs. Mary Lee and Miss Spence decline
to accept tbe Parliamentary nominations offered
them by the S. A. Labor Plebiscite.
Premier Turner’s brilliant success may
direct one’s mind to Deakin, the academic, who
had the ball at his foot, but gave Turner a little
mark.
The recent “ rowdy ” anti-sweating meet-
ing, at Mclb. Town Hall, forcibly suggests the
need for election of Mayors by the citizens, as in
America.
The average direct cost to Government of
Sydney unemployed is something over £lOOO per
month. The first six months of present year
totalled £6319.
John Downer, Q.C., professes to be sick of
politics, and says that he will probably stand out
next year. He is tired of Kingston’s monopoly
of theS.A. bremiership.
“Mr. M’Millan threw his brickbat at the
monotony of it from under the glass roof of one
of the oldest temples of bathetic boredom.” —
“ Outis ” in Sydney Telegraph.
There are signs of trouble between S.A.
land-taxers and tlie Labor-party. Henry Taylor,
one of the foremost land-taxers, lias refused to be
nominated as a Labor candidate for 1896 election.
A Vie. M.P. has fairly aroused the
country to tlie advantages of a State tobacco
monopoly. A Parliamentary Committee is ap-
pointed to consider the question. Drink will
follow the smoke.
The Maoriland House of Representatives
has passed a Bill abolishing the attachment of
wages for debt. The theory is steadily gaining
ground that the State shouldn't be an agency for
collecting anybody’s bad debts.
The Kwong Yick Wah 80, the Sydney
Chinese paper, asserts that through the operation
of Cliinese Restriction legislation iu N.S. W.
Cuiuese agricultural labor is more highly-paid
than European and black-labor.
Premier Kingston protests his sincerity as
a law reformer, and has prepared a measure which
will opeu tlie eyes of even laymen. But the time
is not yec ripe. Law reform will hit lawyers
Symon and Downer in a soft place when election-
time comes.
A metropolitan candidate at last N.S. VV.
election, when asked what he had done during
the 12 months he had been iu Parliament,
answered glibly, “ I have obtained the promise to
build a lock-up in the electorate !” They enthu-
siastically re-elected him.
Q. election gossip : Premier Nelson is to
contest MTlwratih’s seat in North Brisbane, and
Morelmad has refused nomination fur Murauoa.
Huolau will contest Cairns, while T. J. Byrnes
bus an eye on Fortitude Valley. Lilley is to run
for Cunningham ; and Glassey fur Lockyer.
M’Millan has evidently returned to be a
thorn iu tlie side of the Reid Government. “I
am a consistent Freetrader,” said tlie little man
from Burwood (equivalent to “ 1 am a consistent
importer ”); but, all the same, his first speech was
a savage attack on the Reid Ministry, hardly
relieved by a single gleam of approval.
The Victorian sweaters are making a hard
fight against the Govt, minimum wage—con-
tractors engaging men at stipulated rates and
deducting a certain amount weekly for the use of
tools. Paperhaiignig is not yet listed us a mini-
mum wage trade, so another tavurite device is to
employ alleged papel hangers at low wages to do
all sorts of work.
Why should N.S.W. minor Judge Back-
house (Who is removeable at the behest of the
Ministry) be appointed to review the case of
Johnson, in which Supreme Court Justices
Wnideyor and Darley are concerned? —Mr. Win-
deyer having committed the man for alleged per-
jury, and Mr. Darley having tried him. The
situation is an absolutely monstrous one.
One large N.S.W. proprietor objects to
the Land-Tax because it is directly aimed at the
big landlords, a class “who have made N.S.W.
what it is.” This is satisfactory in one sense.
The people who have made N.S.VV. what it is
want to be hanged with all possible speed. The
difficulty hitherto has been to identify them, but
when a big land-holder confesses that he and his
kind are the guilty parties, there is no visible
reason for further delaying the execution.
The N.S. VV. Director of Agriculture, in a
report lo the Legislature, recommends that the
present sugar-duties be retained, and that central
mills bo erected to help along the industry.
There is a very large extent of land, he states,
suitable for sugar-growing, and under the central-
mill system the business might expand enor-
mously, but if the duly is removed the probable
result will be a complete collapse. In short, the
N.S. VV. Government’s own expert reports in effect
that the Government is an ass.
To a deputation asking for the reduction
of Sydney tram-fares, on the ground that ’bus
fares were now largely reduced, N.S. W. Railway
Commissioner Oliver gave some well-deserved
thrusts. He reminded the interviewers that
’buses had no roadway to construct, and that the
’busmen worked many hours a day for very little
money—if the trams were run on the same prin-
ciples, the fares could be much lowered. After
that the deputation withdrew speechless and dis-
gusted, as several of its number were known to
be more or less mixed up in the ’bus industry.
Tlie Commissioner’s view of this fare-reduction
question is the true democratic one.
As bearing upon the anti-alien movement
now again assuming political significance in N.S.
Wales, the fact may be mentioned that in France
no foreigner is allowed to serve the State except
in the capacity of street-sweeper. Even in that
work, foreigners must not exceed one-tenth of the
total body thus employed. In French State
schools the cultivation of the English language
has of late gained a prominent place, and efforts
were made in a few quarters to have the language
taught by an Englishman, but after a brief wranglt
the ruling was given that as Italians are nor
allowed to teach Italian in these schools, soneithee
should Englishmen be allowed to teach English—
always, of course, excepting naturalised foreigners.
Young Parkes (son of old Parkes), with the
Sydney unemployed deputation : “Couldn’t you
spend .£200,000 in forest clearing at once, and get
the sanction of Parliament afterwards?” Of
course. What does Parliament exist for except to
give its sanction afterwards? But The Bulletin
hopes that some day, when a Government has
spent £200,000 on its own authority, Parliament
won’t give its sanction afterwards, and will
demand that the money be repaid, and, if it isn’t
forthcoming, will sell up the tables and chairs and
other belongings of each Minister and make him
insolvent. Then Parliament will begin for the
first time to get some control over the public ex-
penditure. At present it does little save shake a
retrospective fist at the inevitable.
Under the Reid (N.S. VV,)alleged Freetiade
tai iff, the duty on oils doesn’t wholly disappear until
July, 1897 ; that on candles until July, 1899 ; the
duties on biscuits, confectionery, fruits, jams,
jellies and preserves are not to be wholly removed
until July, 1900; and the sugar-duty vanishes in
July, 1901. The Reid party alleges that Protec-
tion raises prices—except when it alleges that it is
Freetrade that raises prices; and, therefore, it
would seem that it proposes to handicap the
N.S. W. manufacturers of biscuits, confectionery,
jams, jellies, etc., by making them pay a duty on
sugar for 12 months, during which they must
compete with imported goods made with sugar
that doesn’t pay duty! If the Reid party believes
in its own theory, the duties should come off
simultaneously.
Scraps from the report of last week’s
Sydney unemployed deputation. Varney Parkes,
M.L.A. (cast-iron Foreign-trader): “The Govern-
ment would have to find employment for 12,000
men.” Whiddon, M.L.A. (cast-iron Foreign-
trader) : “ Couldn’t the Government get an
advance from some fund and immediately start
forest-thinning?” Hughes, M.L.A. (cast-iron
Foreign-trader); “Employment could lie found
in pulling down condemned buildings.” Varney
Parkes; “Why couldn’t they spend £100,000?
There was no reason why the Government should
not go beyond the law and ask Parliament after-
wards. . . . Cannot you at once spend
£200,000 on forest-thinning?” Hughes; “You
can start the work of clearing settlements at once.”
Premier Reid : “If the Upper House blocks the
Loan Bill I will do something or other, . . .
I will galvanise them at once. ... I am
determined that at once the wheels will begin to
go round.” What consolation fur tbe 12 000 unem-
ployed to know that tbe Premier is going to do
something or other 1

The Thursday Island Japs are “ ram-
pageous” at the action of Mr. Satow, of the
Japanese Chib, who has gone home to arrange for
a further immigration from Japan. The Thurs-
day Island Jap sees clearly that this means
reduced wages for him. At a recent meeting of
the club, the old committee was called upon to
resign, and one member was violently assaulted.
If the A.L.F. cared to send an organiser North,
it could easily enlist the Japs under its
banner, and gain an ally to the cause of Australia
for the Australians.
“ The remainder of my life,” says Mr.
M'Millan, M.P. (N.S.W.), “will be taken up iu
studying the great industries of this country, and
making this country what it is destined to be for
the next 30 years—one of the great food-providers
of the world.” Happy prospect! The Corn-
stalk’s destiny is to milk cows and grow pork for
J. Bull. Also, to wear J. Bull’s shoddy.
MAIDEN INNOCENCE.
SHE: “ Tell me, Jack, do they weigh the horses first, or the jockeys,
or both together! ”
Again the walk-abnut Kanaka. It is now
suggested that restrictive legi-iation be intro-
duced from the West Indies, for the benefit of
Tommy Tanna. In Jamaica, no coolie or Chinese
plantation-laborer is allowed to enter or visit any
other plantation without a pass from bis employer
for a certain date, under penalty of being arrested.
Note the countries Bananaland looks to for legis-
lative examples !
When the fitness of a Ministry to hold
office is challenged by Parliamentary resolution,
it suspends business until the indictment is dis-
posed of. This being so, wby should a Supreme
Court Judge, whose fitness to hold office is chal-
lenged, continue to exercise his functions ? Is the
lawyer-politician who manages to hoist himself on
to the Bench to be regarded as superior to the
Legislature of the country? Judge-worship has
gone a lot too far in Australia.
Under the new standing orders of N.S.W.
Assembly, a disorderly member is rushed right
out of the buildings and the grounds, and thrown
into Macquarie-street, whereas formerly he was
only ejected from the Chamber itself and left in
the refreshment-room. M'Elhone was the first
completely-thrown-out member under the new
system. The advantage is that other members
are no longer seized upon, every time they come
out for a smoke, by inflamed persons with ob-
jectionable breaths, who want to relate their
grievances at great length.
Murray Smith, combating Hancocks
efforts to restore the 20 per cent, duty upon cor-
sets in Vic. Assembly, declared that “ the reduced
duty would give relief to the working classes.”
Yet Murray Smith fought against the proposed
abolition of the rice-duty. He evidently believes
that the best way to stay the stomachs of the poor
is from the outside.
An Anarchist-club
existed iu Melbourne a
lustrum back. It had
rooms in the Alexandra
Theatre, in one of the
first-floor windows of
which was displayed a
flag with “ Liberty,
Equality, Fraternity ”
in red. Weekly debates
were held, and the club
rejoiced in the posses-
sion of two schools of
Anarchy—the active
and the passive. Women
orated there occa-
sionally, and one or two
not badly. Subjects
varied from the Refer-
endum to Oneida pro-
blems, and, with all its
defects, the club was an
oasis in the intellectual
desert of Melbourne.
Dear Bulletin, —
You draw attention to
tbe much-diminished
power of Sydney Muni-
cipal Council in the pre-
sentN.S.W. Parliament.
Time has certainly come
for the political aider-
man’s extermination.
He is a dead failure.
Aldermanie fat is not
and never has been the
kind of fat statesmen are
made of. For a com-
pletely abject example of
political failure look at
gilded Matt Harris, self-
centred as the metro-
politan municipal party
in the new Assembly.
Lees, Manning, and
Burdekin have had their
turns, and the ablest
politician of the three
was Burdekin, whose
superfluous cash atoned
for other deficiencies.
Fowler is banished lo
the State charnel-house
(yclept “ the oth e i
place”) and Martin,
Chapman, and Jeanne-
ret were three other per•-
sistent nonentities. Municipal apprenticeship
unfits men for anything larger than the supervision
of gutters.— Thorold.
Kingsbury, M.P. for North Brisbane, and
the glorified apostle of the cottage piano, will
have a tussle at next election. His mercurial
temperament makes him a constant source of
anxiety to local Pat Man, who fears he is not
“reliable.” He can’t be trusted to snore ster-
torously in the bar till the bell rings and then
suffer himself to be led gently to his seat. But
if he sometimes talks like a Democrat, he in-
variably votes like a Tory,
Agent-General Gillies, at a London ban-
quet “ Lord Brassey and the colonies, lam
sure, will work together to promote the benefit of
mankind and to favor human freedom.” Which
incoherent whoop boils down into the plain fact
that Lord Brassey will hang round in a quiet
unobtrusive manner and not look any more absurd
than he can help, and that the “colonies” will
plod along the best way they know how. Why
doesn’t Premier Turner cable Gillies to keep quiet
and get his hair cut ?
John M'Elhone was, as might have been
expected, the first man ejected from the present
N-S. W. Parliament for (leaving epithets at his
fellow-member. At the same time, the dear old
combustible John flatly denies having alluded to
[Fitzpatrick’s “evil eye.” He says that he spok**
of that politician’s 41 eagle eye,” but that the
chairman, being hard of hearing, called him to
order by mistake, and John, being also hard of
hearing, didn’t know lie was being called to order,
consequently he was run out in a state sf bewilder-
ment, and didn’t understand the situation till le
saw it explained in the morning-paper.
Adolph Csesar Shadier and Austin Chaj
man contested Braidwood electorate at last
N.S.VV. election, and Shadier got beaten. There-
upon Shadier summoned Chapman under the
Electoral Act for allowing one Meyer Levinsohn to
pay on his behalf for certain whisky given for the
purpose of corruptly influencing electors. In tin
course of the case (as reported in a daily paper'
a person, described as a reporter, admitted
“ that at the time of the alleged ‘ treating ’ he was
an officer of a local temperance lodge. He hac*
three glasses of spirits that evening.” Prosecutoi
Shadier said he saw Levinsohn paying for drinks ;
he considered that Levinsohn asked the men tc
drink in order to break up his meeting—us a
matter of fact he got no opportunity of expressing
his views. This is quite likely. From what The
Bulletin knows of Mr. Shadier it quite believes
that the ordinary elector would rather drink twice
than hear Mr. Shadier speak once. Also, there
is a lot more Adolph than Ccesar about Shadier
aforesaid.

Aunt Mary’s Baking Powder
SKIN DISEASE
CUBED BY
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla
Mr. Geo. W. Enrtner, Keezletown, Va.,
writes, as follows:
“ Shortly alter leaving college, I was troubled
with a skin disease which showed itself, first, at
the ankles. Physicians pronounced it eczema,
and treated me for that complaint. The erup-
tion crept slowly up my limbs, and on the body,
until it enveloped the whole frame. It gave me
infinite trouble with constant itching, casting off
of dry scales, and a watery liquid which would
exude from under the scales. I treated it for
over three years unsuccessfully, and was unable

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The waves come flawless from the hand of God,
Fresh, and for ever new, and all the ocean
Folds from the orient ecstacy’s emotion,
As if divinity for ever trod
Upon the infinite purple deep and broad ;
And every billow pours its one devotion
To die upon the shore in perfect motion,
White, chaste and shadowless in its last abode.
We, miserable, fallen on a time
Too much o’erwrought and prematurely old,
And only knowing grief is each day’s friend,
Do but despairingly adore the chime
Of the wild surges, chanting without end
Secrets of joy to human ears untold.
Maoriland.
HUBERT CHURCH.
Tasmania is about to adopt “Federal
time ; ” which means advancing her clock lOmin.
40jsec., to the disgust of certain sleepy officers.
At present mariners are warned by the Admiralty
against correcting chronometers by theSlowbart
Time Ball; and a fight, which is really a struggle
for existence by the Slowbart Meteorological
Station, is proceeding.
Tas. Govt, gives married men the prefer-
ence when engaging labor. Recently some work
was “out ” which called for skilled and unskilled
labor. The Govt, provides tools for the latter,
but the former (carpenters, &c.) have to find their
own. They all rank even on the pay-sheet, how-
ever—viz., ss. per day.
Last Westminster Gazette has a singu-
larly original and un-newspapery article on the
British elections. First it points out how thin a
top-layer of the English manhood votes at all, or
is able to do so, in producing the national voice.
Next, it contends that all the party engineering,
which works these sensational swings of the
pendulum, is exerted on a comparatively small
floating mass of electors, of the most inferior class,
while the Radicals and Tories remain solid.
“ How many Australians know how to
make soup out of a kangaroo tail ? ” writes a
Bulletin correspondent. “In nine out of every
ten shops where these delicacies are sold, the
salesman will generously offer to skin the tail for
the customer before sending it to his residence,
and in 99 cases out of 100 the customer accepts the
kindly offer. The remaining genius generally
skins the tail himself in the back-yard, slicing it
off in junks as he would sharpen a pencil. Yet
the skin of the kangaroo tail is the one particular
part which it is highly necessary to preserve in
order to get the full advantage of its glutinous
nature. Kangaroo-tail soup, a skinned article, is
like the water a pound of chops has been boiled
in, while ‘ the real Mackay ’ is a dish fit for the
gods—if there be any such persons.” Ox-tail
soup would doubtless be much more glutinous if
founded chiefly on a basis of bullock-hide. Some
people cannot bring themselves to regard as soup
anything of less consistency than glue.
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
The Auckland (M.L.) Rescue Work Society
is an association of elderly females of both sexes,
which wrestles with the “social evil” and keeps its
inflamed eye constantly to the keyhole, so to
speak. At a recent meeting this body decided to
“ get the opinion of a local gentleman who inter-
ested himself in such matters on the question:
‘ls unchastity in itself not a crime calling for
legal punishment ?’ ” The Bulletin wouldn’t be
that local gentleman and have a reputation for
“interesting itself in such matters ” for a good
deal. At the same time, it would remark that it
is acquainted with a certain financier, with along,
red beard, who is now understood to be floating
mines somewhere or other, and who knows more
about such subjects than anyone else in this
country, and if the Rescue Society likes to lay the
matter before him, his opinion would be valuable.
Last time this paper saw the person in question he
was hastily leaving a restaurant, chiefly, it
believes, on account of the interest he took in such
matters, and the information he could supply
would make the society’s back iiair stand on end.

Priddy’s.
EXTERIOR VIEW.
Decorative display is becoming a prominent
feature among the better class of Sydney business
establishments. Such an exclusive attraction has
lately developed under the three months’ able
architectural supervision of Messrs. Mansfield
and Son right in the very heart of busiest Sydney
—at 424 Goorge-street, adjoining the Royal Hotel.
The accompanying illustrations give interior and
exterior views.
# # *
This shop is the largest of its class in the
Southern Hemisphere, and its harmonious interior
decorations and artistic coloring altogether dis-
tinguish it from any shop of its own size in Aus-
tralia. In its early days it was the head office of
Cobb and Co.’s coaches, and the men of the past
generation who started from here with dreams of
gold would fancy they were in one of the chambers
of Aladdin’s palace could they see the electric-
light flashing over this goodly model of shop-
decorative art.
w • •
The electric-light is not more glorious than the
Western star or the Eastern star, but it has
superior polished fittings and an advantageous
variety of beautiful colors. It is, too, simple in
construction and obviously more useful. Besides,
it has the advantage that it can be turned out at
will by gently pressing a button —a consummation
which it is devoutly wished may yet come for the
over-assertive star of the political firmament,
• * *
Compared with the shop electric-light ofte ;
seen, it is a Boon. It does not hang by a string
from a hole knocked in the ceiling, and, therefore,
no customer need dread the glass falling and
striking off his oar or nose or cutting out his eye.
Nor does it glare satire upon dingy walls and fly-
specked ceiling. But here at night it enhances
the effect of already singular effect, and decorate!
already present decoration.
# * *
For half-a-oentury a great proportion of the
Sydney and N.S.W. male population has gone to
Fame or Fortune with the name of Priddy above
its brain-pan. Only one man during that period
has objected to the wearing of the name. He said ;
“ The name of no man shall I wear above my
head ; purge thou thy name from this hat; let the
inside lining thereof be of linen white and spot-
less.” This man came to Australia for the express
purpose of passing round the hat, and naturally,
therefore, he would not give a hatter a free adver-
tisement.
# * *
It is a sphinx-riddle to know where a man
always get a good head, but at Priddy’s he can
always get a good hat. And as a hat is the
measure of the head, and as the head is the
measure of the man, a good hat cannot be too
forcibly impressed upon the head of this com
munity.
It is the crown that makes the king, and the
hat that makes the man. “ Take everything from
me,” said Beau Brummell, “ but leave me my beat
„.,.. „ . ~
hat.” The naked Maori having put on a bell-
, , , , .. , , ... ' lf „
topper and handsome tie he felt himself constitu-
. j . n t n *. tt “a >•
ted to call at Govt. House. A man, said
~, j c i ■ i i
Thackeray, if he wears a new and fashionable
. . . , , , ,i i • „. Q
hat, may spit in the street and not lose his caste
’ H t n .... t
as a gentleman. but the same man spitting from
, , ■ ~, „„„ _j ..
under a shabby hat is done ; he is a blackguard.
# # #
Let the head be shaped like an Apollo’s, like a
pear, pumpkin, sugarloaf, or marrow ; let it be a
reversion to a former type, or unlike anything on
the earth, in the sky above, or in the waters
beneath, Priddy can perfectly adorn this crest
after measuring it with the hat conformator.
# # #
This is a French machine for ascertaining the
exact shape of the head, and once the conforma-
tion of the specimen is thus differentiated, manu-
factoring a hat to hide it means both business and
pleasure. The modest Turk can have his turban
or fez, and the globetrotter, who six weeks before
dandled his Johnny stick along the sweet shady
side of Pall Mall, can hide his jovelike brow under
the last nail-ka-ag from Bond-st. fashion-shops.
# * #
Priddy has a London agent whose daily duty it
is to mark and seize tiie best and latest hats that
grace Hyde Park. The hat-eye of this agent is
t to be diverted by even the charming bicycle
girl in her bloomers, and thus he keeps the Sydney
establishment most steadfastly up-to-date.
, K nc ,i MM , M
With time, money, and opportunity businesses
’ „„
change. Without these, they change much faster;
B J

r , , , • ~ „

•_ „ .
in annexing the large new premises herein illus-

mercer and exclusive hatter. Also he will still
l cee P on l ” s show at 078 Brickfield Hill, am ,if
possible, improve the already excellent stock of
fc hat well-known institution.
... . , • A . „
A cheap thing is a dear thing, and true economy
is getting one’s money s worth. Priddy has never
thought of attempting the vulgar cheap business,
hence his customers, like his stock, are of t le
good or better class. He lias lately made arrange-
ments for the regular importation of high-class
specially manufactured on his own order and
design to suit the conditions of this climate. As
this original move is the outcome of half-a-
century s experience in the Sydney trade, the
customer can rely not only upon getting the
highest-class goods at the lowest reasonable rate,
but can also rest assured that it will not be
Priddy’s fault if the new and special hat does not
make the man who wears it.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Yio. Legislative gammon re a.bill to prohibit all
bet ting has ended seemingly in an act to suppress betting
in the street. The .existing law provides for raids fob
tobacconists’ shops, and could .shut up>the clubs, if it
felt so disposed. Therefore, this dirty little Act to wipe
out street-bookmakers, who have been illegal practitioners
all the time,.is an insult.to the human understanding.
•’ • » *
A “great” Melb. newspaper states the ease
against small bookmakers with exceeding, .frankness.
They popularise gambling ‘inthe lower strata of the
.community. Their business is-not a. whit more illegal,
dishonest, or sinful than that'of the bigger bookmakers
who exploit higher strata of the community—hut they
are of no value to' the Press'. They don’t organise-subur-
ban race-meetings, or bring any- sort of advts. to a
“great” newspaper - proprietor’s pocket. Therefore,
wipe ’em out in order.that the higher-strata men may do
more business whilst attracting Jess attention than before.
It isn’t a matter of one law for the rich and another for
the poor. The law is always the same, but it works up-
wards .instead of downwards, and stops short before
getting to the glazed surface of racing speculation.
• • * »
The people behind Bruin think that staying is
his forte, and it must be remembered that he finished con-
siderably nearer to Devon in the Melbourne than in the
Caulfield Cup race. Bruin’s weak point is, or was, his
heart. In the Newmarket Handicap, Toorak ditto, and
Caulfield Cup the handsome beast appeared to collapse
when fairly tackled. In the Melb. Cup he never came
bang to the front, but was going vigorously at the finish,
not half a length behind Patron’s tail.
» • • *
Thus far, the most likely winner of Caulfield
Cup has been over-looked/ by newspaper-betting reporters)
whilst a very little less-iikely stable-companion has
figured boldly.
* * ♦ *
After Oct. 1 all persons convicted in Sydney of
running “totes” will be sent to gaol “without the
option.” Double-takers will be fined not less than £lO
for the first offence—for the second offence, gaol.

Ikey Mo squeals in his agony! “James Mitohell,
a bookmaker, who) damaged a totalisator machine at
MorphetviMe, S.A., when enraged on Saturday, was fined
£l5.
« * * •
Melb. Argus has desoended'to the old sporting-
rag' practice of publishing Cup dreams, which seduce
feeble-minded punters into the stupidest of all racing
follies. Last week the Arcus announced, with ghastly
seriousness—“ Since the publication in this column, a
few days ago, of particulars of a rather remarkable
dream bearing on the two Cups, backers have been coining
very strongly for the double, Bruin and Auraria. One
member of, the ring received no fewer than 26 applica-
tions(mostly from the country)! ! for this particular com-
bination in the course of a couple of days. All the money
at 1000 to 1 has been snapped up, and many backers are
taking what maybe considered a false price rather than
miss the double.” Then this precious benefactor of Ikey
Mo goes on to mention that “ dreams, it need hardly be
pointed out, are very delusive.” But there is nothing
delusive, to the initiated, in the system of laying these
fool-traps. Ikey Mo himself is a most industrious
dreamer or instigator of alleged dreams. It need hardly
be pointed out that the man who goes to, the trouble of
getting a visionary “ double ” into print has possibly a
financial object to serve, and that one, at least, of the
horses, he mentions may be regarded with particular
suspicion.
The Argus should,, get it.s sub-editor, or town-
canvasser, or night-porter, to dream that he backed the
winning double for 1895 with 15 bookmakers, none of
whom cashed up. The Bulletin, having lately been
assailed with brazen assertions of- Ikey M'o.’s virtue, and
worth, takes this opportunity to warn mankind against
the deplorable error of backing any “ double ”■ with any
bookmaker, outside of about six men who. conduct this
dangerous branch of their business legitimately. Gener-
ally speaking, there is but one investment a punter
laments more bitterly than a losing “ dopble,” and
that is the miraculous "double” which* “wins.”
Victoria to-day contains fully two hundred unpaid pickers
of Paris and Patron for last year’s Cups, and N.S.W. can
produce a few similar wallers. And, since there is no
authenticated instance of a defaulting.double-event layer
having been murdered or severely walloped for his fraud,
Ikey Mo grows more reckless every time. If a pair of
early fancies got .home for the Clips this year, the average
odds against payment of a •“ lucky ” double wo.uld be 30
to 1 or thereabouts. Fifty per 'cent, of the wagers already
written down are ndt worth sixpence in the £ in the event
of a “ win."

Can’t Dodge a Cold.
Colds laugh at an umbrella, tjop ooat,s, or heavy
boots; the chill and dampness of the foggy
weather courses through even the heaviest cloth-
ing. There is not a man, woqjan, or child in
Australia but what occasionally has a cold. You
do not always know how you get it, but everyone
knows that severe colds are the forerunner of half
thehuman ailments. 7 The feet getcold, the head hot,
circulation becomes unequal, rthe kidneys become
Irritated,-the liver torpid, the bronchial tubes in-
flamed, or the-excess of uric acid in it he blood is
arrested-and the accumulation causes acute rheu-
matic pains.
Read on.
The first shiver, or sneeze, or chilly sensation
you feel, procure a bottle of Warner’s Safe‘Cure,
the timely use of which will save you from these
menacing developments resulting from a cold. It
quickens the circulation, and the kidneys cannot
become inactive under its use. It enlivens the
liver, and' the bile cannot accumulate in excess.
It kills the-germs of'the cold, and acts promptly
in allaying inflammation. Mr. F. Cobcroft,
miner, Newcastle, writes: “Having caught a
severe chill while working in the coal-mines in this
neighbourlmod, alarming symptoms developed, v
and I became very ill. The attack ultimately
settled in the kidneys. The urine became of a
colour, depositing a brick-dust sediment.
I suffered excruciating 'pains in the back, and
periodically in the region of the heart. My limbs
at last became quite numbed and almost useless.
Becoming alarmed* I obtained the advice of a
doctor,-who said I had inflammation of tlie kid-
neys. He prescribed medecines, which, however,
did me no good. I was about to call another doc-

bottles, and two bottles of Warner’s Safe Pills,

racing home in the Derby. It was apparent that he was
the most forward of the field. Condition won the race.
O’Brien paid only 85gs. for the Welcome Jack youngster.

• ♦ * *
The system of letting horses out for an “airing,”
which would bring a Duke of ‘Westminster into bad
odour, is actually commended by the sporting press.
A newspaper nmralist wrote of- a certain horse’s appear-
ance for a race*, the other Saturday, “the cognoscenti
agreed that was only having the perfectly justifi-
able ‘sweat'in public,’one of, in my opinion, the wisest
courses-trainenj<oan pursue, as one race is worth half-a-
dozen private gallops to put condition on-a horse.” This
is utter nonsense, anyway, and pestilential rot from a
sportsman’s point of view. Observe how the theory
adapts itself to the wiles of admittedly “ oronk ” owners.
Who shall decide as to whether a horse had enough
“ condition on him ” when he wasn’t a goer? If he runs
as stiff as the proverbial crutch—well, the noble animal
was having a “ necessary sweat in public,” and his unex-
pected victory in the next race he starts for is the result
of the “ wise course" his jockey pursued a day or two
before.

In the English season’s batting averages,
Qwynn, of Dublin'University, is first with 56, M’Laren
second with 'sl‘2, and Grace third with 51. Gwynn’s
average is.on a small numb.er of innings, and practically
Grace again heads the English batsmen. During the past
-32 years, Grace’s biggest average was,in '7l, with 78‘2,
'his lowest in ’9l, with 197. From ’6B to ’74 his lowest
average was 53'4 ; his highest, 78‘2. This present average
of 51-is his first oyer 50 since ’B7, when he reached 54 •&,
and the s.econd'over sCLsince ’76, when he put up C2‘4 per
innings. He,began in ’64 with an average of 57 - 4, and
shis yearly average in first-class cricket on the total
innings played in ’64, and, the e'iisuing 30 years, comes to
40'7. This year, therefore, Grace has beaten his yearly
average, compiled oirifSl years’ basis, by 10'3 runs.

The crushing defeat inflicted by Stoddart’s
team upon an All-England eleven, captained by Grace,
brings balm to Australian wounds. In the first innings
the'soores were remarkably team 217,
and All-England 220. In the second, after six of Stod-
dart’s team had fallen for 120, ForJaiid Lockwood put on
169 without losing their wickets, Stoddart declaring the
innings closed with 289 f for 6 wickets. All-England then
fell for 68 in two hoursfoefore Richardson and Peel. The
collapse of a whole team so suddenly after the masterly
display of Ford and Lock-wood argues that the wicket was
just sufficiently worn to suit Richardson’s deliveries. A
fast right-hand bowler and a heady medium left-hander
like Peel are an awkward combination if the wicket
" does anything ; ” nevertheless, according to cable,
Riohattdson’is paralysers were the chief factor. Richard-
son is fourth in this season’s English bowling averages,
and Peel si^th.

That opinion of Riobardson’a about the English
elevens beating the next Australian team fairly easily,
will doubtless be verified if, as usual, the English authori-
ties fix the All England v. Australia dates-so late ini the
season that the Australians are stale. Certainly the
English cricketers play hard- through a hard season, but
they are in their own homes, or ought to he, and, there-
fore, they do not come in for travelling hardships, which
are hardships when they are the only rest a man gets
after working at cricket-playing. One team of Aus-
tralian cricketers went in the legs towards the season’s
close, and the ironical English papers would exclaim, in
loudest type, when the Australians did win a match,
“ The Australians have suffered a victory at last.”

ghastly satire on the mcinument-craze in general. To-day
your friends plant your crave with roses ; next week they
won’t trouble to shoo the goats oil.

Fitzsimmons’s first notable appearance in the
ring dates from the visit of Abe Hicken’s travelling
athletic 00. to Timaru, M.L., a few years ago. On open-
ing, Abe offered a pipe to the winner of a lOst. competi-
tion among the local lads. Fitz., being then under
that limit, entered, and, despite his clumsy form, his
long reach and wiry frame managed to soor.e. This win
encouraged him to follow up boxing, and he practised
assiduously, but at first apparently without much im-
provement, judging by the sorry display he gave on his
Sydney d6hut.

Victoria is the proud possessor of one teetotal
bowling-green. When Jimmy Munro made Armidale
Club the present of its site, he attached a condition in
the deed of gift that no liquor.should be sold there. Yet
it has plenty of members, and Armidale Club holds the
premier pennant. But there is a hotel near by ; also,
there are loud grumbles about members having so far to
walk ; likewise, most of the Armidale Club’smatcheS are
played on other (non-teetotal) grounds.

successful, the price of bikes will come down to £B.

• * * •
A novel point in tennis. A strong wind is on.
A, playing against the Wind, returns the ball very high.
It lands in B’s court near the net, but the wind blows' it,
after the bounce, on to A’s side of the net. B runs round
the post, and meeting the ball before its second time of
touching ground, plays it into A’s court successfully.
The rule says “players must stand on opposite sides of
the net,” so, technically, B loses the stroke. But it is
plain that morally B should count the stroke, because the
high wind is what lawyers and ship-men call “ the act of
God,” and, therefore, a matter beyond the responsibility
of the defendant.
* • * •
A 0. of E. bazaar was held somewhere in Aus-
tralia, a few weeks back, and with the idea of drawing
the cash from the army of local barrackers the name of
“Tracker” M'Dougail, a football god, was placed on the
list of patrons. At the opening ceremony, the genial
Tracker turned up in his best suit and stood beside the
secretary, basking in the admiring glances of the
assemblage. Suddenly the sec. felt a tug at his arm.
“ Who’s that old chap ? ” asked the Unparalleled follower.
“Why that’s the bishop” (he’s a very ugly bishop, by
the way), was the reply. “ Him the bishop! If yer gave
him a nut he’d scratch himself.” So much for footballers
as assistants at religious bazaars. And, on consideration,
so much for bishops.

The Vie. authorities sent a double force of
police, with an official shorthand-writer, down to
Yarra Bank Sunday meeting, where J. A.
Andrews made his first public appearance in Mel-
bourne after a five years’ absence in N.S.W. He
told them he was imprisoned in Sydney for,
amongst other things, bringing the police-force
and the administration of justice into contempt.
“Now, I couldn't do that," he remarked by way
of parenthesis—and the crowd smiled audibly.
Two more Victorian attorneys suspended
last week, making seven in all. “We are Seven.’
His Majesty showed great presence of mind, and suc-
ceeded in saving himself, though his attendant was
drowned.— Cabled account of Servian Alexander’B escape
from drowning.
Should the Prince be drowned, and the Serving-
man
Get off with a cold in the head,
’Twould be “Shocking Neglect of a Serving-man,”
And “ A Monarch of Europe Dead
But, since it has chanced that the Servian Prince
Has left the attendant behind,
It’s “ Gallant Escape of a Servian Prince ”•
And “Wonderful Presence of Mind.”
The prospectus of a new Sydney daily, the
Australian Times, is published, capital .£lOO,OOO
in 20,000 £5 shares. It has a wealthy directorate,
and if the editorial part of the business possesses
as much brains as are accumulated on the board
it should do well. So far as can be gathered from
the prospectus, the Times is intended to be the
organ of pure National Ass principles, or else it
intends to sit eloquently on a rail. Anyhow, it
states as distinctly as possible that it intends to
maintain a rousing fight with the morning papers
already existing, and The Bulletin wishes it
success on the general ground that a fight is an
exhilarating thing at all times and good for the
soul.
Someone sends along a picturesque horror
from the Queensland backblocks. The scene was
a bush pub., where a shearer had been swamping
his cheque in rum, and having the jim-jams in the
stable “ between whiles.’’ Finally, he got posses-
sion of a gun and blew most of his head off on the
doorstep, and his remains were laid out in a shed
to await burial. Then a great inspiration came
upon the frowsy houri of the bar. She had a
lover who resided in the house, and who had
recently shown signs of falling away from his
allegiance and offering his soiled hand to a
younger divinity. And the barmaid, who had
long been craving for an overwhelming ven-
geance, now saw a way to get square with
her horny deceiver. That night the young man
came in late with the sidelong air of a dog which
has been stealing a chop, and she asked him if he
would have a nice slice of pork for supper. He
was surprised at her urbanity, but accepted the
offer. Then she took the carving-knife, and
sneaked out to the shed where the deceased was
lying on a stretcher A portion of
the story is here left to the imagina-
tion. An hour later the young man re-
marked, as he finished up with a glass of
beer, that it “ was the tenderest bit of blanky
pork he ever tasted.” And next day a bewildered
jury brought in a verdict that the deceased had
evidently cut three slices off himself with a knife
and concealed them in some place unknown, and
that he had then committed suicide by shooting
himself with a gun. Also, the coroner and the
local chemist agreed that the case was most extra-
ordinary.

Messrs. W. O. YUILLE & CO.’S CERTIFICATE.
We hereby certify that we sold on account of Mr. John Orozier the St. Alban’s Estate to Mr.
W. R. Wilson on March 30, 1890, for the sum of cash. At this time the property was very
lightly stocked. We have examined Mr. Wilson’s books, and certify further that blood stock to the
value (actual cost) of £32,041 has since been added to those originally purchased. Eurther, that the
sales of St. Alban’s Yearlings conducted by us during the seasons 1890-1-2-3 and 1894 realised a

1. —THE ST. ALBAN’S ESTATE, con
sisting of 842 acres, situated 3£ miles east of
Geelong, Victoria, Australia. The property is
divided irito,3o paddocks, many with live hedge
fences. _ „
As a Breeding and Racing Establishment it is
questionable if any more complete place exists in
Europe: certainly it has no rival in Australia.
Originally selected and laid out by Mr. James
Wilson, one of the keenest judges in the colonies,
money has continued to bo freely expended on
improving and perfecting it by Mr. W. R.
Wilson, its present owner, so that it is now one
of the show places of Victoria.
The land is extremely fertile and sound, nearly
all the paddocks being laid down in English
grasses. The Barwon River (2 chains wide and
20ft. deep) intersects the property, thus ensuring
a never-failing supply of fresh water, alike for use
and irrigation. The system for the latter^is per-
fect, the drains being most extensive, and the
steam-pumping plant has a capacity of 120,000
gallons per hour. The rainfall is, however, so
good that it is rarely necessary to irrigate more
than two or three paddocks during a summer.
The plantations and hedges, of which several
miles exist, add to the usefulness and beauty of
the estate in a marked degree.
Shelter Sheds of large capacity are placed in
many of the paddocks, while the Yearling
Quarters consist of 40 loose boxes, cool and well
ventilated, built in quadrangular form, and of
ample size. Stone and brick tanks, windmills,
and feed-bins are placed in convenient places in
fie various paddocks, and the yards, &c., are
ample and perfectly arranged.
In the centre of the Estate the Training Track
and Course proper are laid out (14 miles and 1
mile 5 furlongs round respectively). Both may
well be compared to Flemiugton or Randwick,
the going being good in all weathers.
The Scraping Sheds are moat suitably placed,
and an Elevated Stand commands a most perfect
view of the tracks, river, and estate generally,
with the far-famed Barrabool Hills in the dis-
tance.
The Stallion Boxes are lofty and well-designed,
opening into a large covered-in exercise ground.
Feed rooms, men’s rooms, &c., are under the
i one roof. The Training Stables are perfect, and
contain 15 loose boxes, feed rooms, men’s rooms,
and every necessary adjunct to a first-class estab-
ment. Two cottages for stable boys are in close
proximity, and the out-buildings are extensive
and ample.
THE HOUSE —Brick and of modern design,
jonsists of about 30 rooms, and is a perfectly
appointed gentleman’s residence, beautifully situa-
ted, having a superb view of the You Yang
Ranges and Corio Bay, the latter only two miles
distant.
Sires.

Bards of the Backblocks.
WHEN “ BRUMMY” RINGS THE BELL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’m toiling for a living out-back in New South
Wales
(Good luck I’ve got the muscle that serves when
all else fails), , ■
And as the sun moves slowly west I oft wish for
the “ spell ”
And “smoke ” that follows tucker-time when
Brummy rings the bell.
When Brummy rings the bell, that crack’d old
bullock-bell,
Whose rattling, jangling, tin-pot tone the
“ hands ” all know so well,
Ah ! welcome is the sound to us, for everyone can
tell
It’s time for “scoff”—all hands knock off when
Brummy rings the bell.
We’re bathed in perspiration, the dust cakes
in our eyes,
While round our heads with ceaseless buzz are
circling swarms of flies ;
The air seems like a furnace-blast, it’s just as hot
as hell,
Till sundown ends the day at last, and Brummy
rings the bell.
When Brummy rings the bell, a crack’d old
bullock-bell,
Which every station cook has got, though why
I cannot tell,
We know another day’s “got in,” and hurry off
pell-mell,
With visions of a good square “scoff,” when
Brummy rings the bell.
I fancy when my cheque’s made up I’ll try my
luck round west,
Instead of down to Sydney town to “ blue it ” like
the rest —
And if success attend me I’ll return a howling
swell,
And give all hands a fair blow-out when
Brummy rings the bell.
When Brummy rings the bell, that crack’d old
bullock-bell,
Which every station-hut can boast, to tucker-time
foretell—
Well, if you visit Booberai and ask for me
they’ll tell
How “Ted, the Scoffer,” waltzes in when
Brummy rings the bell.
F.W.S.
AFTER THE SPREE.
A Ballad of Australia.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If you’ve got to travel gratis when you’ve had a
month in town,
Hark to the mutton-track callin’!
You’d best not tarry longer when you’ve changed
the last half-crown,
But hark to the mutton-track callin’ 1
So roll up your fly and your blanket,
Your quart-pot, tommyhawk, and tea;
Have another drink, and when you’ve drank
it,
Take your bloomin’ hat off to your spree !
If you want to hear the thunder of the stampers
where it’s rich,
Hark to the gold-fields callin’!
To sit between the shifts and hear the yarns the
diggers pitch,
Hark to the gold-fields callin’!
But if you make a note and never bank it,
You can reckon that it’s all u.p.
So have another drink, and when you’ve
drank it,
Take your bloomin’ hat off to your spree 1
Do you say that you are frightened of the dusty,
hot-wind trail?
Hark to the locusts callin’ !
That there’ll be a swaggy missin’ should the
water-bottle fail ?
Hark to the locusts callin’!
There’s many curse their luck that ought to
thank it,
And there’s good fish yet in the sea !
So have another drink, and when you’ve
drank it,
Take your bloomin’ hat off to your spree !
If you’ve got to travel gratis there’s some roads
that might be worse :
Hark to the blue-gums callin’!
The mutton-track’s the bullivard where you don’t
require a purse!
Hark to the blue-gums callin’!
So roll up your fly and your blanket,
Your quart-pot, tommyhawk, and tea;
Have another drink, and when you’ve drank it,
Take your bloomin’ hat off to your spree !
H. ST. B. MORE.

Mrs, M‘Cooley, of the Gluepot -
Swamp Receiving Office,
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Here, just you go and nail that hole up at once 1
Nice thing to have the post-office and not know
who posts the letters. Actually fixed up with
wax, too I I wonder who they think is going to
interfere with their letters ? It came from
Orowson’s, up the creek. I’ll bet, but it ain’t his
writing. He must write some nice letters—
actually told his children that they hadn’t got to
answer me if 1 asked them about the letters. No
use asking if you know the writing; you don’t
take no interest in nothing, you don’t.
“ It’s to old Chumlig, the storekeeper at Gallego.
Mrs. Ross told me, the last time she came for her
mail, that Crowson owed Chumlig a lot of money ;
hadn’t paid him all the time he was living at One
Gully. I wonder where he got the wax from ?
It’s reg’lar insulting to seal a letter like this when
it has only to go through my office and Gallego ;
looks as though he suspected a person."
“ Well, I declare, if Crowson didn’t get off all;
old Chumlig’s doings. Leaner, the other J.P.,
was against him at first; but old Chumlig kept on
questioning and bothering the witnesses till he got
them fair mixed up, and he seemed to sort of twist
little Leaner round. It was a regular shame.
Crowson orter got three years, at least. But I
suppose Chumlig knew he couldn’t get his money
if Crowson was in gaol. I always think that letter
had something to do with it. If it hadn’t been for
that wax—or even if you’d let the Post Office
alone, and minded your own business, I could have
got it all out of young Crowson when he posted
the letter.
“ A woman don’t get no chance with the likes
of you about. The only thing I’d be bothe ed
with the office for is to see that everything goes
on fair and square, and there you go and spoil my
best chance. Serve you right to get some of your
own sheep stolen.”
BAKBLOX.
Dear Bulletin, —Re Larry Petrie (an old
friend of mine) and the Australian Workers’
Union. “Edward Agamiau,” who writes you
from ’Frisco, is much astray. The Conferences of
the A.S.U. and G.L.U. delegates, held in Sydney
in ’92 (not ’93), at which Petrie was not present,
resolved to submit a scheme of amalgamation of
the above bodies to a plebiscite vote of their joint
membership—if carried, the name “Australian
Workers’ Union ” to be adopted by the amal-
gamated body. This vote was taken in ’92 and
defeated. In ’93 it was again put to members
and carried in N.S.W. and S.A. branches, but
defeated in Victoria. In ’94 the objecting
branches agreed to come into line, and the whole
organisation finally assumed the new name at the
Albury Convention of the present year. The
major portion of the scheme emanated from the
brain of “Billy Lane,” a Queensland delegate,
but, strange to say, the Northern province has
not yet fully thrown in its lot, although cordially
co-operating, with its Southern brethren. —Yours,
&c., Arthur Rae (President A. W.U.), Sydney,
Ist August, ’95. P.S.—The Queensland Shearers’
and Laborers’ Unions amalgamated in their own
province under the name of the “ Amalgamated ”
Workers’ Union over two years ago. As
paragraphs such as Agamiau’s help to “ make
history,” it is as well they make it correctly.
Hence this note. —A.R.
The Sick Cahriden
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Just shake my pillows up a bit, and take the
rocking-chair,
The cough’s not half so bad to-day, so I’m feeling
pretty fair;
Not as I used to feel, of course, in the days of old
lang syne,
When we didn’t “cab-it” home until the sun
began to shine.
What nights we had together, Bert! the hours
were ten till four
A.m,, deah boy, a.m., by Jove; and sometimes
rather more.
We burnt the candle then both ends, and never
snuff’t the wicks;
We started off with squashes straight, but soon
began to mix.
Open the window wide—that’s right—the room
gets rather warm.
Ah! Bert, if I were well again, back in my
summer form,
I’d play you billiards, fifty-up, and think it fun to
lose,
Or take a turn down Oollins-street in patent-
leather shoes,
I dressed myself last Sunday week, that’s bound
to make you smile,
The pants hung loose and—well, the coat was not
the latest style;
’Twas nine months since I’d had it home, the
collar seemed so strange.
Cut differently to yours. Heigho 1 how soon the
fashions change 1
What was I saying, though ? Ah, yet, the nights
we had, my word !
To be in bed at sunrise, Bert, is awfully
absurd ;
When the light comes stealing in my room, I’m
often wide awake,
And I sigh to be about again, for pretty Flossie’s
sake.
She misses me, I bet she does; you know what
women are;
She liked me best of all the boys who patronised
her bar;
Although we kept the house up late she didn’t
once complain,
Except that night when Phil got “tight” and
wouldn’t shout champagne.
Poor Floss ! I used to send her flowers, the
choicest things in bloom,
You never saw her wear ’em —true, she put them
in her room.
Syd. Saunders had a notion that she gave them
all away,
But I’m sure she always kept ’em, for she told me
so one day.
She didn’t mind my teasing her, I never made
her mad—
What hair! my word! what splendid teeth, and
what a bust she had !
She would have let me kiss her once, I think —in
fact, no doubt —
If she hadn’t been so frightened that the “boss”
might be about.
We sowed some wild oats, rather —yes, by Jove 1
we sowed a crop,
Do you reoolleot those darlings at the tea-and-
coffee shop ?
Nice girls; the little fair one, not the youngest
(she was dark),
Pinched my arm last time I saw her, on the
vacoination-mark.
Have a cigarette, old fellow, in that box you’ll
find a few—
And tell me, how’s the chorus ? Have you spotted
something new,
Or is your heart still constant to the one you
mashed that night
From the stage-box ? You remember, she was
dressed in blue and white.
You love her still —you terror ? and she’s smiling
just the same ;
You ought to try to meet her, Bert, and find out
what’s her name.
My mash, you know, was Maud de Yaux, she
mostly played the page.
It’s hard to have to die before I’ve seen her off the
stage.
But I mustn’t make you gloomy, with this talk
about the past—
They were awf’ly jolly times, and I was awf’ly
jolly fast.
I must contemplate the present, here we are in
“ budding spring,”
And I don’t think my pyjamas are at all the
proper thing.
They are beastly winter patterns, and the buttons,
too, are brutes;
As a favor, deah old fellow, choose me half-a-
dozen suits ;
Let the stripes be bright and lively, but not, of
course, too wide—
And I think I’d like the jackets with a pocket
either side.
They’ll last me out : yes, Bertie, they’ll last me
out, I know ;
What’s the odds ! I’m only going where all other
johnnies go.
I’ve been reading Gordon’s poems—wish the book
was better bound—
And they’ve set me almost longing to be under-
neath the ground.
I took my whack of pleasure, and I sometimes
felt a pain :
Perhaps I’d knock off smoking if I had to live
again,
But I’ve no regrets to speak of ; there’s a heavy
tailor’s bill,
And that my aunt will settle when I’m lying cold
and still.
Suppose you must be toddling, if you promised
Kate you’d call —
A doosid fetching filly, though her eyes are rather
small;
I’d like to stroll down with you for an hour, one
afternoon :
I would lean across the counter, and, by Jingo !
how I’d spoon.
Good-bye, you mustn’t mind my tears —good-bye,
so glad you came;
Remember me to all the girls I used to know by
name,
And raise your hat to Flossie, whom I nevermore
may see —
Yes, raise your hat to pretty Floss, and kiss your
hand— -for me.
Melb.
EDMUND FISHER.
Plucky .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
After all, there are few fatal accident?
among whalers. The calling being lull of danger,
every precaution Is taken, and nothing loft to
chance. Still, there are many “ close shaves.”
« • •
There was little wind. The whaling
barque Derwent, of Hobart, rolled languidly as
she lazily cruised under easy sail. Presently a
cry came from the look-out man aloft—“ Whales
right ahead 1 ” delivered as only whalers can deliver
it. There was a rush for the boats, each man knowing
his place and what was expected of him. Down
dropped the boats and hurriedly they pushed clear
of the ship. “Bend your backs, but break no
oars,” is the “ spouter’s ” creed, and strong arms
made the frail craft fairly fly from the strokes of
the long, light blades. Five men in each boat
pulling their hardest —racing for their prey. The
mate in charge of the leading boat chose his fish ;
the other boats singled theirs.
The mate’s quarry “sounded.” The experi-
enced helmsman calculated on the spot where the
fish would rise again. A short interval, and the dark
body re-appears on the surface, making a swirl of
the waters as it emerges from the depths. Away
goes the boat till within “range.” The bow-man
relinquishes his oar and stands ready, weapon in
hand. “Give it to him!” —the iron hurtles
through the air and fastens. “ Stern all I” The
rowers back water. The line sings as the whale
starts forward. The logger-head smokes as the
boats tears through the water, level with the gun-
wales yet none coming in, so furious is the pace.
Oh, the excitement of it!
• * •
It happened in an instant—no one could
say precisely how. The mate had become en-
tangled in the line, was plucked out of the boat
ere he could utter a cry, and was following in the
wake of the mad monster ahead while the tem-
porarily-dazed crew looked on in blank dismay.
• • •
But they were not all paralysed. The
quick, sinewy darkey, active in the water as a fish
itself, hesitated not a moment. Sheath-knife in
mouth, he clutched the line, and, rapidly hauling
himself along it, hand over hand, disappeared
from view. How time seemed to hang ! though
the whole business could have been summed up in
seconds.
Watching and waiting anxiously, hopelessly,
whilst the motor still dragged them along, those
in the boat felt the line suddenly slacken—then
hang loosely over the bows. Their velocity was
checked, though the boat still ran on with the im-
petus gained.
A second or two later two figures floated on the
surface, one supported by the other. They were
soon picked up—the mate senseless, his dark
deliverer well-nigh exhausted —and no time was
lost in getting back to the ship.
* » •
The negro had grasped the situation at a
glance. The mate was foul of the line, which had
a complete turn round his leg; he could never
have cleared himself. He owed his life to the
black man’s ready steel.
Tas.

[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Princess glanced at me shyly, with
velvety black eyes.
“ I cannot stay, I must go back now,” she said
in broken English and soft, liquid accents.
Then she looked down modestly.
The adjective would seem inappropriate to
people who associate modesty less with glance
than with garment. Of the latter the Princess
had as little as can be discreetly mentioned,
almost as little as Mother Eve before the Fall.
At first sight she shocked my civilised suscepti-
bilities with her beautiful brown bust, bare to the
sun, and the well-defined line of hip under the
thin, tight covering.
1 naturally agreed with the missionaries and
other devout and delicate-minded people that she
was a bold, bad miss who had to be taught
modesty.
Then I fell to considering the beautiful dark
face above the bust, and a new reading dawned
upon me.
1 remembered that Eve, in her days of inno-
cence, “was not ashamed.”
# » *
The Princess was not ashamed.
She moved like an animated bronze statue. The
curve of her hips undulated gracefully as she
swung over the ground with firm, untrammelled
ease. The poise of her head was artistic as she
raised the bundle of washing she did not disdain
to carry. But of none of these was she conscious.
If she moved with an air of statuesque pride it
was from the innate consciousness of her dignity
as a Princess, a maid in whose veins flowed pure,
untainted blood, a Taupo maid to boot, the set-
apart virgin of the village, with whom none dared
to meddle, and whom it behoved to walk about
guarded and attended.
# # *
But she was dimly conscious that my ways
were not as her ways, and with puzzled courtesy
she tried to consider my feelings. When I drew
near she donned a loose, decollete bodice, or a
dark scarf wound round her neck and falling over
her bosom. They belonged to her full dress ward-
robe, and she was quite willing to wear them as a
concession to my civilised weakness.
I felt the courtesy and the concession. I also
felt humiliated thereby. The humiliation was
good for my soul.
I had been preparing a monograph for the Vir-
tuous Literature Society on “ The Influence of
Civilisation in the Development of Modesty and
Decency,” and I had come to reside among non-
virtuous savages for purely literary ends.
I thought I would allow that monograph to
stand over for the present. It struck me that I
had fallen into linguistic error, and confounded
Drapery with Decency.
# # #
My Princess has other ideas.
To her drapery is a question of dress in the
sense of adornment; but decency has no part
therein.
It is also a question of class, not sex, distinction.
It is finally a question of satisfying innate
artistic instincts, unspoilt as yet by thirst for ad-
miration. .
In short, my Princess has her own ethics of
dress, but they are not to be found in the fashion-
books.
When she winds a fresh lava lava round her
beautiful limbs, she chooses it of a bright tartan
pattern to contrast with her brown skin, or a rich
blue to blend therewith; but so also do her father
and brother. When she passes a fragrant white
frangipanni, a scarlet hibiscus, or a yellow fau
blossom, she gathers and places it in the fluffy
black locks which she has dexterously pinned up,
prompted by the same love of coloring that brings
her brother leaping from the bush with a necklace
of scarlet berries and a wreath or kirtle of green
leaves.
But she no more looks in the mirror than
yonder orange-red, black-winged bird, twittering
in the breadfruit-tree, looks in the stream. The
only mirrors she recks of are certain minute ones
which will be piled high on her enormous wig
when she is suitably attired to dance at the com-
ing Siva.
For she is the Village Virgin, queen of festivi-
ties by right of birth, beauty and virtue, and this
it is which makes her at once so pure and so proud
in her savage innocence.
* * *
In that other world, the world of civilisa-
tion from which I come, we do not as a general
thing credit the belle of the season with a retir-
ing disposition.
Neither do we necessarily associate high kicking
and serpentine dancing with modesty and deport-
ment.
My princess is high-kicker, serpentine-dancer,
and society belle, in the native acceptation of the
terms. But her ideas strike me as terribly incon-
sistent with her position.
For instance, when in full-dress she covers up
more of her person than when in ordinary plain—
particularly plain—costume.
Also, when at home among her own people, she
walks about in serene unconsciousness of her Eve-
like appearance; but if she sees it attracting the
roving eye of a cranky foreigner, like myself, she
seeks a slight covering which shall conceal her
outlines.
The society belle of my acquaintance proceeds
in an opposite direction; the more strangers she
expects to meet the more decollete she is. As for
the high-kicker ....
I privately fancy the Princess would take ex-
ception to the general appearance of the high-
kicker.
* * *
For, as I remarked before, she has her
own dress ethics, dimly perceived by me, but not
analysed.
In proof thereof, she eyes with stern disfavor
my print of Leighton’s “ Psyche Unveiled,”
which hangs on the wall. The young lady is a
great deal too discreet to give it any further
notice than that of a startled glance; but her
attendant duenna was more explicit, and enquired,
with an expression of disgust curling her thick
upper-lip, what had happened to Psyche’s lava-
lava.
Now Duenna was, “inher figure,” a very passee
objectionable figure. But she also had her ethics
as well as her dingy-white lava-lava. I must dis-
tinctly mention that Psyche’s sex has “ nothing to
do with the case.” Cupid, without the inevitable
lava-lava or a leaf kilt, would be just as
scandalous. The question of Decency versus
Drapery becomes daily more complicated.
* * *
On one occasion the Princess had a tiff
with the (Protestant) missionaries. She looks too
placid to have a tiff with anyone, but this was a
serious occasion. It was a question of dress
ethics.
The Princess wanted to go to the mission
school; the missionaries were nothing loth ; but
the matter of attire had to be primarily settled,
and, in matters of attire, Protestant missionaries
are stiff.
They are indifferent as to color, but rigid as to
covering. Their pupils must all be dressed in a
loose gown which they originally introduced, but
which, by some strange mistake, is habitually
styled “ native dress.” The term is preposterous.
N o native would ever have invented anything so
unbecoming to a fully-developed figure ; out of 10
native women who wear it eight look clumsy and
slipshod.
But it suited alike the properties of Christianity
and civilisation— i.e., it hid the human form and
suggested that there was something to conceal.
• * #
The Princess’s artistic instinct and her
pride of place alike rebelled.
Her soul revolted at the shapeless gown, but it
revolted still more at a civilised uniform which
would bring her to the level of the common herd.
She was quite willing to appear always in her
short, loose, sleeveless decollete blouse, or in a
long neck scarf. But to cover herself up to the
throat and down to the wrist and hide her lava
lava/ she, the beautiful Taupo, the Village Virgin,
just as if she had some blemish to conceal —the
notion was preposterous, not to say shocking.
The missionaries were inflexible. The Princess
was likewise inflexible. So she went to the nuns,
and the good sisters did not clothe hex-, but took
her in.
» * »
The nuns, who are the most covered-up
ladies of my acquaintance, seem less prejudiced
on this point than their sister missionaries of the
rival faith. If you except the hands, no artist
can make a possible guess at a nun’s figure, or
even assert with positive certainty that she has a
figure at all. Yet in this part of the world the
sisterhood accept with philosophy native views on
the subject of dress. Possibly their own super-
fluity of raiment in a tropical clime makes them
sympathise with the desire for mother Eve’s free-
dom. Anyhow, though they drew the line at
appearances “in her figure,” they allowed the
Princess to drape that figure as slightly as she
pleased.
Still more remarkable, they seem not to have
given her any civilised reason for draping the
figure. Consequently, she left the convent with
her curious code of ethics unaltered. lam not at
all obliged to the .nuns, for they might have saved
me a great deal of unsatisfactory cogitation,
# # *
Some day my Princess will resign her
position as Virgin of the Village to become the
wife of a great chief.
She will be draped in stiff rich mats so heavily
that she can scarcely walk—not for decency,
but for display ; the clan on each side will
come laden with presents for general distribution;
and feasting, and speech-making, and gift-giving
will run riot.
Then her days of dancing and daintiness and
picturesque attire will be over. She will grow fat
and lazy and degenerate into one of the sloppy,
jolly native women who slouch about the place.
Her artistic perception will dwindle, she will take
to the loose wrapper and to a larrikiness hat im-
ported by the island trader. But her moral tone
will not be thereby altered; she will still, on
washing-day, or at tub-hour, cast aside the gown,
appear in the lava-lava, and be “ not ashamed.”
Or she will perchance marry a white man who
will instil some white notions into her dark head.
She will wear her loose gown with a conscious air,
add stockings and French shoes to her wardrobe,
acquire a notion of the improprieties and the art
of blushing—though I believe the latter is out of
date.
• * •
I had serious thoughts of being that white
man. I had serious thoughts of falling in love
with the Princess. She is quite charming enough.
And then there would be all the romance of
rescuing her from her present semi-barbarous con-
dition and surrounding her with the refinements
of enlightened conventionalism. It was a very
pretty frame for a romance, but one thing dis-
turbed me. I could not sketch in my Princess
satisfactorily as central figure. It was out of the
question to introduce her in her lava-lava , and I
fear her loose bodice or neck-scarf would not find
favor even on the most full-dress night of the
“ circle. ’’ There would be nothing for it but civi-
lised attire, and my heart quailed at the visions
this evoked. I remembered divers fat Jewesses
in gala array who were my pet aversion. Could
I ensure my Princess against proving a
second edition of these 1 And could any
romance hold out against the appearance
of a dark, much spread-out young person, in
the silk and furbelows and buckramed waist of
fashion ?
True, my Princess would learn the art of Drap-
ing—the ethics of Decency. I paused and be-
thought me again of a full-dress night in the circle
and “ living models ” on the stage.
With the art of Draping my Princess might
also acquire the arts of Immodesty. She might
perchance learn to be ashamed.
* * #
I refrained from falling in love.
« • •
The Virtuous Literature Society is still
waiting for that monograph.
Apia, Samoa.

Chips from a chapter of the latest Argus
serial, “Guilty Gold ” :
By HEADON HILL.
Author of “ Clues from a Detective’s Camera,”
“ Cabinet Secrets,” “ The Mystery of Bennett’s
Cross,” “ The Rajah’s Second Wife,” &c.
“ Curse him for the greediest scoundrel in the city,”
hissed Vardon. “What does he want?”
“ S’elp me, but I faked ’em expreshly for the Golden
Kloof,” bleated the cringing Hebrew. “ You’re too good
a cushtomer to play hanky with, Mister Vardon.”
“Yes, and I know a little too much about you, Levi,
don’t I, to admit of tricks,” said Vardon, motioning him
to the door.
The whole horrid truth was laid bare to him in one
sharp, heart-piercing slab.
“ Liar and thief !” exclaimed Guy, exasperated by the
other’s sneering coolness. “ You know well enough what
I mean. Answer me the question, aye or nay, before I
move from this spot.”
No wonder the Argus does not advocate Pro-
tection against the imported penny-dreadfuls !

HAVING struck the first blow at the IDOL OP HIGH SELLING, he is determined to continue
his crusade of destruction against this fetish. P. R. LARKIN is satisfied with small profits on
the genuine article. His system of buying in the best HOME MARKETS, and IMPORTING,
affords him a margin of profit which, in other cases, goes to support the middleman—an item of con-
siderable magnitude, which, having slipped thro’ the fingers of the retailer, must inevitably come from
the consumer’s pocket. This is not P. R. LARKIN’S mode of doing business. He is a DIRECT
IMPORTER, and therein lies part of the secret of his unapproachably low prices.
